    Module 3

    Linear Regression: The Reusable Primitive

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain what a model is by writing the linear hypothesis \( \hat{y} = wx + b \) and saying out loud what every symbol means.
    • Measure how wrong a model is using the mean squared error (MSE) cost, and compute it by hand on a tiny dataset.
    • Extend from one feature to many features and write the prediction in clean matrix form.
    • Implement linear regression from scratch in NumPy, including the closed-form normal equation, and verify your numbers.
    • Fit the same model in scikit-learn with three lines and read off coef_ and intercept_.
    • Spot the classic traps: confusing correlation with causation, forgetting to scale, and extrapolating past your data.

    Why should I care?

    Imagine you manage a small coffee cart. You've noticed that on warmer days you sell more iced drinks. You have a notebook with twelve days of data: the temperature each morning and the number of iced drinks you sold. Tomorrow the forecast says 28°C. How many cups of ice and how much milk should you prep? Eyeballing the notebook works badly. Linear regression turns that notebook into a single rule — "expect about 4 extra drinks for every degree warmer" — that gives you a concrete number for any temperature. The very same machinery predicts house prices, estimates how a drug dose affects blood pressure, and forms the literal last layer of many neural networks you'll build in Module 10. Learn this one idea well and you'll reuse it for the rest of the course — that's why we call it the reusable primitive.

    Intuition first

    A model is just a rule that turns inputs into a prediction. The input is called a feature (here, the morning temperature). The thing we want to predict is the target or label (here, drinks sold). Linear regression makes the simplest possible assumption: as the feature goes up, the target goes up (or down) in a straight, steady way. No bends, no surprises — just a straight line drawn through your scattered data points.

    Picture your twelve days as dots on a graph: temperature along the bottom, drinks sold up the side. Warmer days are dots toward the right and higher up. Linear regression asks one question: of all the straight lines I could draw through this cloud of dots, which one passes closest to all of them at once? That best line is your model. To predict tomorrow's sales, you find 28 on the bottom axis, go straight up to the line, and read off the height.

    Analogy — the tightrope through a crowd. Think of stretching a single straight rope across a room full of floating balloons, where each balloon is a data point. You can slide the rope up and down (that's the intercept) and tilt it (that's the slope). Your goal is to position the rope so the total stretch of all the little rubber bands connecting each balloon to the rope is as small as possible. Some balloons end up above the rope, some below — that's fine. You only care that the rope sits snugly in the middle of the whole crowd. Linear regression is the math that finds the exact best rope.

    The line has two knobs you can turn. The slope says how steep the line is: "how many more drinks per extra degree." The intercept says how high the line sits when the feature is zero: "drinks sold at 0°C." Turn those two knobs until the line hugs the data, and you're done.

    Green dots are data; the purple line is the fitted model. The short dashed segments are residuals — the vertical gaps the model is trying to make small.

    Making it precise

    Now we name things with symbols. Each symbol is just a number with a job; define it once and it stays the same throughout.

    For a single feature, the model — also called the hypothesis — is:

    \[ \hat{y} = w\,x + b \]

    where:

    • \( x \) is the feature — one input number for one example (e.g. a temperature like 28).
    • \( \hat{y} \) (read "y-hat") is the model's prediction — the number it guesses for that input. The little hat means "estimated," to distinguish it from the true value \( y \).
    • \( w \) is the weight (also called the slope) — a number we adjust that controls how strongly the feature pushes the prediction up or down.
    • \( b \) is the bias (also called the intercept) — a number we adjust that shifts the whole line up or down; it is the prediction when \( x = 0 \).

    That equation is the whole model. "Learning" means choosing good values for \( w \) and \( b \). To choose them we need a way to score how bad a given pair \( (w, b) \) is. That score is the cost function (also called the loss).

    The most common cost for regression is the mean squared error. The idea in words: for each example, take the gap between the prediction and the truth, square it (so over- and under-shooting both count as positive, and big mistakes count extra), then average over all examples. In symbols:

    \[ J(w, b) = \frac{1}{m} \sum_{i=1}^{m} \left( \hat{y}^{(i)} - y^{(i)} \right)^2 \]

    where:

    • \( m \) is the number of examples (rows in your dataset).
    • The superscript \( (i) \) means "the \( i \)-th example," so \( x^{(i)} \) is the feature of example \( i \) and \( y^{(i)} \) is its true target. It is an index, not a power.
    • \( \hat{y}^{(i)} = w\,x^{(i)} + b \) is the prediction for that example.
    • \( \hat{y}^{(i)} - y^{(i)} \) is the residual (the dashed gap in the figure): how far off we were on example \( i \).
    • \( J(w,b) \) is the cost — one single number measuring total wrongness for the chosen \( w, b \). Lower is better; the best line is the one that makes \( J \) smallest.

    You may see \( \tfrac{1}{2m} \) instead of \( \tfrac{1}{m} \) elsewhere; the extra \( \tfrac12 \) just makes a derivative cleaner in Module 4 and doesn't change which \( (w,b) \) is best. We'll use \( \tfrac1m \) here.

    Play with it: drag the slope and intercept knobs to draw your own line through the dots. Watch the red residuals (and their squared-error squares) grow and shrink, and the MSE update live. Then press Best fit to see the line snap to the least-squares optimum and the MSE bottom out.

    From one feature to many

    Real problems have several features (temperature, day-of-week, whether it rained). Give each feature its own weight and add them up:

    \[ \hat{y} = w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + \dots + w_n x_n + b \]

    where \( n \) is the number of features and \( w_j \) is the weight for feature \( j \). Writing this with a sum for every example gets noisy fast, so we package the numbers into vectors and matrices — this is vectorization, and it makes code both shorter and dramatically faster.

    Stack one example's features into a row vector and all weights into a column vector. A neat trick lets us absorb the bias \( b \) into the weights: add a constant feature equal to \( 1 \) to every example, and give it weight \( b \). Then for the whole dataset:

    \[ \hat{\mathbf{y}} = \mathbf{X}\,\mathbf{w} \]

    where \( \mathbf{X} \) is the design matrix of shape \( m \times (n+1) \) — one row per example, one column per feature plus a leading column of ones; \( \mathbf{w} \) is the weight vector of length \( n+1 \) (its first entry is the bias); and \( \hat{\mathbf{y}} \) is the length-\( m \) vector of all predictions at once. One matrix multiply replaces every loop. (If matrices feel shaky, revisit Module 1 — Just-Enough Math Intuition.)

    The normal equation (closed form)

    For linear regression with MSE there is a remarkable shortcut: the exact best \( \mathbf{w} \) can be computed directly with one formula, no iteration needed. It is called the normal equation:

    \[ \mathbf{w} = \left( \mathbf{X}^{\top} \mathbf{X} \right)^{-1} \mathbf{X}^{\top} \mathbf{y} \]

    where \( \mathbf{X}^{\top} \) is \( \mathbf{X} \) transposed (rows and columns swapped) and the \( {}^{-1} \) means matrix inverse. You don't need to derive this now — just know it gives the same answer that minimizing \( J \) would, in one step. It's perfect for small problems. For huge datasets the inverse gets expensive, which is exactly why Module 4 — Gradient Descent introduces an iterative alternative that scales.

    Worked example — by hand

    Three days of data, one feature. Let \( x \) be a (made-up, tiny) temperature index and \( y \) the drinks sold:

    \( (x,y) = (1, 2),\ (2, 2.5),\ (3, 3.5) \), so \( m = 3 \).

    Suppose we guess the line \( w = 1 \), \( b = 1 \), giving the model \( \hat{y} = 1\cdot x + 1 \). Let's score this guess.

    Step 1 — predictions \( \hat{y} = x + 1 \):

    • \( x=1:\ \hat{y} = 1 + 1 = 2 \)
    • \( x=2:\ \hat{y} = 2 + 1 = 3 \)
    • \( x=3:\ \hat{y} = 3 + 1 = 4 \)

    Step 2 — residuals \( \hat{y} - y \):

    • \( 2 - 2 = 0 \)
    • \( 3 - 2.5 = 0.5 \)
    • \( 4 - 3.5 = 0.5 \)

    Step 3 — squared residuals:

    • \( 0^2 = 0 \)
    • \( 0.5^2 = 0.25 \)
    • \( 0.5^2 = 0.25 \)

    Step 4 — average them (the MSE):

    \[ J = \frac{0 + 0.25 + 0.25}{3} = \frac{0.5}{3} \approx 0.1667 \]

    So our guessed line scores \( J \approx 0.167 \). Is that good? We have nothing to compare it to yet — so let's find the best possible line by hand using the normal equation on this tiny set.

    Step 5 — set up the design matrix (add a column of ones for the bias):

    \[ \mathbf{X} = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 1 & 2 \\ 1 & 3 \end{bmatrix}, \quad \mathbf{y} = \begin{bmatrix} 2 \\ 2.5 \\ 3.5 \end{bmatrix} \]

    Here the first column is the ones (its weight will be \( b \)) and the second column is \( x \) (its weight will be \( w \)).

    Step 6 — compute \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X} \): this 2×2 result counts and sums things.

    \[ \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X} = \begin{bmatrix} m & \sum x \\ \sum x & \sum x^2 \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 3 & 6 \\ 6 & 14 \end{bmatrix} \]

    because \( \sum x = 1+2+3 = 6 \) and \( \sum x^2 = 1+4+9 = 14 \).

    Step 7 — compute \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{y} \):

    \[ \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{y} = \begin{bmatrix} \sum y \\ \sum x y \end{bmatrix} = \begin{bmatrix} 8 \\ 17.5 \end{bmatrix} \]

    because \( \sum y = 2 + 2.5 + 3.5 = 8 \) and \( \sum xy = (1)(2)+(2)(2.5)+(3)(3.5) = 2 + 5 + 10.5 = 17.5 \).

    Step 8 — invert the 2×2 matrix. For \( \begin{bmatrix} a & b \\ c & d \end{bmatrix} \) the inverse is \( \frac{1}{ad-bc}\begin{bmatrix} d & -b \\ -c & a \end{bmatrix} \). Here \( ad - bc = (3)(14) - (6)(6) = 42 - 36 = 6 \).

    \[ (\mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X})^{-1} = \frac{1}{6}\begin{bmatrix} 14 & -6 \\ -6 & 3 \end{bmatrix} \]

    Step 9 — multiply to get the weights \( \mathbf{w} = (\mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X})^{-1}\,\mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{y} \):

    \[ \mathbf{w} = \frac{1}{6}\begin{bmatrix} 14 & -6 \\ -6 & 3 \end{bmatrix}\begin{bmatrix} 8 \\ 17.5 \end{bmatrix} = \frac{1}{6}\begin{bmatrix} 14(8) - 6(17.5) \\ -6(8) + 3(17.5) \end{bmatrix} = \frac{1}{6}\begin{bmatrix} 112 - 105 \\ -48 + 52.5 \end{bmatrix} = \frac{1}{6}\begin{bmatrix} 7 \\ 4.5 \end{bmatrix} \] \[ \mathbf{w} = \begin{bmatrix} 1.1667 \\ 0.75 \end{bmatrix} \]

    So the best line is \( b \approx 1.1667 \) (intercept) and \( w = 0.75 \) (slope): \( \hat{y} = 0.75x + 1.1667 \).

    Step 10 — sanity check its cost. Predictions: \( x=1 \to 1.9167 \), \( x=2 \to 2.6667 \), \( x=3 \to 3.4167 \). Residuals: \( -0.0833,\ 0.1667,\ -0.0833 \). Squared: \( 0.00694,\ 0.02778,\ 0.00694 \). MSE \( = (0.00694+0.02778+0.00694)/3 \approx 0.01389 \). That's far below our earlier guess of \( 0.167 \) — the best line really is much better, exactly as it should be.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's reproduce the worked example in NumPy: first score the guessed line, then solve the normal equation. The numbers should match what we computed by hand.

    import numpy as np

# Tiny dataset from the worked example
x = np.array([1.0, 2.0, 3.0])
y = np.array([2.0, 2.5, 3.5])
m = len(x)

# --- Score a guessed line w=1, b=1 ---
w_guess, b_guess = 1.0, 1.0
y_hat = w_guess * x + b_guess          # predictions: [2, 3, 4]
residuals = y_hat - y                   # [0, 0.5, 0.5]
mse_guess = np.mean(residuals ** 2)     # average of squared residuals
print("Guessed predictions:", y_hat)
print("Guessed MSE:", round(mse_guess, 4))

# --- Solve for the BEST line with the normal equation ---
# Build the design matrix X: a column of ones (for b) and the x column
X = np.column_stack([np.ones(m), x])    # shape (3, 2)
# w_best = (X^T X)^(-1) X^T y
w_best = np.linalg.inv(X.T @ X) @ X.T @ y
b_opt, w_opt = w_best[0], w_best[1]      # first entry is bias, second is slope
print("Best intercept b:", round(b_opt, 4))
print("Best slope w:", round(w_opt, 4))

# Cost of the best line
y_hat_best = X @ w_best
mse_best = np.mean((y_hat_best - y) ** 2)
print("Best MSE:", round(mse_best, 5))

    Running this prints Guessed MSE: 0.1667, then Best intercept b: 1.1667, Best slope w: 0.75, and Best MSE: 0.01389 — exactly our hand calculations. Note X.T @ X uses the @ operator for matrix multiplication and X.T for the transpose. In real code prefer np.linalg.solve(X.T @ X, X.T @ y) over an explicit inverse — it's more accurate and faster — but the inverse makes the formula obvious here.

    In code: the library way

    scikit-learn wraps all of that behind a tidy fit/predict interface. You hand it your features and targets; it finds the best \( w \) and \( b \) for you. Notice the shape: scikit-learn expects features as a 2D array (rows = examples, columns = features), so we reshape our single feature into a column.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import LinearRegression

x = np.array([1.0, 2.0, 3.0]).reshape(-1, 1)   # shape (3, 1): one column = one feature
y = np.array([2.0, 2.5, 3.5])

model = LinearRegression()      # by default it learns an intercept (bias) too
model.fit(x, y)                 # this is where the learning happens

print("slope  (coef_):", model.coef_)        # the learned weight(s)
print("intercept (b):", model.intercept_)    # the learned bias

# Predict for new inputs, e.g. x = 4 and x = 5
new_x = np.array([[4.0], [5.0]])
print("predictions:", model.predict(new_x))

# A built-in goodness-of-fit score (R^2): 1.0 is perfect
print("R^2 on training data:", round(model.score(x, y), 4))

    This prints slope (coef_): [0.75] and intercept (b): 1.1666... — the same answer the normal equation gave, because under the hood scikit-learn solves essentially the same least-squares problem. The predictions for \( x=4,5 \) are about \( 4.17 \) and \( 4.92 \). The R^2 score (about \( 0.96 \)) reports the fraction of the target's variation the line explains; you'll meet it properly in Module 6 — Model Evaluation. Three lines — LinearRegression(), fit, predict — and you have a working model, because you now understand exactly what those lines are doing.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Explain the mean squared error to me with a completely different analogy than the tightrope one, and tell me why we square the residuals instead of taking absolute values."
    • "Generate 5 practice problems where I'm given a small dataset and a guessed \( w \) and \( b \), and I compute the MSE by hand. Then check my answers step by step."
    • "Run the from-scratch NumPy code in this module, then change the dataset to (1,3),(2,5),(3,7),(4,9) and show me what the slope and intercept become — and explain why they came out that way."
    • "Pair with me to implement linear regression from scratch using the normal equation on the California Housing dataset from scikit-learn, then compare my result to LinearRegression and review my code for bugs."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "The slope proves the feature causes the target." No — linear regression measures association, not causation. Ice cream sales and drowning deaths both rise in summer; a regression linking them is real but neither causes the other (hot weather drives both). A model can fit beautifully and still encode a confounder, not a cause.
    • Forgetting that residuals are squared, not absolute. Squaring means a single big miss hurts much more than several small ones. That makes linear regression sensitive to outliers; one wild data point can yank the whole line toward it.
    • Ignoring units and scale with multiple features. A weight of \( 0.001 \) on "house area in square metres" and \( 5 \) on "number of bedrooms" does not mean bedrooms matter more — the features live on different scales. To compare importances or to help iterative methods, standardize features first (Module 7 — Feature Engineering). The normal equation doesn't need scaling, but gradient descent in Module 4 strongly does.
    • Extrapolating far outside the data. A line fit on temperatures 10–30°C may predict negative drink sales at −40°C. The straight-line assumption is only trustworthy in the range you actually observed; predictions far outside it are guesses, not science.
    • Expecting a straight line to fit a curve. If the true relationship bends (sales plateau on very hot days), a single straight line will systematically miss. The cure isn't abandoning linear models — it's adding curved features like \( x^2 \), which keeps the model linear in its weights (you'll see this in Module 7).
    • Reading \( x^{(i)} \) as "x to the power i." The parenthesized superscript is an index naming the example, not an exponent. \( x^{(2)} \) is the feature of the second example; \( x^2 \) is x squared.

    Exercises

    1. Using the model \( \hat{y} = 2x + 1 \), compute the predictions for \( x = 0, 3, 10 \) and state the slope and intercept in plain words.
      Show solution

      Predictions: \( \hat{y}(0) = 2(0)+1 = 1 \); \( \hat{y}(3) = 2(3)+1 = 7 \); \( \hat{y}(10) = 2(10)+1 = 21 \). The slope is 2, meaning the prediction rises by 2 for every increase of 1 in \( x \). The intercept is 1, meaning the prediction is 1 when \( x = 0 \).

    2. For the dataset \( (1,1),(2,3),(3,4) \) and the guessed line \( w=1, b=0 \), compute each residual, each squared residual, and the MSE.
      Show solution

      Predictions \( \hat{y}=x \): at \( x=1,2,3 \) they are \( 1, 2, 3 \). Residuals \( \hat{y}-y \): \( 1-1=0 \), \( 2-3=-1 \), \( 3-4=-1 \). Squared: \( 0, 1, 1 \). MSE \( = (0+1+1)/3 = 2/3 \approx 0.667 \).

    3. You fit a model to predict monthly electricity bill from home size and get \( \hat{y} = 0.8\,x_1 + 25\,x_2 + 40 \), where \( x_1 \) is floor area in square metres and \( x_2 \) is the number of air conditioners. Interpret each weight and the intercept in everyday language.
      Show solution

      The weight \( 0.8 \) on \( x_1 \): each extra square metre of floor area adds about $0.80 to the predicted monthly bill, holding the number of AC units fixed. The weight \( 25 \) on \( x_2 \): each additional air conditioner adds about $25, holding area fixed. The intercept \( 40 \): a (hypothetical) home with zero area and zero AC units would be predicted $40 — often not physically meaningful, just the line's baseline. "Holding the other fixed" matters: in multiple regression each weight describes its feature's effect with the others held constant.

    4. Set up (you may use NumPy) the normal equation for \( (0,1),(1,3),(2,5) \) and find \( w \) and \( b \). Then verify by predicting at the three training points.
      Show solution

      Design matrix \( \mathbf{X}=\begin{bmatrix}1&0\\1&1\\1&2\end{bmatrix} \), \( \mathbf{y}=\begin{bmatrix}1\\3\\5\end{bmatrix} \). Then \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{X}=\begin{bmatrix}3&3\\3&5\end{bmatrix} \), \( \mathbf{X}^{\top}\mathbf{y}=\begin{bmatrix}9\\13\end{bmatrix} \). Determinant \( =3(5)-3(3)=6 \), inverse \( =\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}5&-3\\-3&3\end{bmatrix} \). Weights \( =\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}5(9)-3(13)\\-3(9)+3(13)\end{bmatrix}=\tfrac16\begin{bmatrix}6\\12\end{bmatrix}=\begin{bmatrix}1\\2\end{bmatrix} \). So \( b=1, w=2 \), i.e. \( \hat{y}=2x+1 \). Check: \( x=0\to1 \), \( x=1\to3 \), \( x=2\to5 \) — exact fit (MSE \( =0 \)), as expected since the points are perfectly collinear.

    5. A friend fits a regression of city temperature (feature) on ice-cream sales (target) and another of ice-cream sales (feature) on temperature (target). They're surprised the two slopes aren't reciprocals of each other and conclude one model is "wrong." What would you tell them?
      Show solution

      Neither is wrong; they answer different questions and minimize different residuals. Regressing \( y \) on \( x \) minimizes vertical gaps in \( y \); regressing \( x \) on \( y \) minimizes horizontal gaps in \( x \). Only with a perfect (\( R^2=1 \)) fit do the slopes become exact reciprocals; with any scatter they differ. The deeper lesson echoes a pitfall above: linear regression is directional and descriptive, not a statement about causation or a symmetric physical law. Choose the direction that matches what you actually want to predict.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Write \( \hat{y}=wx+b \) and explain, without notes, what \( w \), \( b \), \( x \), and \( \hat{y} \) each mean.
    • Compute the MSE of a given line on a 3- or 4-point dataset by hand and get the right number.
    • Explain why we add a column of ones to the design matrix and what the normal equation does.
    • Fit a LinearRegression in scikit-learn and correctly read off coef_ and intercept_.
    • Give a concrete example of why a good fit does not prove causation.

    Curated resources

    • StatQuest — Linear Regression playlist — Josh Starmer's gentle, visual walk-through of fitting lines and least squares; perfect first watch.
    • Andrew Ng's Machine Learning Specialization — Course 1 covers exactly this hypothesis and cost function; the canonical beginner treatment.
    • scikit-learn User Guide — Linear Models — the official reference for LinearRegression and its many cousins you'll meet later.
    • An Introduction to Statistical Learning (free PDF) — Chapter 3 is a rigorous-but-readable deep dive into linear regression and interpreting coefficients.
    Module 4

    Gradient Descent: How Models Learn

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Picture a model's cost as a landscape and explain why "learning" means walking downhill on it.
    • Say in plain words what a gradient is, and why we step in the negative gradient direction.
    • Apply the update rule \( w := w - \alpha \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) by hand, and explain what each piece means.
    • Choose a sensible learning rate \( \alpha \) and predict what happens when it is too big or too small.
    • Distinguish batch, mini-batch, and stochastic gradient descent, and say when you'd use each.
    • Write a correct gradient-descent loop from scratch in NumPy that fits a line, and run the same fit with scikit-learn.

    Why should I care?

    In Module 3 — Linear Regression you met a model \( \hat{y} = wx + b \) and a cost (mean squared error) that scores how wrong it is. But we cheated: we said "find the \( w \) and \( b \) that make the cost small" without saying how. Gradient descent is the how. It is the single algorithm that trains almost everything you'll ever build — linear regression, logistic regression (Module 5), and every neural network and large language model in Modules 10–14. When ChatGPT was trained, the same idea you'll code in 15 lines today was run billions of times over trillions of words. Learn it once, deeply, and you understand the engine under the entire field. Concretely: imagine you run a coffee cart and want to predict daily sales from the temperature. You have a hunch sales rise with heat, but by how much? Gradient descent is the procedure that nudges your guess for "extra cups per degree" up and down until it best matches what actually happened.

    Intuition first

    A model has knobs (we call them parameters: the weight \( w \) and the bias \( b \)). For every setting of the knobs there is a single number that says "how badly are we doing?" — the cost. If you imagine the knobs as a position and the cost as height, you get a landscape: hills where the model is bad, valleys where it is good. Training is just finding the bottom of a valley.

    The foggy mountain. You're standing somewhere on a hillside in thick fog. You can't see the bottom of the valley, but you can feel the slope under your feet. A perfectly reasonable plan: feel which way is steepest downhill, take a small step that way, and repeat. Step by step you descend, even though you never saw the whole mountain. Gradient descent is exactly this — the "slope under your feet" is the gradient, and your step size is the learning rate. The catch (which we'll return to): if you only ever walk downhill, you might settle in a small dip that isn't the deepest valley. That's the local minimum problem.

    A second picture that beginners find sticky: a ball rolling in a bowl. Drop a marble into a smooth bowl and it rolls toward the lowest point, slowing as it nears the bottom. For the simple cost shapes in this module the landscape really is a bowl (a single lowest point), so the ball-in-a-bowl image is not just cute — it's accurate.

    Each green dot is one update. The steeper the bowl, the bigger the step; near the bottom the slope flattens and steps shrink, so we glide gently into the minimum.

    Notice something the picture already tells us: where the bowl is steep, the slope is large, so we move a lot; where it flattens out near the bottom, the slope is near zero, so we barely move. Gradient descent is self-braking. That's why it doesn't fly past the minimum (as long as the steps aren't too big — more on that under learning rate).

    Play with it: press Step to take one update on the bowl J(w) = (w − 3)², or Run to auto-descend. Drag the learning-rate slider: around 0.1–0.3 the ball glides to w = 3, a tiny α crawls, and α ≥ 1.0 makes it overshoot and diverge. Watch the red slope line (the gradient) flatten as it nears the bottom.

    Making it precise

    Let's name things. We want to minimize a cost function \( J \) — a number measuring total error — by choosing a parameter \( w \) (a single adjustable number, the "knob"). The key quantity is the derivative of the cost with respect to the knob, written \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) and read "the partial derivative of \( J \) with respect to \( w \)." In plain words it answers one question:

    "If I nudge \( w \) up by a tiny amount, does the cost go up or down, and how fast?" A positive value means "increasing \( w \) increases the cost" (so we should decrease \( w \)); a negative value means "increasing \( w \) decreases the cost" (so we should increase \( w \)); a value near zero means "we're on flat ground — possibly at the bottom." The word partial just signals that when there are several knobs (like \( w \) and \( b \)), we measure the slope in one knob's direction at a time, holding the others fixed.

    The collection of all the partial derivatives, one per parameter, is the gradient, written \( \nabla J \) (the symbol is "nabla"). The gradient is a list of slopes that, taken together, points in the direction of steepest ascent — the fastest way uphill. We want to go down, so we step in the opposite direction: the negative gradient. That single fact is the whole algorithm.

    Here is the update rule. Read \( := \) as "becomes" (assignment, like in code):

    \[ w := w - \alpha \, \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \]

    In words: "the new value of \( w \) is the old value, minus the learning rate times the slope at the current point." Each symbol:

    • \( w \) — the parameter we're tuning (a number).
    • \( \alpha \) — the learning rate (read "alpha"), a small positive number you choose, like \( 0.1 \) or \( 0.01 \). It controls how big a step we take. It is a hyperparameter: a setting you pick, not something the algorithm learns.
    • \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) — the slope of the cost at the current \( w \): how cost changes when \( w \) changes.
    • The minus sign — this is what makes us go downhill. If the slope is positive (cost rising as \( w \) grows), we subtract a positive number and \( w \) shrinks; if the slope is negative, subtracting a negative grows \( w \). Either way we move toward lower cost.

    When there are two knobs, we just apply the same rule to each, using each one's own partial derivative:

    \[ w := w - \alpha \, \frac{\partial J}{\partial w}, \qquad b := b - \alpha \, \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} \]

    We compute both slopes first from the current \( (w, b) \), then update both — never update \( w \) and then recompute \( b \)'s slope from the changed \( w \). They move together, one synchronized step.

    The exact gradients for linear regression (MSE)

    For linear regression the prediction is \( \hat{y}_i = w x_i + b \) for example \( i \), where \( x_i \) is the input and \( \hat{y}_i \) (read "y-hat") is the model's guess. With \( n \) examples and true labels \( y_i \), the mean squared error cost is:

    \[ J(w, b) = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \hat{y}_i - y_i \right)^2 = \frac{1}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( w x_i + b - y_i \right)^2 \]

    where \( \sum_{i=1}^{n} \) means "add up over all \( n \) examples," and \( (\hat{y}_i - y_i) \) is the residual (how far off prediction \( i \) is). Squaring makes every error positive and punishes big misses more. The slopes work out (using the chain rule from Module 1 — Just-Enough Math Intuition; the factor of 2 falls out of differentiating the square) to:

    \[ \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} = \frac{2}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \hat{y}_i - y_i \right) x_i, \qquad \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} = \frac{2}{n} \sum_{i=1}^{n} \left( \hat{y}_i - y_i \right) \]

    You don't need to derive these by hand right now — but read them in words, because the words are intuitive. The slope for the bias \( b \) is just (twice) the average residual: if we're predicting too low on average, the residuals are negative, the slope is negative, and the update raises \( b \). The slope for \( w \) is (twice) the average residual weighted by the input \( x_i \): an example with a large \( x \) pulls harder on the slope, because changing \( w \) affects its prediction more. Keep these two formulas nearby — you'll code them in a minute.

    Worked example — by hand

    Part A: a one-knob bowl. Forget data for a moment and just minimize the clean cost \( J(w) = (w - 3)^2 \). This is a bowl whose lowest point is obviously at \( w = 3 \) (where the cost is 0). Let's pretend we don't know that and let gradient descent find it. The derivative of \( (w-3)^2 \) is \( \frac{dJ}{dw} = 2(w - 3) \) (chain rule: bring the power down, multiply by the inside's derivative, which is 1). We start at \( w = 0 \) with learning rate \( \alpha = 0.1 \), and apply \( w := w - 0.1 \cdot 2(w-3) \).

    • Step 1. At \( w = 0 \): slope \( = 2(0 - 3) = -6 \). Update: \( w = 0 - 0.1 \times (-6) = 0 + 0.6 = \mathbf{0.6} \). The slope was negative (cost falls as \( w \) rises), so we moved right, toward 3. Good.
    • Step 2. At \( w = 0.6 \): slope \( = 2(0.6 - 3) = -4.8 \). Update: \( w = 0.6 - 0.1 \times (-4.8) = 0.6 + 0.48 = \mathbf{1.08} \).
    • Step 3. At \( w = 1.08 \): slope \( = 2(1.08 - 3) = -3.84 \). Update: \( w = 1.08 + 0.384 = \mathbf{1.464} \).
    • Step 4. At \( w = 1.464 \): slope \( = 2(1.464 - 3) = -3.072 \). Update: \( w = 1.464 + 0.3072 = \mathbf{1.7712} \).

    The values march \( 0 \to 0.6 \to 1.08 \to 1.464 \to 1.7712 \to \dots \), each step smaller than the last (because the slope keeps shrinking as we near the bottom), homing in on \( w = 3 \). In fact there's a tidy pattern here: each step the distance to the target multiplies by \( (1 - 2\alpha) = 0.8 \). Check: distance starts at 3; after step 1 it's \( 3 - 0.6 = 2.4 = 0.8 \times 3 \); after step 2 it's \( 3 - 1.08 = 1.92 = 0.8 \times 2.4 \). So \( w \) never quite reaches 3, but gets exponentially close — that's why we run "enough" iterations rather than expecting a perfect landing.

    Part B: one real GD step on tiny data. Now a real (if minuscule) linear-regression fit. Two points: \( (x_1, y_1) = (1, 2) \) and \( (x_2, y_2) = (2, 3) \). Start at \( w = 0 \), \( b = 0 \), learning rate \( \alpha = 0.1 \), with \( n = 2 \).

    1. Predict. \( \hat{y}_1 = 0 \cdot 1 + 0 = 0 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 0 \cdot 2 + 0 = 0 \).
    2. Residuals \( (\hat{y}_i - y_i) \): \( 0 - 2 = -2 \) and \( 0 - 3 = -3 \). Both negative — we're predicting too low everywhere, as expected from an all-zero model.
    3. Slope for \( w \): \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} = \frac{2}{2}\big[(-2)(1) + (-3)(2)\big] = 1 \times (-2 - 6) = -8 \).
    4. Slope for \( b \): \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} = \frac{2}{2}\big[(-2) + (-3)\big] = 1 \times (-5) = -5 \).
    5. Update both at once. \( w = 0 - 0.1 \times (-8) = \mathbf{0.8} \); \( b = 0 - 0.1 \times (-5) = \mathbf{0.5} \).

    Did cost actually drop? Before: residuals \( (-2, -3) \), so \( J = \frac{1}{2}\big[(-2)^2 + (-3)^2\big] = \frac{1}{2}(4 + 9) = 6.5 \). After: new predictions \( \hat{y}_1 = 0.8(1) + 0.5 = 1.3 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 0.8(2) + 0.5 = 2.1 \); residuals \( (1.3 - 2,\; 2.1 - 3) = (-0.7, -0.9) \); \( J = \frac{1}{2}\big[(-0.7)^2 + (-0.9)^2\big] = \frac{1}{2}(0.49 + 0.81) = 0.65 \). The cost fell from \( 6.5 \) to \( 0.65 \) in a single step. That is gradient descent earning its keep.

    The learning rate: the one dial you must respect

    The learning rate \( \alpha \) decides how far you step. It is the most common thing beginners get wrong, so let's make it concrete using the same bowl \( J(w) = (w-3)^2 \) starting at \( w = 0 \).

    • Just right (\( \alpha = 0.1 \)). As we saw: \( 0 \to 0.6 \to 1.08 \to 1.464 \to \dots \to 3 \). Smooth, steady descent.
    • Too small (\( \alpha = 0.001 \)). Step 1: \( w = 0 - 0.001 \times (-6) = 0.006 \). After step 1 you've moved \( 0.006 \) toward a target \( 3 \) units away. It will get there — but it might take thousands of iterations. Correct direction, painfully slow. You waste compute.
    • Too big (\( \alpha = 1.1 \)). Step 1: slope \( = -6 \), \( w = 0 - 1.1 \times (-6) = 6.6 \). You overshot the minimum (3) and landed further away on the other side (distance 3.6, was 3). Step 2: slope \( = 2(6.6 - 3) = 7.2 \), \( w = 6.6 - 1.1 \times 7.2 = 6.6 - 7.92 = -1.32 \). Now you've overshot back past 3 to the other side again, even further out. The values bounce \( 0 \to 6.6 \to -1.32 \to \dots \) with the distance growing every step. This is divergence — the cost explodes to infinity. Recall the rule from Part A: the distance to the target multiplies by \( (1 - 2\alpha) \) each step. With \( \alpha = 1.1 \) that factor is \( 1 - 2.2 = -1.2 \), and a factor bigger than 1 in size means runaway growth.

    The practical lesson: when training and your cost is going up or printing nan (not-a-number, from overflow), your first suspect is "learning rate too high." Cut it by 10x and try again. When the cost barely moves, your learning rate may be too small (or your features need scaling — see pitfalls). A good habit is to try \( \alpha \in \{0.001, 0.01, 0.1\} \) and watch the cost curve.

    Left: tiny steps crawl down. Middle: well-sized steps converge cleanly. Right: oversized steps bounce up the walls and fly apart (divergence).

    Batch, mini-batch, and stochastic gradient descent

    Every gradient formula above sums over all \( n \) examples. With a few hundred rows that's fine. With millions (or the trillions of tokens an LLM trains on), computing the full sum for every single step is wastefully slow. The three flavours differ only in how much data you look at before taking each step:

    • Batch gradient descent. Use all examples to compute the gradient for each step. Pros: the gradient is exact, so the path is smooth and stable. Cons: each step is expensive; one step needs a full pass over the data. This is what we'll code from scratch — it's the clearest.
    • Stochastic gradient descent (SGD). Use one randomly-chosen example per step. Pros: dirt cheap per step, so you take many steps fast; the randomness can even jiggle you out of shallow dips. Cons: each gradient is a noisy estimate of the true one, so the path zig-zags toward the minimum instead of gliding.
    • Mini-batch gradient descent. Use a small group — a mini-batch, typically 32 to 256 examples — per step. This is the practical sweet spot everyone actually uses: far cheaper than batch, far less noisy than pure SGD, and it maps beautifully onto GPU hardware. One full pass through all the mini-batches is called an epoch.

    A useful mental model: batch GD takes a few confident strides; SGD takes many tipsy little steps; mini-batch takes brisk, mostly-sober steps. All three head downhill; they just trade accuracy-per-step against steps-per-second. You'll lean on mini-batch SGD constantly once we reach neural networks in Module 10 and Module 11 — Training Deep Networks That Actually Work.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's fit a line \( \hat{y} = wx + b \) to data that secretly follows \( y \approx 2x + 1 \) (plus a little noise), using nothing but NumPy and the exact MSE gradients above. We'll print the cost every so often so you can watch it fall.

    import numpy as np

# --- make some data that roughly follows y = 2x + 1 ---
rng = np.random.default_rng(0)          # fixed seed -> reproducible
x = np.linspace(0, 5, 50)               # 50 inputs from 0 to 5
y = 2.0 * x + 1.0 + rng.normal(0, 0.5, size=x.shape)   # true line + noise
n = len(x)

# --- start the knobs at zero ---
w, b = 0.0, 0.0
alpha = 0.01                            # learning rate
epochs = 1000                           # how many full passes (batch GD)

def cost(w, b):
    yhat = w * x + b
    return np.mean((yhat - y) ** 2)     # mean squared error

for epoch in range(epochs):
    yhat = w * x + b                    # predictions for ALL points
    error = yhat - y                    # residuals (y-hat minus y)
    # exact MSE gradients:
    dw = (2 / n) * np.sum(error * x)    # dJ/dw
    db = (2 / n) * np.sum(error)        # dJ/db
    # one synchronized step downhill:
    w = w - alpha * dw
    b = b - alpha * db
    if epoch % 200 == 0:
        print(f"epoch {epoch:4d}  cost {cost(w, b):.4f}  w {w:.3f}  b {b:.3f}")

print(f"\nlearned:  w = {w:.3f},  b = {b:.3f}   (true was w=2, b=1)")

    Running this prints a cost that drops every line and parameters that climb toward the truth, ending near \( w \approx 2 \), \( b \approx 1 \). Your exact decimals depend on the random noise, but the shape of the output is always the same — something like:

    epoch    0  cost 31.1602  w 0.395  b 0.121
epoch  200  cost 0.1789   w 2.130  b 0.729
epoch  400  cost 0.1784   w 2.121  b 0.757
epoch  600  cost 0.1784   w 2.118  b 0.768
epoch  800  cost 0.1783   w 2.117  b 0.772

learned:  w = 2.116,  b = 0.773   (true was w=2, b=1)

    Read the story in those numbers: the cost crashes from over 30 down to roughly 0.18 and then stops improving — that floor isn't a failure, it's the leftover noise we sprinkled in (we can't fit randomness). With noise standard deviation 0.5 you'd expect a floor near \( 0.5^2 = 0.25 \); we land a touch below it (about 0.18) because the fitted line also soaks up a little of the random wiggle in these 50 particular points. The learned \( w \approx 2.12 \) sits right next to the true \( 2 \), and \( b \approx 0.77 \) lands in the neighbourhood of the true \( 1 \) — it doesn't hit it exactly because the noise in this small sample pulls the best-fit line slightly off the underlying truth. The two lines you'd flag if something broke: a cost that rises (learning rate too high) or a cost that's still falling fast at the last epoch (run more epochs, or raise the rate). If you set a different seed in default_rng you'll get slightly different numbers — that's expected and fine.

    In code: the library way

    Once you understand the loop, scikit-learn does it for you. SGDRegressor is linear regression trained by (mini-)stochastic gradient descent — the library version of everything above. One important real-world detail it forces on you: scale your features first (see pitfalls for why). We chain a scaler and the regressor with a Pipeline so the scaling is handled automatically.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.linear_model import SGDRegressor
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.pipeline import make_pipeline

rng = np.random.default_rng(0)
x = np.linspace(0, 5, 50)
y = 2.0 * x + 1.0 + rng.normal(0, 0.5, size=x.shape)
X = x.reshape(-1, 1)                    # sklearn wants a 2D array of features

model = make_pipeline(
    StandardScaler(),                  # center & scale x  -> faster, stabler GD
    SGDRegressor(loss="squared_error", # plain MSE (the cost we used)
                 learning_rate="constant", eta0=0.05,  # eta0 is alpha
                 max_iter=1000, random_state=0)
)
model.fit(X, y)

pred = model.predict([[2.5]])          # predict y when x = 2.5  (truth ~ 6.0)
print(f"prediction at x=2.5: {pred[0]:.3f}")
print(f"R^2 on training data: {model.score(X, y):.3f}")

    This prints a prediction near \( 6.0 \) for \( x = 2.5 \) (since \( 2 \times 2.5 + 1 = 6 \)) and an \( R^2 \) close to \( 1.0 \), meaning the line explains almost all the variation in \( y \). Note eta0 is scikit-learn's name for the learning rate \( \alpha \). For a closed-form, no-learning-rate fit you'd reach for LinearRegression (Module 3) — but SGDRegressor is the one that scales to huge data and shares its DNA with neural-network training.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Explain gradient descent to me with a brand-new analogy that isn't a mountain or a bowl, then point out where the analogy breaks down."
    • "Run my from-scratch NumPy code, then change alpha to 0.5 and to 0.0001 and show me the printed cost for each — explain what diverging and crawling look like in the numbers."
    • "Generate 5 by-hand practice problems where I do two gradient-descent steps on a quadratic cost, then check my arithmetic step by step."
    • "Pair with me to add mini-batch updates to my from-scratch loop (batch size 8) and review whether my shuffling and indexing are correct."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "Bigger learning rate = faster learning." Up to a point, yes — then it flips. Too big and you overshoot the minimum and the cost grows every step until it overflows to nan. If cost ever rises, your first fix is to shrink \( \alpha \) by 10x.
    • Forgetting to scale features. If one feature ranges 0–1 and another ranges 0–100000, the cost landscape becomes a long, skinny ravine instead of a round bowl. Gradient descent then zig-zags painfully slowly across the narrow direction. Standardizing features (subtract the mean, divide by the standard deviation) rounds out the bowl and is essential for GD-based methods. We'll go deep on this in Module 7 — Feature Engineering and Preprocessing.
    • Updating one parameter using the other's already-updated value. Compute all gradients from the current parameters first, then apply every update. Interleaving them breaks the math.
    • Thinking gradient descent always finds the best possible answer. For the bowl-shaped (convex) costs of linear and logistic regression it does — there's one valley. For neural networks the landscape has many valleys (local minima) and flat plateaus; GD finds a good minimum, not provably the global one. In practice good-enough is what we ship.
    • Confusing the learning rate with the number of epochs. \( \alpha \) sets how far each step goes; epochs set how many steps. A too-small \( \alpha \) can be partly rescued by more epochs, but a too-big \( \alpha \) cannot be fixed by any number of epochs — it diverges.
    • Dropping or mismatching the factor of 2 in the gradient. The MSE gradient has a \( \frac{2}{n} \). If you accidentally use \( \frac{1}{n} \), nothing breaks catastrophically — it's mathematically equivalent to halving your learning rate. But know which convention you're using so your \( \alpha \) means what you think.

    Exercises

    1. Using \( J(w) = (w-3)^2 \) with derivative \( 2(w-3) \), start at \( w = 5 \) with \( \alpha = 0.1 \) and compute the first two updates by hand. Is the cost decreasing?
      Show solution

      Step 1: slope \( = 2(5-3) = 4 \); \( w = 5 - 0.1 \times 4 = 4.6 \). Step 2: slope \( = 2(4.6-3) = 3.2 \); \( w = 4.6 - 0.1 \times 3.2 = 4.28 \). The values \( 5 \to 4.6 \to 4.28 \) move down toward 3 (this time from the right, so \( w \) decreases). Cost before: \( (5-3)^2 = 4 \); after step 1: \( (4.6-3)^2 = 2.56 \); after step 2: \( (4.28-3)^2 \approx 1.64 \). Decreasing, as expected.

    2. For the same bowl starting at \( w = 0 \), what does \( \alpha = 1.0 \) do? Compute two steps and describe the behaviour.
      Show solution

      Step 1: slope \( = 2(0-3) = -6 \); \( w = 0 - 1.0 \times (-6) = 6 \). Step 2: slope \( = 2(6-3) = 6 \); \( w = 6 - 1.0 \times 6 = 0 \). You bounce \( 0 \to 6 \to 0 \to 6 \to \dots \) forever, mirror-imaged across the minimum at 3, never getting closer. The distance-multiplier is \( 1 - 2\alpha = 1 - 2 = -1 \): magnitude exactly 1, so it neither converges nor diverges — it oscillates. Any \( \alpha \) larger than 1 would diverge; the borderline is at \( \alpha = 1 \).

    3. Take the 2-point dataset \( (1,2), (2,3) \) again, but start from the result of one step we computed earlier: \( w = 0.8 \), \( b = 0.5 \), with \( \alpha = 0.1 \), \( n = 2 \). Do one more full GD step (both parameters) and confirm cost drops below 0.65.
      Show solution

      Predict: \( \hat{y}_1 = 0.8(1)+0.5 = 1.3 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 0.8(2)+0.5 = 2.1 \). Residuals: \( 1.3-2 = -0.7 \), \( 2.1-3 = -0.9 \). Slopes: \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} = \frac{2}{2}[(-0.7)(1) + (-0.9)(2)] = -0.7 - 1.8 = -2.5 \); \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} = \frac{2}{2}[(-0.7)+(-0.9)] = -1.6 \). Update: \( w = 0.8 - 0.1(-2.5) = 1.05 \); \( b = 0.5 - 0.1(-1.6) = 0.66 \). New predictions: \( \hat{y}_1 = 1.05+0.66 = 1.71 \), \( \hat{y}_2 = 2.10+0.66 = 2.76 \); residuals \( -0.29, -0.24 \); cost \( = \frac{1}{2}(0.0841 + 0.0576) = \frac{1}{2}(0.1417) \approx 0.071 \). It dropped from 0.65 to about 0.071 — still falling toward the true line \( y = x + 1 \) (where \( w=1, b=1 \)).

    4. In the from-scratch NumPy code, why do we compute dw and db from yhat before updating w and b, rather than updating w and then recomputing yhat for db? What could go wrong otherwise?
      Show solution

      The gradient is defined at a single point \( (w, b) \) — both partial derivatives describe the slope of the landscape at the current location. If you update w first and then recompute error for db, you'd be measuring \( b \)'s slope at a different point than \( w \)'s, so the two updates no longer form one coherent downhill step. The result is a subtly wrong trajectory that can slow convergence or, in nastier landscapes, destabilize it. Always snapshot the predictions/residuals once, derive every gradient from that snapshot, then apply all updates together.

    5. You train a model and the printed cost goes \( 12 \to 40 \to 380 \to \text{nan} \). Name the most likely cause and give two concrete things to try.
      Show solution

      The cost is exploding, the classic signature of a learning rate that is too high — steps overshoot the minimum and grow each time until the numbers overflow to nan. Two fixes: (1) reduce \( \alpha \) by a factor of 10 (e.g. 0.05 → 0.005) and rerun; (2) standardize your features (subtract mean, divide by standard deviation) so the cost bowl is rounder and tolerant of larger steps. If both fail, double-check you didn't drop the \( \frac{1}{n} \) normalization in the gradient, which inflates the effective step size on large datasets.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Explain, without notes, why we step in the negative gradient direction.
    • State the update rule \( w := w - \alpha \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) and say what each symbol does.
    • Predict the qualitative behaviour (converge / crawl / diverge) for a learning rate that's just right, too small, or too big.
    • Write the MSE gradients \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial w} \) and \( \frac{\partial J}{\partial b} \) from memory and explain each in words.
    • Describe the difference between batch, mini-batch, and stochastic gradient descent in one sentence each.

    Projects: build it yourself

    Reading about descent isn't the same as feeling it work. Pick at least one of these and code it end to end. Each lists a goal, a dataset you can grab in minutes, concrete milestones, a success bar to hit, and a stretch if you want more.

    1. Project 1 — Gradient descent visualizer.
      • Goal: Build an animation that plots the bowl \( J(w) = (w-3)^2 \) and drops a dot that steps downhill, so you can see the self-braking behaviour.
      • Dataset: None needed — generate \( w \) values on a grid with np.linspace(-2, 8, 200) and plot \( J \) against them.
      • Milestones: (1) plot the parabola; (2) run GD from \( w = 0 \) storing every \( w \); (3) overlay the visited points as dots connected by arrows; (4) print the step number, \( w \), and cost each iteration.
      • Success: The dots visibly bunch up near \( w = 3 \) and the printed cost decreases monotonically with \( \alpha = 0.1 \).
      • Stretch: Add a slider (or a loop over \( \alpha \in \{0.01, 0.1, 0.9, 1.1\} \)) and capture, in one figure each, "crawl", "converge", "oscillate", and "diverge".
    2. Project 2 — From-scratch line fitter on real data.
      • Goal: Reuse your NumPy batch-GD loop to fit a single-feature linear model to a real dataset, and beat a naive "predict the mean" baseline.
      • Dataset: scikit-learn's diabetes dataset, using one feature (BMI): from sklearn.datasets import load_diabetes, then take column index 2 as \( x \) and the target as \( y \).
      • Milestones: (1) standardize \( x \) (subtract mean, divide by std); (2) run your GD loop; (3) plot the data and your fitted line; (4) compute MSE of your model vs. MSE of always predicting the mean of \( y \).
      • Success: Your model's MSE is clearly lower than the mean-baseline MSE, and the cost curve flattens out instead of rising.
      • Stretch: Add a second feature so \( \hat{y} = w_1 x_1 + w_2 x_2 + b \) and extend the gradients to both weights.
    3. Project 3 — Learning-rate sweep report.
      • Goal: Quantify, not just describe, how the learning rate changes convergence.
      • Dataset: The same synthetic \( y \approx 2x + 1 \) data from the code section (seed 0).
      • Milestones: (1) wrap your GD loop in a function returning the cost history; (2) run it for \( \alpha \in \{0.001, 0.01, 0.1, 0.3, 0.6\} \); (3) plot all cost curves on one chart with a log-scaled y-axis; (4) write one sentence per curve describing what happened.
      • Success: You can point to the smallest \( \alpha \) that still converges within 1000 epochs and the \( \alpha \) at which it starts to diverge.
      • Stretch: Add a simple decaying schedule \( \alpha_t = \alpha_0 / (1 + 0.001\,t) \) and compare it against the best constant rate.
    4. Project 4 — Batch vs. mini-batch vs. stochastic.
      • Goal: Implement all three flavours and compare their paths to the minimum.
      • Dataset: Generate 2000 points from \( y \approx 2x + 1 \) with noise (so the difference in cost-per-step is visible).
      • Milestones: (1) write batch GD; (2) write SGD (one random point per step); (3) write mini-batch (size 32, shuffled each epoch); (4) plot the cost vs. number of examples seen (not epochs) for all three on one chart.
      • Success: Batch is the smoothest curve, SGD is the noisiest, and mini-batch sits between — and all three reach a similar final cost.
      • Stretch: Time each method and report cost reached per second of wall-clock; explain why mini-batch usually wins in practice.
    5. Project 5 — Reproduce SGDRegressor by hand.
      • Goal: Convince yourself the library is doing exactly what you coded by matching its fitted coefficients.
      • Dataset: Any single-feature regression set (the synthetic data or diabetes BMI).
      • Milestones: (1) fit SGDRegressor inside a StandardScaler pipeline; (2) read out its learned slope and intercept; (3) standardize the data yourself and run your own GD until it converges; (4) compare your \( (w, b) \) against the library's to two decimals.
      • Success: Your hand-rolled coefficients agree with scikit-learn's to within a small tolerance (say, 0.05) on standardized data.
      • Stretch: Add L2 regularization (a \( \lambda w \) term in the weight gradient) and show your result tracks SGDRegressor(penalty="l2").

    Curated resources

    • StatQuest — Gradient Descent (YouTube) — Josh Starmer walks the bowl-and-steps picture with friendly, slow visuals; the perfect companion to this module.
    • Google ML Crash Course — Reducing Loss — short, interactive lessons on gradients and learning rates with sliders you can play with.
    • Andrew Ng — Machine Learning Specialization — Course 1 derives and implements gradient descent for linear regression in exactly the spirit of this module.
