    Module 5

    Logistic Regression and Classification

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain the difference between predicting a number (regression) and predicting a category (classification), and say why we predict a probability in between.
    • Use the sigmoid function \( \sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1+e^{-z}} \) to squash any score into a probability between 0 and 1, by hand and in code.
    • Describe what a decision boundary is, and explain why the 0.5 threshold is a convenient default rather than a law of nature.
    • Explain cross-entropy / log loss in plain words first, then compute it for a single prediction by hand.
    • Implement logistic regression from scratch in NumPy — sigmoid, log loss, and a gradient descent loop — and watch it learn a boundary.
    • Train the same model in three lines with scikit-learn, read predict_proba, and extend the idea to many classes with softmax.

    Why should I care?

    An email arrives. Is it spam, or not? A patient's blood test comes back. Does this point to a disease, or not? A user hovers over a "Buy" button. Will they click, or not? None of these questions wants a number like "spam-ness = 73.4". They want a decision — but a good system should also tell you how sure it is. "This email is 98% likely to be spam" lets you auto-delete it; "this email is 55% likely to be spam" tells you to keep it in the inbox just in case. Logistic regression is the simplest, most-used tool for exactly this: turning evidence into a calibrated probability and then into a yes/no call. It is the workhorse behind credit scoring, medical risk models, and click prediction — and, beautifully, it reuses everything you already learned in Module 3 — Linear Regression and Module 4 — Gradient Descent. You are not starting over; you are adding one small twist.

    Intuition first

    In Module 3 you built a model that produces a number: \( z = w x + b \), a straight line. That number can be anything — minus a million, zero, plus a thousand. For "will the user click?" a raw number is awkward: what would "click-ness = 42" even mean? We want an answer between "definitely no" and "definitely yes", and the natural language for that is probability: a value from 0 (no way) to 1 (certainly).

    So logistic regression does two steps. First it computes the same old linear score \( z = w x + b \) — call this the evidence. A big positive \( z \) means "lots of evidence for yes"; a big negative \( z \) means "lots of evidence for no"; \( z \) near zero means "on the fence". Second, it squashes that evidence into a probability using a special S-shaped curve called the sigmoid. Huge positive evidence maps to a probability near 1, huge negative evidence to near 0, and zero evidence to exactly 0.5 — a coin flip.

    Analogy — the dimmer switch. A regular light switch is brutal: fully on or fully off, nothing in between. That is what we'd get if we tried to output 0 or 1 directly. The sigmoid is a dimmer switch. As you turn the dial (increase the evidence \( z \)) the light brightens smoothly from off (probability 0) through half-bright (0.5) up to fully on (probability 1). It never quite hits pure 0 or pure 1 — there's always a sliver of doubt — and around the middle a small turn of the dial makes a big difference, while at the extremes you can crank the dial hard and barely change the brightness. That smoothness is not just pretty: it is exactly what lets gradient descent feel which way to nudge the dial.

    Here is the sigmoid curve. Notice the gentle S: flat at the far left near 0, steep through the middle, flat at the far right near 1.

    The sigmoid maps any real score \( z \) (horizontal) to a probability between 0 and 1 (vertical). At \( z=0 \) it passes through exactly 0.5.

    Play with it: drag w to stretch or squash the S-curve and b to slide it left or right. Watch the orange decision boundary (where w·x + b = 0) move, and see each point flip between predicted class 0 and class 1 as it crosses p = 0.5.

    Once we have a probability, making a decision is easy: pick a threshold. The default is 0.5 — if the predicted probability is at least 0.5, say "yes" (class 1); otherwise "no" (class 0). The set of inputs where the probability is exactly 0.5 is the decision boundary: the dividing line between the two predicted classes. With one input feature that boundary is a single point on the number line; with two features it is a straight line on a 2D plane.

    Making it precise

    Let \( x \) be an input feature (a number describing the example, e.g. an email's link-count). Let \( w \) be the weight (a number we adjust during training that says how strongly \( x \) pushes toward "yes") and \( b \) the bias (a number we adjust that shifts the whole curve left or right). The linear score, exactly as in Module 3, is

    \[ z = w x + b \]

    where \( z \) is the raw evidence — any real number. We turn \( z \) into a probability with the sigmoid function \( \sigma \) (the Greek letter "sigma"):

    \[ \sigma(z) = \frac{1}{1 + e^{-z}} \]

    Here \( e \approx 2.718 \) is Euler's number (a fixed constant), and \( e^{-z} \) means \( e \) raised to the power \( -z \). Read the formula in words: when \( z \) is large and positive, \( -z \) is large and negative, so \( e^{-z} \) is tiny (close to 0), so the denominator is about 1 and \( \sigma(z) \approx 1 \). When \( z \) is large and negative, \( e^{-z} \) is huge, so the fraction is tiny and \( \sigma(z) \approx 0 \). When \( z = 0 \), \( e^{0} = 1 \), so \( \sigma(0) = \frac{1}{1+1} = 0.5 \). That single formula produces the whole S-curve.

    We write the model's predicted probability that the example is class 1 as

    \[ \hat{y} = \sigma(w x + b) \]

    where \( \hat{y} \) (read "y-hat") is our estimate of \( P(\text{class} = 1) \). The predicted class is then class 1 if \( \hat{y} \ge 0.5 \), else class 0. Because \( \sigma(z) \ge 0.5 \) happens exactly when \( z \ge 0 \), the decision boundary is simply the place where \( w x + b = 0 \).

    How wrong is a probability? Cross-entropy, in words first

    In Module 3 we measured error with squared distance, \( (\hat{y} - y)^2 \). For probabilities that is a poor fit. We want a loss that does two things: it should be near zero when we confidently predict the correct class, and it should grow very large when we confidently predict the wrong class. Confident-and-wrong should hurt a lot — that is how the model learns humility.

    The loss that does this is log loss (also called cross-entropy). The intuition: it asks "what probability did you assign to the answer that actually happened?" and then penalises you by the negative logarithm of that probability. If you said the right answer was 99% likely and it happened, \( -\ln(0.99) \) is almost 0 — barely any penalty. If you said it was 1% likely and it still happened, \( -\ln(0.01) \approx 4.6 \) — a big penalty. The logarithm is what makes overconfident mistakes explode toward infinity.

    For a single example with true label \( y \in \{0, 1\} \) (0 or 1) and predicted probability \( \hat{y} \), the formula is

    \[ L(\hat{y}, y) = -\big[\, y \ln(\hat{y}) + (1 - y)\ln(1 - \hat{y}) \,\big] \]

    where \( \ln \) is the natural logarithm (log base \( e \)). This looks like two terms but only one is ever "switched on": if the true label \( y = 1 \), then \( (1-y) = 0 \) kills the second term and the loss is \( -\ln(\hat{y}) \) — penalise by how little probability you gave to "yes". If \( y = 0 \), the first term vanishes and the loss is \( -\ln(1 - \hat{y}) \) — penalise by how little probability you gave to "no". The \( y \) and \( (1-y) \) are just a tidy on/off switch for picking the right penalty. Averaging \( L \) over all training examples gives the total cost we minimise.

    Worked example — by hand

    Part 1 — squashing scores with the sigmoid. Let's compute \( \sigma(z) \) for three scores, using \( e \approx 2.718 \).

    • \( z = 0 \): \( e^{-0} = 1 \), so \( \sigma(0) = \frac{1}{1+1} = \frac{1}{2} = 0.5 \). Zero evidence, a perfect coin flip.
    • \( z = 2 \): \( e^{-2} = \frac{1}{e^{2}} = \frac{1}{2.718^2} = \frac{1}{7.389} \approx 0.135 \). Then \( \sigma(2) = \frac{1}{1 + 0.135} = \frac{1}{1.135} \approx 0.881 \). Strong positive evidence, about 88% confident in "yes".
    • \( z = -1 \): \( e^{-(-1)} = e^{1} = 2.718 \). Then \( \sigma(-1) = \frac{1}{1 + 2.718} = \frac{1}{3.718} \approx 0.269 \). Negative evidence, about 27% — i.e. roughly 73% confident in "no".

    Sanity check: as \( z \) went from \( -1 \) to \( 0 \) to \( 2 \), the probability climbed from 0.27 to 0.50 to 0.88. More evidence, more confidence, smoothly. Good.

    Part 2 — the cost of a single prediction. Suppose the model predicts \( \hat{y} = 0.9 \) (90% sure it's class 1). Compare two worlds.

    • If the true label is \( y = 1 \) (we were right): loss \( = -\ln(0.9) = -(-0.105) \approx 0.105 \). Small — we are gently nudged because we were confident and correct, but not perfectly certain.
    • If the true label is \( y = 0 \) (we were confidently wrong): loss \( = -\ln(1 - 0.9) = -\ln(0.1) = -(-2.302) \approx 2.302 \). More than twenty times larger. The model is strongly punished for being sure of the wrong thing.

    That asymmetry — 0.105 versus 2.302 for the very same prediction — is the whole point of log loss. It teaches the model to be confident only when it has earned it.

    Reusing gradient descent — no new optimizer

    Here is the delightful part. To train the model we minimise the average log loss by gradient descent, exactly the procedure from Module 4 — Gradient Descent. We just need the gradients (the slopes telling us which way to nudge \( w \) and \( b \)). Remarkably, after the calculus settles, they look identical in shape to linear regression's:

    \[ \frac{\partial L}{\partial w} = (\hat{y} - y)\,x, \qquad \frac{\partial L}{\partial b} = (\hat{y} - y) \]

    In words: the gradient is the prediction error \( (\hat{y} - y) \) times the input \( x \) (for the weight) or just the error (for the bias). The only change from Module 3 is that \( \hat{y} \) is now \( \sigma(wx+b) \) instead of the raw \( wx+b \). So the update rule is unchanged:

    \[ w \leftarrow w - \alpha\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial w}, \qquad b \leftarrow b - \alpha\,\frac{\partial L}{\partial b} \]

    where \( \alpha \) (alpha) is the learning rate, a small positive number controlling step size, and \( \leftarrow \) means "becomes". You already know how to run this loop. Logistic regression is linear regression wearing a sigmoid hat and a log-loss coat.

    With two features, the decision boundary is a straight line. Points on one side get probability above 0.5 (predicted blue), points on the other side below 0.5 (predicted green).

    In code: from scratch

    Let's build the whole thing in NumPy on a tiny 2D dataset: blue points (class 0) clustered low-left, green points (class 1) high-right. We implement the sigmoid, the log loss, and a plain gradient descent loop, then watch the loss fall and read off the learned boundary.

    import numpy as np

# --- tiny 2D dataset: 4 points class 0 (lower-left), 4 points class 1 (upper-right)
X = np.array([
    [1.0, 1.0], [1.5, 0.5], [0.5, 1.5], [1.0, 2.0],   # class 0
    [4.0, 4.0], [4.5, 3.5], [3.5, 4.5], [4.0, 5.0],   # class 1
])
y = np.array([0, 0, 0, 0, 1, 1, 1, 1], dtype=float)

def sigmoid(z):
    return 1.0 / (1.0 + np.exp(-z))

def log_loss(y_true, y_hat):
    eps = 1e-12  # clip so we never take log(0)
    y_hat = np.clip(y_hat, eps, 1 - eps)
    return -np.mean(y_true * np.log(y_hat) + (1 - y_true) * np.log(1 - y_hat))

# --- parameters: one weight per feature, plus a bias
w = np.zeros(2)     # [w1, w2]
b = 0.0
alpha = 0.1         # learning rate
n = len(y)

for step in range(2001):
    z = X @ w + b               # linear score for every example
    y_hat = sigmoid(z)          # squash to probabilities
    error = y_hat - y           # (y_hat - y), the shared gradient piece
    grad_w = (X.T @ error) / n  # average gradient for the weights
    grad_b = error.mean()       # average gradient for the bias
    w -= alpha * grad_w         # gradient descent step
    b -= alpha * grad_b
    if step % 500 == 0:
        print(f"step {step:4d}  loss {log_loss(y, y_hat):.4f}  w={np.round(w,3)} b={b:.3f}")

# --- evaluate
probs = sigmoid(X @ w + b)
preds = (probs >= 0.5).astype(int)
print("predicted classes:", preds)
print("accuracy:", (preds == y).mean())

    Running this prints a loss that starts near 0.693 (that's \( -\ln 0.5 \), the loss of a model guessing 0.5 for everything) and steadily falls toward roughly 0.05 as the weights grow. The final line reports accuracy: 1.0 — all eight points classified correctly. The decision boundary is wherever X @ w + b == 0; because both learned weights are positive, that boundary is the diagonal line separating lower-left from upper-right, exactly matching the picture above. Notice we wrote no new optimizer: the error = y_hat - y trick is the same gradient shape you used in Module 4.

    In code: the library way

    Once you understand the mechanics, scikit-learn does all of it in a few lines — and adds predict_proba so you can read the calibrated probabilities, not just the hard labels. Here we use the classic Iris dataset, reduced to a binary problem: is the flower the species setosa, or not?

    from sklearn.datasets import load_iris
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split

iris = load_iris()
X = iris.data                       # 4 features per flower
y = (iris.target == 0).astype(int) # 1 if species 'setosa', else 0

X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.3, random_state=42)

model = LogisticRegression()
model.fit(X_train, y_train)         # gradient-based optimisation, under the hood

print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test, y_test))

# probabilities for the first 3 test flowers: P(class 0), P(class 1)
print(model.predict_proba(X_test[:3]).round(3))
print("hard predictions:", model.predict(X_test[:3]))

    This prints test accuracy: 1.0 (setosa is famously easy to separate), then a small table where each row is two numbers summing to 1 — for example [0.998 0.002] meaning "99.8% not-setosa, 0.2% setosa". The .predict method just applies the 0.5 threshold to those probabilities for you. Same idea as your from-scratch loop, fewer keystrokes, plus battle-tested numerical care.

    More than two classes: softmax

    What if you have three or more categories — say all three Iris species, or the ten handwritten digits 0–9? We generalise the sigmoid to the softmax function. Instead of one score \( z \), we compute one score \( z_k \) per class \( k \), then convert the whole bundle of scores into a set of probabilities that are all positive and sum to 1:

    \[ P(\text{class}=k) = \frac{e^{z_k}}{\sum_{j} e^{z_j}} \]

    where \( z_k \) is the score for class \( k \), and the denominator \( \sum_j e^{z_j} \) adds up \( e^{z_j} \) over every class \( j \) so the probabilities are normalised. In words: exponentiate every score (making them all positive), then divide each by the total. The biggest score gets the biggest probability; the chosen class is the one with the highest probability. The matching loss is categorical cross-entropy, the same "negative log of the probability you gave the true class" idea extended to many classes. Scikit-learn handles this automatically — call LogisticRegression on a target with three labels and it does the multiclass version for you. You will meet softmax again as the last layer of classification neural networks in Module 10 — Neural Network Fundamentals.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Explain why we use log loss instead of squared error for logistic regression, using a different analogy than the dimmer switch, and show me what goes wrong if I use squared error."
    • "Run my from-scratch logistic regression code, then change the learning rate to 1.0 and to 0.001 and show me how the loss curve and final boundary change. Explain what you see."
    • "Generate 5 practice problems on computing sigmoid and log loss by hand with small numbers, wait for my answers, then check them and explain any mistakes."
    • "Pair with me to extend my NumPy code to three classes using softmax and categorical cross-entropy, and review my implementation line by line."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "Logistic regression is a kind of regression, so it predicts numbers." The name is a historical accident. It is a classification algorithm. The only number it predicts is a probability, which you immediately turn into a class label.
    • Judging an imbalanced problem by accuracy. If 99% of emails are legitimate, a lazy model that always says "not spam" scores 99% accuracy while catching zero spam. On imbalanced data, accuracy lies — use precision, recall, or AUC instead (you'll meet these in Module 6 — Model Evaluation).
    • Treating the 0.5 threshold as sacred. 0.5 is just the default. For cancer screening you might flag anyone above 0.1 (better to over-refer than miss a case); for an expensive automated action you might require 0.9. Move the threshold to trade off false alarms against misses.
    • Reading probabilities as certainties. A predicted 0.7 does not mean the model is "70% right"; it means that, among inputs like this one, roughly 70% are class 1. Probabilities describe frequencies, not guarantees.
    • Forgetting to scale features. Like linear regression, logistic regression converges faster and behaves better when features are on similar scales. If one feature is in thousands and another in fractions, standardise them first (Module 7 — Feature Engineering).
    • Confusing sigmoid with softmax. Sigmoid is for binary (one probability); softmax is for multiclass (a probability per class that sum to 1). Using two sigmoids for two classes is fine; using a sigmoid for ten classes is not.

    Exercises

    1. Compute \( \sigma(z) \) by hand for \( z = 1 \) and \( z = -2 \). Use \( e \approx 2.718 \). Which prediction is more confident, and in which direction?
      Show solution

      For \( z = 1 \): \( e^{-1} = 1/2.718 \approx 0.368 \), so \( \sigma(1) = \frac{1}{1+0.368} = \frac{1}{1.368} \approx 0.731 \) — about 73% confident in class 1.

      For \( z = -2 \): \( e^{2} \approx 7.389 \), so \( \sigma(-2) = \frac{1}{1+7.389} = \frac{1}{8.389} \approx 0.119 \) — about 12% for class 1, i.e. ~88% confident in class 0.

      \( z = -2 \) is the more confident prediction (0.119 is further from the uncertain 0.5 than 0.731 is), and it points toward class 0.

    2. A model predicts \( \hat{y} = 0.6 \). Compute the log loss if the true label is \( y = 1 \), and again if \( y = 0 \). Which is larger and why?
      Show solution

      If \( y = 1 \): loss \( = -\ln(0.6) \approx -(-0.511) = 0.511 \).

      If \( y = 0 \): loss \( = -\ln(1 - 0.6) = -\ln(0.4) \approx -(-0.916) = 0.916 \).

      The \( y = 0 \) case is larger because the model leaned the wrong way (it gave only 0.4 probability to the correct class 0), so it is penalised more than when it leaned the right way.

    3. The decision boundary is where \( w x + b = 0 \). If \( w = 2 \) and \( b = -4 \) for a single feature \( x \), at what value of \( x \) does the prediction flip from class 0 to class 1? Predict the class for \( x = 1 \) and \( x = 3 \).
      Show solution

      Boundary: \( 2x - 4 = 0 \Rightarrow x = 2 \). For \( x \gt 2 \), \( z \gt 0 \) so \( \hat{y} \gt 0.5 \) (class 1); for \( x \lt 2 \), class 0.

      \( x = 1 \): \( z = 2(1) - 4 = -2 \), \( \sigma(-2) \approx 0.12 \) → class 0. \( x = 3 \): \( z = 2(3) - 4 = 2 \), \( \sigma(2) \approx 0.88 \) → class 1.

    4. In the from-scratch code, the gradient line is error = y_hat - y. Explain why the bias gradient is just the mean of error while the weight gradient multiplies by X. (Hint: revisit the formulas under "Reusing gradient descent".)
      Show solution

      The per-example gradients are \( \partial L/\partial w = (\hat{y}-y)\,x \) and \( \partial L/\partial b = (\hat{y}-y) \). The bias has no input attached (its "feature" is the constant 1), so its gradient is just the error itself; averaging over examples gives error.mean(). Each weight \( w_i \) is tied to feature \( x_i \), so its gradient scales the error by that feature value — in matrix form X.T @ error / n computes the average of \( (\hat{y}-y)\,x_i \) for every feature at once.

    5. Challenge. Suppose 5% of transactions are fraud. A model always predicts "not fraud". What is its accuracy, and why is that a terrible model? Suggest a better threshold strategy and a better metric.
      Show solution

      Accuracy = 95% (it is right on every legitimate transaction, which are 95% of the data), yet it catches zero fraud — useless for the actual goal. This is the imbalanced-data trap. Better: lower the decision threshold well below 0.5 so borderline-suspicious transactions get flagged (accepting more false alarms to catch more fraud), and evaluate with recall (fraction of true fraud caught) and precision (fraction of flags that are real), or the area under the precision–recall curve, instead of raw accuracy. You'll formalise these in Module 6.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Explain in one breath how logistic regression turns \( wx + b \) into a class label, naming \( z \), \( \sigma \), and the threshold.
    • Compute \( \sigma(z) \) and the log loss for a single example by hand.
    • Say why confident-and-wrong predictions get punished much harder than confident-and-right ones.
    • Point to where the decision boundary lives and explain why 0.5 is a choice, not a law.
    • Describe how softmax extends the idea to three or more classes.

    Projects — build something real

    Reading and hand-calculation get you understanding; shipping a small project gets you fluency. Pick one (or work through several) and do it end to end. Each lists a concrete goal, a dataset you can grab today, milestones to pace yourself, a success bar, and a stretch for when you want more.

    Project 1 — Spam vs. ham classifier

    • Goal: Train a logistic regression model that labels a short text message as spam (1) or not-spam (0) and reports a calibrated probability for each message.
    • Dataset: The SMS Spam Collection (~5,500 labelled texts), available from the UCI Machine Learning Repository or as a Kaggle download.
    • Milestones: (1) Load the data and split into train/test. (2) Turn each message into numeric features with CountVectorizer or TfidfVectorizer. (3) Fit LogisticRegression and print test accuracy. (4) Inspect predict_proba on five messages and eyeball whether the confidence makes sense.
    • Success: Test accuracy above 0.95, and you can explain one false positive and one false negative by reading the message.
    • Stretch: Plot precision and recall as you sweep the threshold from 0.1 to 0.9, and pick the threshold you would actually deploy if missing spam costs more than a false alarm.

    Project 2 — From-scratch trainer, no scikit-learn

    • Goal: Reproduce the from-scratch NumPy model above on a real (small) dataset and match scikit-learn's accuracy within a couple of percent.
    • Dataset: The Breast Cancer Wisconsin dataset, built into scikit-learn via load_breast_cancer() (569 examples, 30 features, binary target).
    • Milestones: (1) Standardise the features (subtract mean, divide by standard deviation) so gradient descent behaves. (2) Run your own sigmoid + log-loss + gradient-descent loop. (3) Track the loss every 100 steps and confirm it decreases. (4) Compare your accuracy to a fitted LogisticRegression.
    • Success: Your hand-rolled model lands within 2 percentage points of scikit-learn on a held-out test set, and the loss curve is monotonically (mostly) decreasing.
    • Stretch: Add L2 regularisation (subtract \( \lambda w \) from each weight gradient) and observe how it shrinks the weights and affects test accuracy.

    Project 3 — Threshold tuning for an imbalanced problem

    • Goal: Take an imbalanced dataset and show concretely why accuracy misleads, then choose a threshold that optimises a metric you actually care about.
    • Dataset: The Credit Card Fraud Detection dataset on Kaggle (284,807 transactions, only ~0.17% fraud) — heavily imbalanced by design.
    • Milestones: (1) Fit logistic regression and note the deceptively high accuracy. (2) Compute precision, recall, and the confusion matrix at threshold 0.5. (3) Sweep the threshold and plot the precision-recall curve. (4) Report the threshold that gives recall above 0.80.
    • Success: You can state, in one sentence, why the 99.8% accuracy model is useless and what threshold you would ship instead.
    • Stretch: Re-train with class_weight="balanced" and compare the precision-recall curve to the unweighted model.

    Project 4 — Multiclass with softmax

    • Goal: Move beyond binary: classify handwritten digits into ten classes (0 through 9) using softmax logistic regression.
    • Dataset: The digits dataset built into scikit-learn via load_digits() (1,797 8x8 grayscale images, ten classes).
    • Milestones: (1) Flatten each 8x8 image into a 64-length feature vector. (2) Fit LogisticRegression (it uses softmax for more than two classes automatically). (3) Print the test accuracy and the per-class confusion matrix. (4) Find the two digits most often confused with each other.
    • Success: Test accuracy above 0.93, and you can name which digit pair the model confuses most.
    • Stretch: Visualise the learned weight vector for one class as an 8x8 image and describe what pattern it has learned to detect.

    Project 5 — Probability calibration check

    • Goal: Test whether your model's stated probabilities are honest — when it says "70%", does the event really happen about 70% of the time?
    • Dataset: Any binary dataset from the projects above; the Titanic survival dataset (Kaggle) works well because the signal is moderate, not perfect.
    • Milestones: (1) Fit logistic regression and get test-set probabilities. (2) Bin predictions into ten buckets (0.0-0.1, 0.1-0.2, ...). (3) For each bucket, compare the average predicted probability to the actual fraction of positives. (4) Plot the two against each other — this is a reliability diagram.
    • Success: You produce a reliability diagram and can say whether the model is over-confident, under-confident, or well-calibrated.
    • Stretch: Apply CalibratedClassifierCV and show whether it pulls the reliability curve closer to the diagonal.

    Curated resources

    • StatQuest — Logistic Regression — Josh Starmer's clearest-on-the-internet visual walkthrough of the sigmoid and the fit.
    • Google ML Crash Course — short, hands-on lessons on logistic regression, log loss, and classification thresholds.
    • scikit-learn User Guide — the official reference for LogisticRegression, predict_proba, and multiclass options.
    • An Introduction to Statistical Learning (free PDF) — Chapter 4 gives a gentle but rigorous treatment of classification.
    Module 6

    Model Evaluation: Overfitting, Bias-Variance, Cross-Validation, Regularization

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Diagnose overfitting and underfitting by comparing training and test performance, and explain the train-test gap in plain words.
    • Explain the bias-variance tradeoff using the dartboard picture and say which way to push a model that is too simple or too wiggly.
    • Run k-fold cross-validation by hand and with scikit-learn, and say why a single train-test split can fool you.
    • Read a confusion matrix and compute accuracy, precision, recall, and F1 from it — and choose the right one for the job.
    • Pick sensible regression metrics (RMSE, MAE) and understand when each is preferable.
    • Apply regularization (Ridge / L2 and Lasso / L1) as the main lever for fighting overfitting, and explain what \( \lambda \) controls.

    Why should I care?

    Imagine you build a model to flag fraudulent credit-card transactions. You proudly report 99.5% accuracy. Sounds amazing — until you realize that only 0.5% of transactions are actually fraud, so a lazy model that screams "not fraud!" every single time also scores 99.5% and catches zero criminals. Accuracy lied to you. In this module you'll learn the tools that stop a number from fooling you: how to tell whether a model truly learned or just memorized, how to measure the things you actually care about (catching fraud, not just being "right"), and how to tune a model so it generalizes to data it has never seen. This is the difference between a demo that wows in a slide deck and a system that survives contact with the real world.

    Intuition first

    Every model has one real job: do well on data it has never seen before. That is what "learning" means. A model that scores perfectly on the examples it was trained on but flops on new examples hasn't learned anything useful — it just memorized.

    To catch this, we split our data into two piles before training:

    • The training set — the examples the model is allowed to study.
    • The test set — examples we hide away and only use at the very end to grade the model, like a final exam it never got to peek at.

    Now compare the two scores. The gap between them is your diagnostic:

    • Underfitting: the model is bad on the training set and the test set. It's too simple to capture the pattern — like trying to trace a curvy river with a single straight ruler.
    • Overfitting: the model is great on the training set but bad on the test set. It memorized the noise in the training data — like a student who memorized last year's exam answers word-for-word and is helpless when the questions change.
    • Just right: good on training, and nearly as good on test. The gap is small.

    The studying analogy. Underfitting is barely studying — you fail the practice tests and the real exam. Overfitting is memorizing the exact practice questions and answers without understanding — you ace the practice, then bomb the real exam because the questions are worded differently. What you actually want is to understand the concepts, so you do well on practice and on the real thing. The "concepts" are the genuine, repeatable pattern in the data; the "exact wording" is the noise.

    Here is the same idea as a picture. The dots are data points. The straight line on the left is too simple (underfit); the wild wiggly curve on the right passes through every single point but is clearly chasing noise (overfit); the gentle curve in the middle captures the trend without obsessing over each point.

    Same six points, three model complexities. The overfit curve nails every point but would predict nonsense between them.

    Play with it: the picture above is fixed, but the demo below is live. Drag the polynomial degree slider from 1 to 9 and watch the fitted curve change. Notice how the training error keeps falling as the degree rises, while the test error (on data the fit never saw) bottoms out in the middle and then climbs — the signature of overfitting. Hit "Resample data" to draw fresh noise and confirm the pattern is real, not a fluke of one dataset.

    The bias-variance tradeoff

    Why can't we just always use a more flexible model? Because flexibility cuts both ways. The classic way to understand this is the dartboard. Imagine you train your model many times on slightly different samples of data. Each trained model is a dart thrown at the bullseye (the true answer).

    • Bias is how far, on average, your darts land from the bullseye. High bias = consistently off in the same direction. This is what an underfit, too-simple model looks like: it keeps making the same kind of mistake no matter what data you give it.
    • Variance is how spread out your darts are from each other. High variance = the darts scatter wildly depending on which training sample you happened to get. This is what an overfit model looks like: tiny changes in the training data swing it dramatically.

    Green = the true target. Blue dots = models trained on different samples. Bias is distance from center; variance is spread.

    The tradeoff is this: as you make a model more flexible, bias goes down (it can fit more shapes) but variance goes up (it overreacts to noise). Make it simpler and the reverse happens. The total error you actually experience is, roughly:

    \[ \text{Expected test error} = \text{Bias}^2 + \text{Variance} + \text{irreducible noise} \]

    where bias measures systematic wrongness, variance measures sensitivity to the particular training sample, and the irreducible noise is randomness in the world that no model can ever predict (e.g., a fair coin flip). The sweet spot is the model complexity that minimizes the sum of the first two terms — not too simple, not too wiggly.

    Making it precise: cross-validation

    One train-test split has a problem: the score you get depends on which rows happened to land in the test set. Get an unlucky split and you'll judge a good model harshly; get a lucky split and you'll over-praise a bad one. With small datasets this luck-of-the-draw can swing your numbers by a lot.

    k-fold cross-validation fixes this by not relying on a single split. You chop the data into \( k \) equal-sized chunks called folds (a common choice is \( k = 5 \)). Then you train and test \( k \) times. Each round, one fold plays the role of the test set and the other \( k-1 \) folds are the training set. Every row gets to be in the test set exactly once.

    5-fold CV: every fold is the test set once. Average the 5 scores for a stable estimate.

    Your final cross-validated score is the average of the \( k \) test scores. As a formula, if \( s_i \) is the score (say, accuracy) on fold \( i \):

    \[ \text{CV score} = \frac{1}{k} \sum_{i=1}^{k} s_i \]

    where \( k \) is the number of folds and \( s_i \) is the score when fold \( i \) was held out for testing. The spread of those \( k \) scores (their standard deviation) also tells you how stable the model is — a huge spread is itself a warning of high variance.

    Classification metrics: beyond accuracy

    For a yes/no classifier, every prediction falls into one of four buckets, summarized in a confusion matrix:

    • True Positive (TP): predicted positive, and it really was positive. (Correctly flagged fraud.)
    • False Positive (FP): predicted positive, but it was actually negative. (Flagged a legit purchase — a false alarm.)
    • False Negative (FN): predicted negative, but it was actually positive. (Missed real fraud.)
    • True Negative (TN): predicted negative, and it really was negative. (Correctly let a legit purchase through.)

    From these four counts we build the metrics. Each answers a different question:

    Accuracy — "Of all predictions, what fraction were correct?"

    \[ \text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP + TN}{TP + TN + FP + FN} \]

    Simple, but misleading on imbalanced data (recall the fraud example).

    Precision — "When the model says positive, how often is it right?"

    \[ \text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP + FP} \]

    High precision means few false alarms. You care about precision when a false positive is expensive — e.g., flagging a customer's legit card and getting it declined at a restaurant.

    Recall (also called sensitivity) — "Of all the actual positives, how many did we catch?"

    \[ \text{Recall} = \frac{TP}{TP + FN} \]

    High recall means few misses. You care about recall when a false negative is dangerous — e.g., a cancer screen that misses a real tumor.

    F1 score — a single number that balances precision and recall. It is their harmonic mean, which punishes the case where one is high and the other is low:

    \[ F_1 = 2 \cdot \frac{\text{Precision} \cdot \text{Recall}}{\text{Precision} + \text{Recall}} \]

    Here the only symbols are precision and recall as defined above; the leading 2 makes the formula equal 1 when both are perfect.

    The threshold, ROC, and AUC. Most classifiers don't output a hard yes/no — they output a probability, like 0.83. You then pick a threshold (commonly 0.5): predict positive if the probability is above it. Lower the threshold and you catch more positives (recall up) but raise false alarms (precision down). The ROC curve plots the true-positive rate against the false-positive rate as you sweep the threshold from 0 to 1. The AUC (Area Under the Curve) collapses that whole curve into one number from 0.5 (random guessing) to 1.0 (perfect), measuring how well the model ranks positives above negatives regardless of any single threshold.

    Regression metrics

    When you're predicting a number (a price, a temperature) rather than a class, two metrics dominate. Let \( y_i \) be the true value, \( \hat{y}_i \) ("y-hat") the predicted value, and \( n \) the number of examples.

    MAE (Mean Absolute Error) — the average size of the mistakes, treating an over-prediction and an under-prediction of the same size equally:

    \[ \text{MAE} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n} |y_i - \hat{y}_i| \]

    RMSE (Root Mean Squared Error) — square the errors before averaging, then take the square root to get back to original units:

    \[ \text{RMSE} = \sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n} (y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2} \]

    The squaring in RMSE means big mistakes hurt disproportionately, so RMSE is the metric to watch when a single large error is much worse than several small ones. MAE is more forgiving of outliers and easier to interpret ("on average we're off by $4,200"). You met the squared-error idea in Module 3 — Linear Regression; here it becomes a reporting metric.

    Regularization: the lever against overfitting

    Overfitting often shows up as huge weights. To pass through every noisy point, a model cranks its coefficients to extreme values, producing the wild wiggles you saw earlier. Regularization fights this by adding a penalty to the loss for being too "loud." The model now has to trade off fitting the data against keeping its weights small.

    L2 regularization (Ridge). Add the sum of squared weights to the loss:

    \[ \text{Loss}_{\text{ridge}} = \underbrace{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2}_{\text{fit the data}} + \underbrace{\lambda \sum_{j=1}^{p} w_j^2}_{\text{stay small}} \]

    Here \( w_j \) is the \( j \)-th weight (coefficient), \( p \) is the number of weights, and \( \lambda \) ("lambda") is the regularization strength — a knob you choose. When \( \lambda = 0 \) you get plain regression. As \( \lambda \) grows, the penalty dominates and weights are squeezed toward zero, making the model simpler (lower variance, higher bias). Ridge shrinks weights smoothly but rarely sets them exactly to zero.

    L1 regularization (Lasso). Same idea but penalize the absolute values of weights:

    \[ \text{Loss}_{\text{lasso}} = \frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(y_i - \hat{y}_i)^2 + \lambda \sum_{j=1}^{p} |w_j| \]

    The absolute-value penalty has a special effect: it can drive some weights exactly to zero, effectively deleting those features. That makes Lasso a built-in feature selector — handy when you suspect many of your features are useless. You'll choose \( \lambda \) the proper way (with cross-validation!) rather than by eyeballing it.

    Worked example — by hand

    A spam classifier is evaluated on 100 emails. Its confusion matrix is:

    • TP = 40 (spam correctly caught)
    • FP = 10 (good email wrongly marked spam)
    • FN = 20 (spam that slipped through)
    • TN = 30 (good email correctly delivered)

    Let's compute every metric, showing each fraction.

    Accuracy — correct predictions over all predictions:

    \[ \text{Accuracy} = \frac{TP + TN}{TP+TN+FP+FN} = \frac{40 + 30}{40+30+10+20} = \frac{70}{100} = 0.70 \]

    So 70% of emails were classified correctly.

    Precision — of everything we called spam, how much really was spam:

    \[ \text{Precision} = \frac{TP}{TP+FP} = \frac{40}{40+10} = \frac{40}{50} = 0.80 \]

    When this filter says "spam," it's right 80% of the time.

    Recall — of all the real spam, how much we caught:

    \[ \text{Recall} = \frac{TP}{TP+FN} = \frac{40}{40+20} = \frac{40}{60} = 0.6\overline{6} \approx 0.667 \]

    We caught about 67% of the spam; the other 33% slipped into inboxes.

    F1 — harmonic mean of the two. First the numerator and denominator:

    \[ F_1 = 2 \cdot \frac{0.80 \times 0.667}{0.80 + 0.667} = 2 \cdot \frac{0.5336}{1.467} = 2 \cdot 0.3637 \approx 0.727 \]

    So accuracy 0.70, precision 0.80, recall 0.667, F1 0.727. Notice how F1 sits between precision and recall, pulled toward the smaller one — exactly the "balance" behavior we wanted.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's reproduce the worked example with plain Python — just the four counts and arithmetic — then build k-fold split indices by hand so the mechanism is fully transparent.

    def classification_metrics(tp, fp, fn, tn):
    accuracy = (tp + tn) / (tp + fp + fn + tn)
    precision = tp / (tp + fp) if (tp + fp) > 0 else 0.0
    recall = tp / (tp + fn) if (tp + fn) > 0 else 0.0
    if precision + recall > 0:
        f1 = 2 * precision * recall / (precision + recall)
    else:
        f1 = 0.0
    return accuracy, precision, recall, f1

acc, prec, rec, f1 = classification_metrics(tp=40, fp=10, fn=20, tn=30)
print(f"accuracy={acc:.3f} precision={prec:.3f} recall={rec:.3f} f1={f1:.3f}")

# K-fold split indices by hand (no libraries)
def kfold_indices(n_samples, k):
    indices = list(range(n_samples))
    fold_size = n_samples // k
    folds = []
    start = 0
    for i in range(k):
        # last fold absorbs any remainder
        end = start + fold_size if i < k - 1 else n_samples
        test_idx = indices[start:end]
        train_idx = indices[:start] + indices[end:]
        folds.append((train_idx, test_idx))
        start = end
    return folds

for fold_num, (train_idx, test_idx) in enumerate(kfold_indices(10, k=5), 1):
    print(f"Fold {fold_num}: test={test_idx} train={train_idx}")

    Running this prints accuracy=0.700 precision=0.800 recall=0.667 f1=0.727 — matching our hand calculation exactly. The k-fold loop prints five rows; fold 1 tests on indices [0, 1] and trains on [2..9], fold 2 tests on [2, 3], and so on, so every index appears in a test set exactly once. (In practice you'd shuffle the indices first so the folds aren't biased by row order.)

    In code: the library way

    scikit-learn gives you all of this in a few lines. We'll cross-validate a model, print a full confusion matrix and report, and compare Ridge vs Lasso regularization strengths.

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.datasets import make_classification, make_regression
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression, Ridge, Lasso
from sklearn.model_selection import cross_val_score, train_test_split
from sklearn.metrics import confusion_matrix, classification_report

# --- classification: cross-validation + confusion matrix ---
X, y = make_classification(n_samples=400, n_features=8, weights=[0.7, 0.3],
                           random_state=0)
clf = LogisticRegression(max_iter=1000)

scores = cross_val_score(clf, X, y, cv=5, scoring="f1")
print(f"5-fold F1: {scores}")
print(f"mean={scores.mean():.3f}  std={scores.std():.3f}")

X_tr, X_te, y_tr, y_te = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)
clf.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
pred = clf.predict(X_te)
print("Confusion matrix [[TN FP] [FN TP]]:")
print(confusion_matrix(y_te, pred))
print(classification_report(y_te, pred, digits=3))

# --- regression: Ridge vs Lasso as lambda (alpha) grows ---
Xr, yr = make_regression(n_samples=200, n_features=20, n_informative=5,
                         noise=10.0, random_state=0)
for alpha in [0.1, 1.0, 10.0]:
    ridge = Ridge(alpha=alpha).fit(Xr, yr)
    lasso = Lasso(alpha=alpha).fit(Xr, yr)
    n_zero = int(np.sum(np.abs(lasso.coef_) < 1e-8))
    print(f"alpha={alpha:>4}: ridge||w||={np.linalg.norm(ridge.coef_):.1f}  "
          f"lasso zeros={n_zero}/20")

    Note scikit-learn calls the regularization strength alpha (our \( \lambda \)). The cross-validation block prints five F1 scores plus their mean and standard deviation — a much more trustworthy estimate than any single split. As alpha grows, Ridge's total weight magnitude shrinks, and Lasso zeroes out more of the 20 features (it should keep roughly the 5 truly informative ones), demonstrating L1's automatic feature selection.

    • "Generate 5 confusion matrices with different TP/FP/FN/TN values and have me compute accuracy, precision, recall, and F1 by hand, then check my answers."
    • "Run the scikit-learn code from this module and show me what happens to the Lasso zero-count and Ridge weight norm if I change alpha to 0.01 and to 100."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • Tuning on the test set. If you keep tweaking your model and re-checking the test score until it looks good, you've secretly fit to the test set — your final number is now optimistic. Fix: tune using cross-validation on the training data, and touch the test set once, at the very end. Better still, keep a separate validation set for tuning.
    • The accuracy paradox on imbalanced data. With 99% negatives, "always predict negative" scores 99% accuracy while being useless. Fix: report precision, recall, F1, or AUC — not accuracy alone — whenever classes are imbalanced.
    • Confusing precision and recall. Precision is about the predictions you made ("when I say positive, am I right?"); recall is about the positives that exist ("did I find them all?"). They pull in opposite directions as you move the threshold.
    • Data leakage via scaling before the CV split. If you standardize (subtract the mean, divide by std) using the whole dataset and then cross-validate, statistics from the test folds leaked into training — your scores will be too rosy. Fix: put scaling inside a Pipeline so it's refit on each fold's training portion only. (More on this in Module 7 — Feature Engineering and Preprocessing.)
    • Thinking more data complexity always helps. Beyond the sweet spot, added flexibility raises variance and hurts test performance. Use a validation curve to find the right complexity.
    • Treating a 0.5 threshold as sacred. The default threshold is just a convention. If missing a positive is costly, lower it to raise recall; the ROC/AUC view helps you choose deliberately.

    Exercises

    1. A model on a test set produces TP = 90, FP = 30, FN = 10, TN = 870. Compute accuracy, precision, recall, and F1. Is accuracy a fair summary here? Why or why not?
      Show solution

      Accuracy = (90 + 870) / (90 + 30 + 10 + 870) = 960 / 1000 = 0.960. Precision = 90 / (90 + 30) = 90/120 = 0.75. Recall = 90 / (90 + 10) = 90/100 = 0.90. F1 = 2 · (0.75 · 0.90)/(0.75 + 0.90) = 2 · 0.675/1.65 ≈ 0.818. Accuracy looks great (0.96) but the data is imbalanced (only 100 positives out of 1000), so accuracy is inflated by the easy negatives. Precision/recall/F1 give a more honest picture.

    2. You have a dataset of 12 rows and want 4-fold cross-validation. List the test indices for each of the 4 folds (assume no shuffling), and state how many times each row is used for training.
      Show solution

      12 rows, k = 4, so each fold has 12/4 = 3 rows. Fold 1 test = [0,1,2], Fold 2 test = [3,4,5], Fold 3 test = [6,7,8], Fold 4 test = [9,10,11]. Each row is in the test set exactly once and therefore in the training set in the other 3 rounds (k − 1 = 3 times).

    3. For a hospital screening test, would you prioritize precision or recall, and why? What happens to each as you lower the decision threshold?
      Show solution

      Prioritize recall: a false negative means telling a sick patient they're healthy, which can be life-threatening, so you want to catch as many true cases as possible even at the cost of more false alarms (which can be ruled out by follow-up tests). Lowering the threshold predicts "positive" more readily: recall goes up (you catch more true positives) while precision goes down (more false positives sneak in).

    4. Two regression models predict house prices. Model A's errors (in $1000s) are [2, 2, 2, 2, 2]. Model B's errors are [0, 0, 0, 0, 10]. Compute MAE and RMSE for each. Which metric reveals Model B's big single mistake?
      Show solution

      Model A: MAE = (2+2+2+2+2)/5 = 2.0. RMSE = √((4·5)/5) = √4 = 2.0. Model B: MAE = (0+0+0+0+10)/5 = 2.0. RMSE = √((0+0+0+0+100)/5) = √20 ≈ 4.47. Both have the same MAE (2.0), but RMSE is much larger for Model B (4.47 vs 2.0) because squaring magnifies the single $10k error. RMSE is the metric that flags the big mistake.

    5. You train Ridge with \( \lambda = 0 \), then increase \( \lambda \) to a very large value. Describe what happens to (a) the weights, (b) the bias, and (c) the variance of the model. At \( \lambda \to \infty \), what does the model effectively predict?
      Show solution

      (a) The weights shrink toward zero as \( \lambda \) grows, because the penalty \( \lambda \sum w_j^2 \) increasingly outweighs the fit term. (b) Bias increases — the model becomes simpler and less able to follow the data's shape. (c) Variance decreases — small weights mean the model reacts less to noise in any particular training sample. As \( \lambda \to \infty \), all weights are forced to ~0, so the model ignores the features entirely and just predicts the constant mean of the training targets (maximal bias, near-zero variance).

    You've got this if you can…

    • Look at a train score of 0.99 and a test score of 0.71 and immediately name the problem (overfitting) and a fix (regularize / simplify / get more data).
    • Compute precision, recall, and F1 from a confusion matrix without looking up the formulas.
    • Explain to a friend why one train-test split can mislead you and how k-fold cross-validation helps.
    • Decide whether precision or recall matters more for a given real-world task and justify it.
    • Say what \( \lambda \) does in Ridge/Lasso and which of bias and variance each direction of \( \lambda \) affects.

    Curated resources

    • StatQuest (Josh Starmer) — his bias-variance, ROC/AUC, and cross-validation videos are the clearest beginner explanations anywhere.
    • Google ML Crash Course — concise, interactive sections on classification thresholds, precision/recall, and ROC.
    • scikit-learn User Guide — the authoritative reference for cross-validation, metrics, and Ridge/Lasso, with runnable examples.
    • An Introduction to Statistical Learning (free PDF) — Chapters 2 and 5-6 cover bias-variance, resampling, and regularization rigorously yet accessibly.
    Module 7

    Feature Engineering and Preprocessing

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Scale numbers so a model treats them fairly — using standardization (z-scores) and min-max normalization — and explain exactly why this speeds up gradient descent.
    • Turn raw columns into more useful ones with numerical transforms, polynomial features, and interaction features.
    • Convert text categories into numbers correctly with one-hot and ordinal encoding, and recognize when target encoding causes data leakage.
    • Handle missing values with imputation and deal sensibly with outliers.
    • Engineer real features on a messy dataset (the Titanic passenger list) by hand and with scikit-learn.
    • Build a leak-free scikit-learn Pipeline with a ColumnTransformer that fits on training data only.

    Why should I care?

    Imagine you're predicting whether a loan will be repaid. Your spreadsheet has an income column in dollars (values like 45000) and an age column in years (values like 27). To a learning algorithm that only sees raw numbers, income looks roughly 1,600 times "bigger" than age — so it will obsess over income and barely notice age, purely because of the units someone happened to type in. Worse, the table has blanks where applicants skipped a field, a "home ownership" column full of words like "rent" and "own" that no equation can multiply, and a few suspicious entries claiming an income of 99,999,999. None of the beautiful models from Modules 3–6 can touch this data as-is. Feature engineering is the unglamorous, high-leverage work of turning a raw, messy table into clean numbers that let a model actually learn. Practitioners routinely find that better features beat a fancier algorithm — and this is the module where you learn to make them.

    Intuition first

    A model never sees your data the way you do. It sees a grid of numbers and tries to find weights to multiply them by. "Feature engineering" is everything you do to that grid before the model touches it, so that the patterns you care about become easy to find. There are four jobs in this module, and they all serve that one goal.

    Job 1: Put numbers on a common scale. Suppose two features are "age in years" (around 20–60) and "salary in dollars" (around 30,000–100,000). In Module 4 you learned that gradient descent walks downhill on a cost surface. When one feature is thousands of times larger than another, that surface becomes a long, skinny valley instead of a round bowl. Gradient descent then zig-zags down the narrow valley, taking tiny careful steps — slow and frustrating. Rescale both features to a comparable range and the valley becomes a nice round bowl: gradient descent walks straight to the bottom.

    The recipe analogy. Picture a recipe written half in grams and half in truckloads. "Add 200 grams of flour and 0.0001 truckloads of salt." Technically correct, but your brain (and a model) struggles to weigh ingredients measured in wildly different units. Convert everything to grams first and suddenly the relative amounts are obvious. Scaling does exactly this: it rewrites every feature in comparable "units" so the model can judge their importance on the merits, not on the accident of measurement.

    Left: with unscaled features the contours are stretched, so gradient descent (green) zig-zags. Right: after scaling the contours are circular and the path is a straight shot to the minimum.

    Job 2: Create features the model can't invent on its own. A linear model can only add up weighted inputs; it cannot, by itself, multiply two columns together or square one. If the true pattern is "risk is high only when someone is both young and has high debt," you can hand the model that idea by building a new column equal to age × debt. That's an interaction feature. Squaring a column (a polynomial feature) lets a straight-line model bend into a curve. You're doing some of the model's thinking for it.

    Job 3: Turn words into numbers. A column like color with values "red", "blue", "green" means nothing to arithmetic. If you naively map them to 1, 2, 3 you've secretly told the model that green (3) is "three times" red (1) and that blue (2) sits exactly between them — a false ordering. One-hot encoding avoids this by giving each category its own yes/no column.

    Job 4: Patch holes and tame extremes. Real data has blanks (missing values) and freak values (outliers). You'll fill the blanks sensibly (imputation) and decide what to do about the freaks, because both can quietly wreck a model.

    One last framing that will pay off later: feature engineering is the classical machine-learning ancestor of prompt engineering. In both, you can't change the model's internal machinery much, so you change its input to make the right answer easy to produce. Crafting an age × debt column for linear regression and crafting a careful instruction for a large language model are the same instinct — shape the input so the model succeeds. You'll meet the LLM side in Module 14 — Transformers and LLMs.

    Making it precise

    Standardization (z-scores)

    Standardization rescales a feature so it has mean 0 and standard deviation 1. For a single feature with values \( x \), we compute

    \[ z = \frac{x - \mu}{\sigma} \]

    where \( x \) is one raw value, \( \mu \) (mu) is the mean of that feature (the average of all its values), and \( \sigma \) (sigma) is the standard deviation (a measure of how spread out the values are). In plain words: subtract the average to recenter the data around zero, then divide by the spread so the typical distance from zero becomes about 1. The result \( z \) is the "z-score" — how many standard deviations a value sits above (positive) or below (negative) the mean.

    The standard deviation itself is \( \sigma = \sqrt{\frac{1}{n}\sum_{i=1}^{n}(x_i-\mu)^2} \), where \( n \) is the number of values, \( x_i \) is the \( i \)-th value, and the big \( \sum \) (sigma sum) just means "add up over all values." Inside: take each value's distance from the mean, square it (so negatives don't cancel positives), average those squared distances, then take the square root to get back to the original units. This is the same population standard deviation that scikit-learn's StandardScaler uses.

    Min-max normalization

    Min-max squeezes every value into the range from 0 to 1:

    \[ x' = \frac{x - x_{\min}}{x_{\max} - x_{\min}} \]

    where \( x_{\min} \) and \( x_{\max} \) are the smallest and largest values of the feature. The smallest value maps to 0, the largest to 1, and everything else lands proportionally in between. Use min-max when you need a bounded range (some neural-network inputs, image pixels); use standardization when the data has outliers or you don't need a hard 0–1 box, because one extreme value can crush all the others toward 0 under min-max but only shifts the mean and spread under standardization.

    One-hot encoding

    For a categorical feature with \( k \) distinct categories, one-hot encoding replaces it with \( k \) new columns (one per category). Each row gets a 1 in the column for its category and 0 everywhere else. The category "red" with categories {red, blue, green} becomes the row \( (1, 0, 0) \). No false ordering is introduced because no category is numerically larger than another.

    The golden rule: fit on train only

    Every transform above has parameters it must learn from data: standardization needs \( \mu \) and \( \sigma \); min-max needs \( x_{\min} \) and \( x_{\max} \); imputation needs the fill value; one-hot needs the list of categories. You must compute these from the training set only, then apply the exact same numbers to the test set. If you compute \( \mu \) over the whole dataset, information from the test set leaks into training — exactly the data leakage you met in Module 2 — The ML Mindset, and your reported accuracy becomes a lie. We call this "fit on train, transform on both."

    Worked example — by hand

    Part A — standardize the list \( [10, 20, 30] \).

    Step 1, the mean: \( \mu = \frac{10 + 20 + 30}{3} = \frac{60}{3} = 20 \).

    Step 2, the squared distances from the mean: \( (10-20)^2 = 100 \), \( (20-20)^2 = 0 \), \( (30-20)^2 = 100 \).

    Step 3, the variance (average of those): \( \frac{100 + 0 + 100}{3} = \frac{200}{3} \approx 66.67 \).

    Step 4, the standard deviation: \( \sigma = \sqrt{66.67} \approx 8.165 \).

    Step 5, the z-scores \( z = \frac{x-20}{8.165} \):

    • \( z_{10} = \frac{10-20}{8.165} = \frac{-10}{8.165} \approx -1.22 \)
    • \( z_{20} = \frac{20-20}{8.165} = 0 \)
    • \( z_{30} = \frac{30-20}{8.165} = \frac{10}{8.165} \approx +1.22 \)

    So \( [10, 20, 30] \) becomes \( [-1.22,\ 0,\ 1.22] \). Notice the shape is preserved (evenly spaced, symmetric around the middle) but the units are gone — these are now "standard deviations from the mean."

    Part B — one-hot encode the colors \( [\text{red}, \text{blue}, \text{red}] \).

    The distinct categories are {blue, red} (two of them). We make one column per category, in alphabetical order, and place a 1 where the row matches:

    rowcoloris_blueis_red
    1red01
    2blue10
    3red01

    One word column became two numeric columns the model can multiply by weights. (If a third color "green" had appeared, you'd have an is_green column too — three columns for three categories.)

    In code: from scratch

    Let's implement standardization and one-hot encoding ourselves with NumPy and pandas, so nothing is hidden. We deliberately compute the mean and std on a "train" array and reuse them on a "test" array, to make the fit-on-train rule concrete.

    import numpy as np
import pandas as pd

# --- Standardization from scratch ---
train = np.array([10.0, 20.0, 30.0])
test  = np.array([25.0, 40.0])          # new, unseen values

mu = train.mean()                        # learned from TRAIN only
sigma = train.std()                      # population std (divides by n)
print("mu =", mu, " sigma =", round(sigma, 3))

train_z = (train - mu) / sigma           # transform train
test_z  = (test  - mu) / sigma           # transform test with SAME mu, sigma
print("train standardized:", np.round(train_z, 2))
print("test  standardized:", np.round(test_z, 2))

# --- One-hot encoding from scratch with pandas ---
colors = pd.Series(["red", "blue", "red"], name="color")
onehot = pd.get_dummies(colors, prefix="color").astype(int)
print(onehot)

    This prints mu = 20.0 sigma = 8.165, then train standardized: [-1.22 0. 1.22] — exactly our hand calculation. The test values become [ 0.61 2.45]: 25 is a little above the training mean, and 40 is far above it (about 2.45 training-standard-deviations up). Critically, we never recomputed the mean on the test set. The one-hot block prints two columns, color_blue and color_red, matching the worked example.

    In code: the library way

    scikit-learn gives every transform a .fit() method (learn the parameters) and a .transform() method (apply them). fit_transform does both at once and belongs on the training data; on test data you call transform only. Below we build a realistic end-to-end preprocessor with a ColumnTransformer (different handling per column) wrapped in a Pipeline (chain steps so they fit together and never leak).

    import pandas as pd
from sklearn.compose import ColumnTransformer
from sklearn.pipeline import Pipeline
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler, OneHotEncoder
from sklearn.impute import SimpleImputer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.linear_model import LogisticRegression

# A tiny messy dataset: some numbers, some categories, some blanks.
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "age":   [22, 38, None, 26, 35, 58, 40, None],
    "fare":  [7.25, 71.3, 7.92, 53.1, 8.05, 51.9, 21.0, 13.0],
    "city":  ["NYC", "LA", "NYC", None, "LA", "SF", "SF", "NYC"],
    "survived": [0, 1, 1, 1, 0, 0, 1, 0],
})
X = df.drop(columns="survived")
y = df["survived"]

# Split FIRST, so nothing learns from the test rows (Module 2 leakage rule).
X_train, X_test, y_train, y_test = train_test_split(
    X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

numeric = ["age", "fare"]
categorical = ["city"]

# Numbers: fill blanks with the (training) median, then standardize.
num_pipe = Pipeline([
    ("impute", SimpleImputer(strategy="median")),
    ("scale", StandardScaler()),
])
# Categories: fill blanks with the most frequent value, then one-hot.
# handle_unknown="ignore" makes unseen test categories all-zeros instead of crashing.
cat_pipe = Pipeline([
    ("impute", SimpleImputer(strategy="most_frequent")),
    ("onehot", OneHotEncoder(handle_unknown="ignore")),
])

pre = ColumnTransformer([
    ("num", num_pipe, numeric),
    ("cat", cat_pipe, categorical),
])

model = Pipeline([("pre", pre), ("clf", LogisticRegression())])
model.fit(X_train, y_train)               # fits imputers/scaler/encoder on TRAIN only
print("test accuracy:", model.score(X_test, y_test))

    Because everything lives inside one Pipeline, calling model.fit(X_train, y_train) learns the medians, the mean/std, and the category list from the training rows alone; model.score(X_test, y_test) then applies those frozen numbers to the test rows. There is no way to accidentally leak, which is the whole point of pipelines. (This toy set is tiny, so don't read into the exact accuracy — the structure is the lesson.)

    Hands-on: feature engineering on messy data (Titanic)

    The Titanic passenger list is the classic playground for this skill. Three engineered features reliably help predict survival, and each shows a different technique.

    import pandas as pd

# Pretend we've loaded the Titanic CSV into a DataFrame `df` with columns like:
# Name, Sex, Age, SibSp (siblings/spouses), Parch (parents/children), Fare.
df = pd.DataFrame({
    "Name": ["Braund, Mr. Owen", "Cumings, Mrs. John",
             "Heikkinen, Miss. Laina", "Allen, Master. William"],
    "SibSp": [1, 1, 0, 0],
    "Parch": [0, 0, 0, 1],
    "Fare":  [7.25, 71.28, 7.92, 21.07],
})

# 1) Title extracted from Name: "Mr", "Mrs", "Miss", "Master" carry age/sex/class signal.
df["Title"] = df["Name"].str.extract(r",\s*([^.]+)\.")
# Group rare titles so we don't one-hot a hundred near-empty columns.
common = ["Mr", "Mrs", "Miss", "Master"]
df["Title"] = df["Title"].where(df["Title"].isin(common), other="Rare")

# 2) Family size: traveling alone vs. in a group changes survival odds.
df["FamilySize"] = df["SibSp"] + df["Parch"] + 1   # +1 counts the passenger
df["IsAlone"] = (df["FamilySize"] == 1).astype(int)

# 3) Fare bins: turn a skewed continuous fare into ordered buckets.
df["FareBin"] = pd.qcut(df["Fare"], q=2, labels=["low", "high"])

print(df[["Title", "FamilySize", "IsAlone", "FareBin"]])

    You've created four new columns from raw text and counts: Title (a category extracted with a regular expression, then trimmed so rare titles collapse into "Rare"), FamilySize and IsAlone (interaction-style features combining two raw columns), and FareBin (a continuous number bucketed into ordered groups with pd.qcut, which splits at quantiles so each bin holds roughly equal counts). None of these existed in the raw data; each encodes a human hunch about what matters, handed to the model as a ready-made column. That is feature engineering.

    A note on encoding choices and leakage

    Ordinal encoding maps categories to ordered integers and is correct only when a real order exists — e.g. shirt size {S, M, L} → {0, 1, 2}. Use it for genuinely ordered categories; never for unordered ones like city or color.

    Target (mean) encoding replaces each category with the average target value for that category — e.g. replace "city = SF" with the survival rate of SF passengers. It can be powerful for high-cardinality columns (thousands of categories), but it is a leakage magnet: if you compute those averages using the row's own target, the model effectively peeks at the answer, and accuracy looks great until it collapses on new data. If you ever reach for it, compute the averages with cross-validation folds so a row is never encoded using its own label. As a beginner, prefer one-hot until you understand exactly why you'd switch.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Explain to me with a fresh analogy why feature scaling makes gradient descent converge faster, then show me a plot of the cost contours before and after scaling."
    • "Generate 5 practice problems on choosing between standardization, min-max, one-hot, and ordinal encoding for given columns, then check my answers and explain any I get wrong."
    • "Run the Titanic feature-engineering code on the real dataset from seaborn (seaborn.load_dataset('titanic')), then show me what happens to test accuracy if I scale features before the train/test split instead of inside a Pipeline."
    • "Pair with me to implement min-max normalization from scratch in NumPy with a fit-on-train, transform-on-both interface, then review my code for leakage bugs."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • Scaling before the split. If you call fit_transform on the whole dataset and then split, the test set's mean and spread leaked into the scaler. Always split first, fit the scaler on train, transform both. A Pipeline enforces this automatically.
    • One-hot dummy explosion. A column with thousands of categories (like zip code) becomes thousands of columns, most of them almost all zeros — slow, memory-hungry, and prone to overfitting. Group rare categories into "Other," or use target encoding with proper cross-validation.
    • Unseen categories at test time. If "SF" appears only in the test set, a naive encoder crashes or invents a column the model never trained on. Set handle_unknown="ignore" in OneHotEncoder so unknown values become all-zeros.
    • Scaling tree-based models unnecessarily. Decision trees and random forests (Module 8 — Classical Algorithms) split on thresholds like "age > 30," which are unaffected by rescaling. Scaling them wastes effort and can confuse you about what helped. Scale for linear models, logistic regression, neural nets, and distance-based methods; skip it for trees.
    • Ordinal-encoding unordered categories. Mapping {red, blue, green} → {0, 1, 2} secretly tells the model green is "bigger" than red. Use one-hot for unordered categories; reserve ordinal for true orders.
    • Imputing with the test set's statistics. Filling missing values with the median computed over all data leaks test information. Learn the median on train, apply it to both — again, what a Pipeline guarantees.

    Exercises

    1. Standardize the list \( [2, 4, 6, 8] \) by hand. Give \( \mu \), \( \sigma \), and the four z-scores.
      Show solution

      Mean: \( \mu = (2+4+6+8)/4 = 20/4 = 5 \). Squared distances: \( (2-5)^2=9,\ (4-5)^2=1,\ (6-5)^2=1,\ (8-5)^2=9 \), summing to 20. Variance: \( 20/4 = 5 \). Std: \( \sigma=\sqrt{5}\approx 2.236 \). Z-scores: \( (2-5)/2.236\approx -1.34 \), \( (4-5)/2.236\approx -0.45 \), \( (6-5)/2.236\approx 0.45 \), \( (8-5)/2.236\approx 1.34 \). So \( [-1.34, -0.45, 0.45, 1.34] \).

    2. Apply min-max normalization to \( [10, 20, 30] \). Then say what min-max value the unseen test value 40 would get, using the train min and max.
      Show solution

      Train min = 10, max = 30, range = 20. \( (10-10)/20=0 \), \( (20-10)/20=0.5 \), \( (30-10)/20=1 \). So \( [0, 0.5, 1] \). The test value 40: \( (40-10)/20 = 30/20 = 1.5 \). Note it lands above 1 — min-max does not clip, and unseen values can fall outside [0, 1]. That's expected and fine; just don't assume the range is guaranteed for new data.

    3. One-hot encode the column \( [\text{cat}, \text{dog}, \text{cat}, \text{bird}] \). How many columns, and what does the "dog" row look like?
      Show solution

      Three distinct categories {bird, cat, dog} → three columns is_bird, is_cat, is_dog (alphabetical). The rows: cat → (0,1,0); dog → (0,0,1); cat → (0,1,0); bird → (1,0,0). The "dog" row is \( (0, 0, 1) \).

    4. You have features height_cm (range 150–200) and weight_kg (range 45–120), and you'll train logistic regression with gradient descent. Explain in two or three sentences what goes wrong without scaling and how standardization fixes it.
      Show solution

      Without scaling, weight spans about 75 units while height spans about 50, and more importantly both are large compared to a 0–1 target signal, so the cost surface is an elongated valley. Gradient descent zig-zags slowly across the narrow direction and needs a tiny learning rate to stay stable. Standardizing both to mean 0, std 1 makes their scales comparable, rounding the cost contours so gradient descent heads almost straight to the minimum and converges in far fewer steps.

    5. A teammate writes: scaler.fit_transform(X); X_train, X_test = split(X). Identify the bug, explain the consequence, and write the corrected order in words (or pseudocode is fine).
      Show solution

      The bug: the scaler is fit on the full dataset before splitting, so the test rows' mean and standard deviation leak into the scaling parameters applied to the training rows. The consequence is optimistic, unreliable test scores — the evaluation no longer simulates truly unseen data. Correct order: split first (X_train, X_test = split(X)), then scaler.fit(X_train) to learn \( \mu, \sigma \) from train only, then scaler.transform(X_train) and scaler.transform(X_test) with those same numbers. Best practice: put the scaler in a Pipeline so this can't be done wrong.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Standardize a small list by hand and explain what a z-score of +1.5 means.
    • Say when to use min-max vs. standardization, and one-hot vs. ordinal encoding.
    • Explain, with the cost-bowl picture, why scaling speeds up gradient descent.
    • Spot data leakage caused by fitting a transform before the train/test split, and fix it with a Pipeline.
    • Engineer at least three new features from a raw column (extract, combine, bin).
