    Module 8

    Classical Algorithms Toolbox

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain and hand-compute k-Nearest Neighbors (kNN), and implement it from scratch in NumPy.
    • Read a decision tree, compute Gini impurity and information gain to choose a split, and explain why deep trees overfit.
    • Describe how random forests use bagging and feature randomness to turn many weak trees into one strong model.
    • Explain gradient boosting (and where XGBoost fits) at a practical level, and contrast it with bagging.
    • State the max-margin intuition behind support vector machines and what the kernel trick buys you.
    • Apply naive Bayes, explain the generative vs discriminative distinction, and pick a sensible first algorithm for any tabular problem.

    Why should I care?

    You've spent Modules 3–7 mastering linear and logistic regression — straight lines and S-curves. They're wonderful, but they all share one assumption: that the relationship you're modeling is essentially a weighted sum of your features. The real world is full of patterns that aren't. "Approve this loan only if the applicant is young and has high debt and lives in a high-risk region" is a nest of if-then rules, not a line. "These two customers behave alike because they're near each other in feature space" is about distance, not slope. The algorithms in this module — neighbors, trees, forests, boosting, margins, and probabilities — each capture a different shape of pattern. And here's the punchline that working data scientists know in their bones: on ordinary tabular data (rows and columns in a spreadsheet — the bread and butter of business ML), a gradient-boosted tree like XGBoost still routinely beats a neural network. The fancy deep learning of Modules 10–14 rules images and text; this classical toolbox quietly wins the Kaggle competitions and powers the dashboards. Learn it well and you can solve most real business problems before lunch.

    Intuition first

    Every algorithm here answers the same question — "given this new example, what's my prediction?" — but each has a completely different theory of how the world works. Let's meet them as personalities before we touch any math.

    k-Nearest Neighbors: "you are who you hang out with"

    kNN is the laziest, most intuitive model imaginable. To classify a new point, it looks at the k closest already-labeled points and takes a vote. That's the entire idea. There is no "training" beyond memorizing the data. If you want to know whether a new fruit is an apple or an orange, you find the 3 most similar fruits you've already seen and go with the majority.

    The new-neighborhood analogy. You move to a new town and want to guess whether your street is quiet or lively. You don't run a regression — you just look at the five nearest houses. If four of them throw weekend parties, you bet "lively." kNN is exactly this: it makes no grand theory of the whole town, it just trusts the immediate neighbors. The choice of how many neighbors to consult (k) is the only real dial: ask too few (k=1) and one weird neighbor sways you; ask too many and you start averaging in houses across town that aren't really like yours.

    With k=3, the new point's three nearest neighbors are two green (class B) and one purple (class A). Majority vote: class B.

    Play with it: drag the white "?" point around the plane and watch its nearest neighbors (the ringed points) and its predicted class change live. Slide k from 1 up to 15 and notice how the shaded decision regions go from jagged and noisy to smooth.

    Decision trees: "a flowchart it builds for itself"

    A decision tree is a series of yes/no questions arranged like a flowchart, ending in a prediction. "Is age below 30? If yes, is debt above $5,000? If yes, predict 'high risk'." The clever part is that the tree learns which questions to ask, and in what order, from the data. At each step it searches every feature for the single split that best separates the classes, then repeats on each branch. It's how a doctor diagnoses (asking the most informative question first) turned into an algorithm.

    The 20-questions analogy. A good 20-questions player never asks "Is it a platypus?" first. They ask "Is it alive?" — a question that splits the universe roughly in half. Each answer carves away as much uncertainty as possible. A decision tree picks its questions by exactly this principle: choose the split that most reduces the "messiness" of the remaining group. We'll measure messiness with a number called Gini impurity.

    Random forests: "ask a crowd, not one expert"

    A single deep tree is a brilliant but erratic expert — it memorizes quirks of the training data and overfits. A random forest grows hundreds of trees, each on a slightly different random slice of the data and a random subset of features, then averages their votes. No single tree is great, but their mistakes are uncorrelated, so they cancel out. This is the wisdom of crowds, mechanized.

    Gradient boosting: "a team that fixes each other's mistakes"

    Boosting also builds many trees, but sequentially and with a twist: each new tree is trained specifically to correct the errors the previous trees made so far. The first tree makes a rough prediction; the second tree focuses on the examples the first got wrong; the third focuses on what's still wrong; and so on. It's relentless self-correction. XGBoost is the famous, fast implementation. On tabular data, this is usually the strongest single tool you own.

    Support vector machines: "find the widest safe road"

    An SVM draws a boundary between two classes, but not just any boundary — the one with the largest possible margin, the widest empty buffer between the boundary and the nearest points of each class. A wide buffer means a confident, robust decision. The "kernel trick" lets it draw curvy boundaries by secretly measuring similarity in a higher-dimensional space, without ever building that space.

    Naive Bayes: "multiply the clues together"

    Naive Bayes computes the probability of each class by combining the evidence from each feature, using Bayes' rule. It's "naive" because it pretends the features are independent (that knowing one tells you nothing about another) — usually false, yet often harmless. It's the classic spam filter: each suspicious word nudges the probability, and the nudges multiply.

    Making it precise

    kNN: distance and voting

    To find "nearest," we need a notion of distance. The default is Euclidean distance — ordinary straight-line distance. For two points \( \mathbf{a} \) and \( \mathbf{b} \), each a list of feature values, the distance is

    \[ d(\mathbf{a},\mathbf{b}) = \sqrt{\sum_{j=1}^{m}(a_j - b_j)^2} \]

    where \( m \) is the number of features, \( a_j \) is the \( j \)-th feature of point \( \mathbf{a} \), and \( b_j \) is the \( j \)-th feature of point \( \mathbf{b} \). In words: for each feature, take the difference, square it (so the sign doesn't matter), add up all those squares, and take the square root. This is the Pythagorean theorem generalized to many dimensions. To classify a new point, compute its distance to every training point, keep the \( k \) smallest, and let those \( k \) labels vote; the most common label wins. There is no equation to "fit" — kNN just stores the data, which is why it's called a lazy learner.

    Scaling is not optional for kNN. Because distance squares each feature's difference, a feature measured in big units (salary in dollars) dominates a feature in small units (age in years). Always standardize features first (Module 7 — Feature Engineering) or kNN measures distance almost entirely along whichever column happens to have the largest numbers.

    Decision trees: measuring impurity with Gini

    A tree needs a number that says "how mixed are the classes in this group?" The Gini impurity of a node is

    \[ G = 1 - \sum_{c=1}^{C} p_c^2 \]

    where \( C \) is the number of classes and \( p_c \) is the fraction of examples in the node that belong to class \( c \). In words: square the proportion of each class, add those up, and subtract from 1. A pure node (all one class) has one \( p_c = 1 \), so \( G = 1 - 1 = 0 \) — zero impurity, perfectly clean. A 50/50 two-class node has \( G = 1 - (0.5^2 + 0.5^2) = 0.5 \) — maximum mess. Gini ranges from 0 (pure) to a max of \( 1 - 1/C \).

    To evaluate a candidate split, we compute the weighted impurity after the split: the Gini of each child node, weighted by how many examples land in it.

    \[ G_{\text{split}} = \frac{n_L}{n}G_L + \frac{n_R}{n}G_R \]

    where \( n \) is the number of examples at the parent, \( n_L \) and \( n_R \) are the counts going left and right, and \( G_L, G_R \) are the children's Gini impurities. The information gain (here, the Gini reduction) is \( G_{\text{parent}} - G_{\text{split}} \). The tree tries every feature and every threshold, and picks the split with the largest gain — the question that cleans up the data the most. (Some trees use entropy instead of Gini; they behave almost identically, so we'll stick with Gini.)

    Random forests and bagging

    Bagging (bootstrap aggregating) means: build each tree on a bootstrap sample — a dataset of the same size drawn with replacement from the original, so some rows repeat and some are left out. Each tree therefore sees a slightly different world. A random forest adds one more trick: at each split, the tree may only consider a random subset of features (often \( \sqrt{m} \) of them). This stops every tree from leaning on the same one dominant feature, making the trees more different from one another. The final prediction is the majority vote (classification) or average (regression) across all trees. Why does averaging help? If each tree's error is partly random and the errors are roughly independent, averaging \( T \) trees shrinks the variance of that random error by roughly a factor of \( T \), while leaving the genuine signal intact.

    Gradient boosting

    Boosting builds the prediction as a running sum of small trees. Start with a constant guess \( F_0 \) (e.g. the overall class rate). Then for each round \( t \), fit a small tree \( h_t \) to the current errors (the gradient of the loss, hence "gradient" boosting) and update

    \[ F_t(\mathbf{x}) = F_{t-1}(\mathbf{x}) + \eta\, h_t(\mathbf{x}) \]

    where \( F_t \) is the model after \( t \) rounds, \( h_t \) is the new little tree, and \( \eta \) (eta) is the learning rate — a small number like 0.1 that controls how big a correction each tree is allowed to make. Small \( \eta \) plus many trees = careful, accurate learning. This is the same gradient-descent idea from Module 4 — Gradient Descent, except each "step downhill" is taken by adding a whole tree rather than nudging a weight. XGBoost, LightGBM, and CatBoost are optimized, regularized versions of this idea; they're the workhorses of tabular ML.

    Support vector machines: the max-margin idea

    For two classes, an SVM finds a boundary (a line in 2-D, a flat hyperplane in higher dimensions) defined by \( \mathbf{w}\cdot\mathbf{x} + b = 0 \), where \( \mathbf{w} \) is a weight vector giving the boundary's orientation, \( \mathbf{x} \) is an input point, the dot \( \cdot \) means multiply matching components and add, and \( b \) shifts the boundary. Among all boundaries that separate the classes, the SVM picks the one that maximizes the margin — the perpendicular distance to the closest points of either class. Those closest points are the support vectors; they alone determine the boundary. The kernel trick replaces the dot product \( \mathbf{x}_i\cdot\mathbf{x}_j \) with a kernel function \( K(\mathbf{x}_i,\mathbf{x}_j) \) that measures similarity as if the points had been mapped into a much higher-dimensional space — letting a straight margin in that hidden space appear as a curved boundary in the original one, all without ever computing the high-dimensional coordinates.

    The solid line is the decision boundary; the dashed lines mark the margin. The two circled points touching the margin are the support vectors — only they matter.

    Naive Bayes and generative vs discriminative

    Bayes' rule lets us flip a conditional probability. To classify, we want \( P(\text{class}\mid \text{features}) \) — the probability of a class given what we observed. Bayes' rule says it's proportional to \( P(\text{class}) \times P(\text{features}\mid \text{class}) \): the class's overall prevalence times how likely those features are if that class were true. The "naive" assumption is that the features are conditionally independent, so the joint feature likelihood factors into a product:

    \[ P(y\mid x_1,\dots,x_m) \;\propto\; P(y)\prod_{j=1}^{m} P(x_j\mid y) \]

    where \( y \) is the class, \( x_j \) is the \( j \)-th feature, \( P(y) \) is the prior class probability, and \( P(x_j\mid y) \) is how likely feature \( j \)'s value is within class \( y \). The \( \prod \) symbol means "multiply over all features." We compute this product for each class and pick the largest. Because it models \( P(\text{features}\mid \text{class}) \) — how the data is generated within each class — naive Bayes is a generative model. Logistic regression (Module 5) instead models the boundary \( P(\text{class}\mid \text{features}) \) directly; it's discriminative. Generative models can be trained from less data and can even invent new samples; discriminative models usually win on raw accuracy when data is plentiful. Most of deep learning is discriminative — except modern generative AI, which brings the generative idea roaring back at enormous scale (Module 14).

    Worked example — by hand

    Part 1: kNN with k=3. You have five labeled points with two features each, and you must classify a new point \( q = (3, 3) \).

    Pointx₁x₂Label
    P112A
    P223A
    P335B
    P454B
    P542B

    Compute the Euclidean distance from \( q=(3,3) \) to each point:

    • P1: \( \sqrt{(3-1)^2+(3-2)^2} = \sqrt{4+1} = \sqrt{5} \approx 2.24 \)
    • P2: \( \sqrt{(3-2)^2+(3-3)^2} = \sqrt{1+0} = \sqrt{1} = 1.00 \)
    • P3: \( \sqrt{(3-3)^2+(3-5)^2} = \sqrt{0+4} = \sqrt{4} = 2.00 \)
    • P4: \( \sqrt{(3-5)^2+(3-4)^2} = \sqrt{4+1} = \sqrt{5} \approx 2.24 \)
    • P5: \( \sqrt{(3-4)^2+(3-2)^2} = \sqrt{1+1} = \sqrt{2} \approx 1.41 \)

    Sort by distance: P2 (1.00), P5 (1.41), P3 (2.00), then P1 and P4 (2.24). The three nearest are P2 (A), P5 (B), P3 (B). Vote: A=1, B=2. Prediction: class B. Notice that with k=1 we'd have picked P2 and predicted A — the choice of k genuinely changes the answer.

    Part 2: Gini impurity of a node. A node holds 4 positives and 6 negatives (10 total). The class fractions are \( p_+ = 4/10 = 0.4 \) and \( p_- = 6/10 = 0.6 \). Then

    \[ G = 1 - (0.4^2 + 0.6^2) = 1 - (0.16 + 0.36) = 1 - 0.52 = 0.48 \]

    So this node has Gini impurity 0.48 — quite mixed (the max for two classes is 0.5).

    Part 3: Gini of a split. Suppose a candidate split sends 4 examples left (3 positive, 1 negative) and 6 right (1 positive, 5 negative). Left child: \( G_L = 1 - ((3/4)^2 + (1/4)^2) = 1 - (0.5625 + 0.0625) = 0.375 \). Right child: \( G_R = 1 - ((1/6)^2 + (5/6)^2) = 1 - (0.0278 + 0.6944) = 0.2778 \). Weighted impurity:

    \[ G_{\text{split}} = \tfrac{4}{10}(0.375) + \tfrac{6}{10}(0.2778) = 0.15 + 0.1667 = 0.3167 \]

    Information gain \( = 0.48 - 0.3167 = 0.1633 \). The split reduced impurity from 0.48 to about 0.317, a solid gain — the tree would happily make this split.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's build kNN classification in pure NumPy so nothing is hidden: compute all distances, take the k nearest, and majority-vote. We'll reproduce the worked example above to confirm we get class B.

    import numpy as np
from collections import Counter

def knn_predict(X_train, y_train, query, k=3):
    # Euclidean distance from the query to every training point.
    # (X_train - query) broadcasts the query across all rows.
    diffs = X_train - query                 # shape (n_points, n_features)
    dists = np.sqrt(np.sum(diffs**2, axis=1))   # one distance per point
    # Indices of the k smallest distances.
    nearest = np.argsort(dists)[:k]
    nearest_labels = y_train[nearest]
    # Majority vote: most_common returns [(label, count)], take the label.
    winner = Counter(nearest_labels).most_common(1)[0][0]
    return winner, dists, nearest

X_train = np.array([[1,2],[2,3],[3,5],[5,4],[4,2]], dtype=float)
y_train = np.array(["A","A","B","B","B"])
query = np.array([3,3], dtype=float)

pred, dists, nearest = knn_predict(X_train, y_train, query, k=3)
print("distances:", np.round(dists, 2))
print("3 nearest indices:", nearest, "-> labels:", y_train[nearest])
print("prediction:", pred)

    This prints distances [2.24 1. 2. 2.24 1.41], the three nearest indices [1 4 2] with labels ['A' 'B' 'B'], and prediction: B — exactly matching the by-hand result. The whole "training" was just storing X_train and y_train; all the work happens at prediction time.

    In code: the library way

    Now the real point of this module: once you understand each idea, scikit-learn lets you swap algorithms with one line. Here we train six classical models on the same dataset (breast-cancer diagnosis, built into scikit-learn) and compare test accuracy. Note the StandardScaler for kNN and SVM — both are distance-based and need scaled features.

    from sklearn.datasets import load_breast_cancer
from sklearn.model_selection import train_test_split
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.pipeline import make_pipeline
from sklearn.neighbors import KNeighborsClassifier
from sklearn.tree import DecisionTreeClassifier
from sklearn.ensemble import RandomForestClassifier, GradientBoostingClassifier
from sklearn.svm import SVC
from sklearn.naive_bayes import GaussianNB

X, y = load_breast_cancer(return_X_y=True)
X_tr, X_te, y_tr, y_te = train_test_split(X, y, test_size=0.25, random_state=0)

models = {
    "kNN (k=5)":        make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), KNeighborsClassifier(n_neighbors=5)),
    "Decision Tree":    DecisionTreeClassifier(max_depth=4, random_state=0),
    "Random Forest":    RandomForestClassifier(n_estimators=200, random_state=0),
    "Gradient Boosting":GradientBoostingClassifier(random_state=0),
    "SVM (RBF)":        make_pipeline(StandardScaler(), SVC(kernel="rbf")),
    "Naive Bayes":      GaussianNB(),
}

for name, model in models.items():
    model.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
    acc = model.score(X_te, y_te)
    print(f"{name:18s} test accuracy = {acc:.3f}")

    The API is identical for every model: fit to learn, score to evaluate. On this dataset you'll see all six land in the mid-0.90s, with the tree ensembles (random forest, gradient boosting) and the scaled SVM typically at the top and the single decision tree slightly behind. That uniform fit/predict/score interface is scikit-learn's superpower — you can audition a whole toolbox in a loop.

    For production-grade gradient boosting, install and swap in XGBoost — same shape of code:

    # pip install xgboost
from xgboost import XGBClassifier
xgb = XGBClassifier(n_estimators=300, learning_rate=0.1, max_depth=3,
                    subsample=0.8, eval_metric="logloss", random_state=0)
xgb.fit(X_tr, y_tr)
print("XGBoost test accuracy =", round(xgb.score(X_te, y_te), 3))

    The key knobs are n_estimators (how many trees), learning_rate (how big each correction is — smaller needs more trees), and max_depth (how complex each tree is). These three control the bias-variance trade-off you met in Module 6 — Model Evaluation.

    Which algorithm when? A guide for tabular data

    A practical decision order for a new spreadsheet-style problem:

    • Start with a baseline: logistic/linear regression (Modules 3 and 5). Fast, interpretable, tells you if the problem is even learnable.
    • Want the best accuracy with little fuss? Reach for gradient boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM). It handles mixed feature scales, missing values, and nonlinear interactions, and usually wins on tabular data.
    • Want a robust, low-tuning ensemble? Random forest — great defaults, hard to break, gives feature importances for free.
    • Need a model a human can read aloud? A shallow decision tree — every prediction is an explainable chain of questions.
    • Small dataset, clear margins, well-scaled features? SVM can shine, especially with an RBF kernel.
    • Text/spam, very high-dimensional sparse counts, need it fast? Naive Bayes — a strong, cheap baseline.
    • Few features, smooth boundaries, and you want zero training time? kNN — but remember to scale, and beware large datasets (prediction is slow).

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Generate 5 practice problems where I compute Gini impurity for a node and for a candidate split, then check my arithmetic step by step."
    • "Run my from-scratch kNN code and show me what the prediction becomes if I change k from 3 to 1, and explain why it flips."
    • "Pair with me to extend my NumPy kNN to do distance-weighted voting (closer neighbors count more) and review my implementation for bugs."
    • "Take the six-model scikit-learn comparison and add 5-fold cross-validation with error bars, then tell me which differences are actually significant."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "kNN doesn't need preprocessing." It needs scaling more than almost any model. Unscaled features let the largest-unit column hijack the distance. Always standardize first.
    • "A deeper tree is a better tree." A tree grown until every leaf is pure has memorized the training set and will overfit badly. Limit depth (max_depth), require a minimum number of samples per leaf, or prune.
    • "More trees in a forest always helps." Adding trees reduces variance with diminishing returns and never reduces bias. Past a point (often a few hundred) you're just spending compute. More trees also won't fix a forest of overfit-prone, identical features.
    • "Boosting and bagging are the same." Bagging (random forest) builds independent trees in parallel and averages — it reduces variance. Boosting builds dependent trees in sequence, each fixing the last — it reduces bias and is more prone to overfitting if you over-train, so watch the learning rate and tree count.
    • "SVM works out of the box." SVMs are very sensitive to feature scaling and to the kernel and regularization parameters (C, gamma). Scale your features and tune those hyperparameters, or expect mediocre results.
    • "Naive Bayes is wrong because features aren't independent." The independence assumption is usually false, yet the model often classifies well anyway, because it only needs the ranking of class probabilities to be right, not their exact values. Don't trust its probability numbers, but do trust it as a fast baseline.

    Exercises

    1. Using the worked-example data, classify the query point \( q=(3,3) \) with k=1 and with k=5. What does each predict?
      Show solutionThe sorted distances were P2 (1.00, A), P5 (1.41, B), P3 (2.00, B), P1 (2.24, A), P4 (2.24, B). With k=1 the nearest is P2, so the prediction is A. With k=5 we use all points: labels are A, B, B, A, B → A=2, B=3 → prediction B. This shows how k smooths the decision: tiny k is jumpy, larger k follows the overall majority.
    2. Compute the Gini impurity of a node with 9 positives and 1 negative. Is it more or less pure than the 4-positive/6-negative node (Gini 0.48)?
      Show solution\( p_+ = 0.9,\ p_- = 0.1 \). \( G = 1 - (0.9^2 + 0.1^2) = 1 - (0.81 + 0.01) = 1 - 0.82 = 0.18 \). At 0.18 this node is much purer than the 0.48 node — most examples agree, so there's far less uncertainty to resolve.
    3. A candidate split sends 5 examples left (5 positive, 0 negative) and 5 right (0 positive, 5 negative), from a parent of 5 positives and 5 negatives. Compute the parent Gini, the weighted split Gini, and the information gain. What does the result tell you?
      Show solutionParent: \( G = 1 - (0.5^2+0.5^2) = 0.5 \). Left child is pure (all positive): \( G_L = 0 \). Right child is pure (all negative): \( G_R = 0 \). Weighted split Gini \( = \tfrac{5}{10}(0) + \tfrac{5}{10}(0) = 0 \). Information gain \( = 0.5 - 0 = 0.5 \). This is a perfect split — it fully separates the classes, achieving the maximum possible gain for a 50/50 parent.
    4. Explain, in two or three sentences each, why a random forest is harder to overfit than a single decision tree, and why gradient boosting can overfit if you train too long.
      Show solutionRandom forest: each tree sees a different bootstrap sample and a random subset of features, so the trees make different, largely uncorrelated mistakes. Averaging their votes cancels much of that random error (variance), and because trees are built independently, adding more never increases overfitting. Gradient boosting: each new tree deliberately fits the residual errors of the current model, so the ensemble keeps chasing the training data ever more closely. Train for too many rounds (or with too high a learning rate) and it starts modeling noise — classic overfitting. The fix is early stopping, a small learning rate, and shallow trees.
    5. You have a 5-million-row dataset with 80 mixed numeric and categorical features, lots of missing values, and you need strong accuracy without much hand-tuning. Which algorithm from this module do you reach for first, and which would you specifically avoid? Justify both.
      Show solutionReach for gradient boosting (XGBoost/LightGBM). It handles mixed feature scales without scaling, has built-in handling for missing values, captures nonlinear feature interactions automatically, and gives top-tier tabular accuracy with modest tuning. Avoid kNN. With 5 million rows, kNN must compute distances to (potentially) every stored point at prediction time, making inference painfully slow; it also degrades in high dimensions and would require careful scaling and encoding of the categoricals first. (A single decision tree would be interpretable but less accurate; naive Bayes is fast but its independence assumption is shaky across 80 correlated features.)

    Module 9

    Unsupervised Learning

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain what unsupervised learning is and how it differs from the supervised learning of Modules 3–8 (there are no labels to predict).
    • Run the k-means clustering loop in your head — assign every point to its nearest centroid, then move each centroid to the average of its points — and implement it from scratch in NumPy.
    • Choose a sensible number of clusters k with the elbow method using the within-cluster sum of squares (inertia).
    • Describe anomaly detection and decide when it beats a supervised classifier.
    • Explain PCA in plain words — it finds the directions of maximum variance — and read explained_variance_ratio_ from scikit-learn.
    • Connect all of this to embeddings: learned low-dimensional representations you'll meet again in Modules 13–14.

    Why should I care?

    A music streaming service has 100 million listeners and no idea who they "really" are — nobody labelled them. Yet to build a useful "Discover Weekly" playlist, the company wants to group listeners who behave alike: the late-night lo-fi crowd, the gym EDM crowd, the parents-of-toddlers nursery-rhyme crowd. Nobody handed the algorithm those groups; it has to find them in the raw listening data. That's clustering. The same company also wants to flag a credit card charge that looks nothing like your normal spending — without ever having seen that exact fraud before. That's anomaly detection. And to even store each listener's taste, it compresses thousands of "did they play this song" columns down to a handful of meaningful numbers — a learned embedding. Every one of these tasks works without labels. That is unsupervised learning, and it's how you make sense of the 99% of real-world data that nobody has ever annotated.

    Intuition first

    Everything you've done so far has been supervised: each training row came with a correct answer — a house price (Module 3), a spam/not-spam label (Module 5). The model's job was to copy that mapping from inputs to known outputs. Unsupervised learning throws away the answer column. You hand the algorithm only the inputs and ask it to find structure on its own: which rows resemble each other, which rows are weird, and which directions in the data actually carry information.

    There are three big questions in this module, and each has a classic tool:

    • "Which rows group together?" → clustering (we use k-means).
    • "Which rows are abnormal?" → anomaly detection.
    • "Can I describe each row with fewer numbers?" → dimensionality reduction (we use PCA), which leads to the idea of embeddings.

    Clustering: the assign-then-update dance

    Imagine a scatter of points on a table and you want to split them into, say, 3 groups. K-means does something almost embarrassingly simple, and it works. It keeps 3 "team flags" (called centroids) and repeats two steps until nothing moves:

    1. Assign. Every point joins the nearest flag's team. (Closest centroid wins.)
    2. Update. Each flag walks to the centre — the average position — of its current team.

    Then you re-assign (some points are now closer to a different, moved flag), re-average, and so on. Each round, points and flags settle a little more, until a round changes nothing. That's convergence.

    The party analogy. Three friends — Ana, Ben, Cleo — host a party and stand in different corners of a big room. Every guest walks to the nearest host (assign step). Now each host looks at their little crowd and shuffles to stand right in the middle of it (update step). Because a host moved, a few guests near the boundary realise another host is now closer, and they switch crowds. The hosts re-centre again. After a few rounds, everyone has settled into three stable clusters and nobody wants to move. The hosts' final positions are the centroids; the crowds are the clusters. K-means is exactly this dance, done with arithmetic instead of feet.

    Each colour is a cluster; the orange star is its centroid — the average location of its members.

    Play with it: press Step to watch one phase at a time — first points recolour to their nearest centroid (✕), then each centroid glides to the mean of its members. Run repeats until a round changes nothing (convergence). Try different k, and hit Reset / new centroids a few times to see how the starting positions can lead to better or worse clusterings.

    Anomaly detection: learn "normal", flag the rest

    Sometimes you don't want groups — you want the one weird row. Anomaly detection builds a picture of what "normal" looks like (most of your data) and then flags points that don't fit. The crucial reason this is often unsupervised: with fraud, machine faults, or network intrusions, you have mountains of normal examples and almost no examples of the rare bad thing — and tomorrow's fraud won't look like yesterday's. A supervised classifier (Module 5) needs many labelled examples of both classes; anomaly detection only needs to know what normal looks like, so it can catch new kinds of weirdness it has never seen.

    Dimensionality reduction: fewer numbers, same story

    A dataset with 50 columns lives in 50 dimensions — impossible to picture and slow to compute on. But the columns are usually redundant: height in centimetres and height in inches carry the same information twice. PCA asks: along which direction do the points spread out the most? That direction (the first principal component) captures the most "story". The next direction, at right angles, captures the most of what's left, and so on. Keep the first two or three and you can often plot 50-dimensional data on a flat page while losing very little.

    Making it precise

    K-means, exactly

    You have \( n \) data points \( x_1, x_2, \dots, x_n \) (each \( x_i \) is a point, possibly with several coordinates). You pick \( k \), the number of clusters. K-means keeps \( k \) centroids \( \mu_1, \dots, \mu_k \) (the symbol \( \mu \), "mu", is just a name for a centroid — itself a point in the same space). It also gives every point a label \( c_i \in \{1, \dots, k\} \) saying which cluster point \( i \) belongs to.

    Assign step. Each point goes to the nearest centroid:

    \[ c_i = \arg\min_{j \in \{1,\dots,k\}} \; \lVert x_i - \mu_j \rVert^2 \]

    In words: for point \( i \), try every centroid \( \mu_j \), measure how far the point is from it, and keep the index \( j \) of the closest one. The notation \( \lVert x_i - \mu_j \rVert^2 \) is the squared distance between the point and the centroid — subtract coordinate by coordinate, square, and add up. The \( \arg\min \) means "the argument (here, the cluster index \( j \)) that makes this smallest".

    Update step. Each centroid moves to the mean of its assigned points:

    \[ \mu_j = \frac{1}{|S_j|} \sum_{i \in S_j} x_i \]

    where \( S_j \) is the set of points currently labelled \( j \), and \( |S_j| \) is how many points are in it. So you add up all the points in cluster \( j \) and divide by the count — the ordinary average, done separately for each coordinate.

    K-means is secretly minimising one quantity, the within-cluster sum of squares, also called inertia:

    \[ J = \sum_{i=1}^{n} \lVert x_i - \mu_{c_i} \rVert^2 \]

    This reads: for every point, take the squared distance to its own centroid \( \mu_{c_i} \), and sum over all points. Lower \( J \) means tighter, more compact clusters. The assign step lowers \( J \) (each point picks its closest centroid); the update step lowers \( J \) too (the mean is the single point that minimises summed squared distance). Because both steps only ever decrease \( J \), the algorithm is guaranteed to stop — though possibly at a not-quite-best arrangement, which is why initialization matters.

    Choosing k with the elbow

    If you increase \( k \), inertia \( J \) always drops (more centroids can hug points more tightly; with \( k = n \), every point is its own centroid and \( J = 0 \)). So you can't just minimise \( J \). Instead you plot \( J \) against \( k \) and look for the elbow — the value of \( k \) after which adding more clusters barely helps. Before the elbow, each new cluster buys a big drop in \( J \); after it, you're just splitting already-tight groups.

    PCA, gently

    PCA finds the direction \( v \) (a unit-length arrow) along which the data, once centred, has the largest variance — variance being the average squared spread (you saw it in Module 1). Project every point onto \( v \) and you get one number per point; PCA chooses \( v \) so those numbers are as spread out as possible, because spread = information. The first principal component is that best direction; the second is the best direction perpendicular to the first; and so on. Scikit-learn reports explained_variance_ratio_: the fraction of the total variance each component captures. If the first two ratios add to 0.95, two numbers preserve 95% of the spread of the whole dataset. We won't grind through the linear algebra (eigenvectors of the covariance matrix) — the intuition "find the directions of maximum variance" is what you need, and you'll see the algebra properly if you take Module 1's resources further.

    PC1 (green) runs along the direction the cloud is widest; PC2 (red) is perpendicular and captures the leftover spread.

    Worked example — by hand

    Let's run one full k-means iteration on six points along a line (1D, so a point is just a number). The points are:

    \[ 1,\quad 2,\quad 4,\quad 7,\quad 8,\quad 12 \]

    We pick \( k = 2 \) and start with centroids \( \mu_1 = 2 \) and \( \mu_2 = 8 \). In 1D the distance is just the absolute difference \( |x - \mu| \).

    Step 1 — Assign. For each point, compare its distance to each centroid and pick the smaller:

    • Point 1: to \( \mu_1 \) is \( |1-2| = 1 \); to \( \mu_2 \) is \( |1-8| = 7 \). Nearer 2 → cluster 1.
    • Point 2: \( |2-2| = 0 \) vs \( |2-8| = 6 \). → cluster 1.
    • Point 4: \( |4-2| = 2 \) vs \( |4-8| = 4 \). → cluster 1.
    • Point 7: \( |7-2| = 5 \) vs \( |7-8| = 1 \). → cluster 2.
    • Point 8: \( |8-2| = 6 \) vs \( |8-8| = 0 \). → cluster 2.
    • Point 12: \( |12-2| = 10 \) vs \( |12-8| = 4 \). → cluster 2.

    So cluster 1 = {1, 2, 4} and cluster 2 = {7, 8, 12}.

    Step 2 — Update. Move each centroid to the mean of its members:

    \[ \mu_1 = \frac{1 + 2 + 4}{3} = \frac{7}{3} \approx 2.33, \qquad \mu_2 = \frac{7 + 8 + 12}{3} = \frac{27}{3} = 9 \]

    The new centroids are \( \mu_1 \approx 2.33 \) and \( \mu_2 = 9 \). That's one complete iteration.

    What happens next? Re-assign with the new centroids: every point's nearest centroid is unchanged (1, 2, 4 are still nearer 2.33; 7, 8, 12 still nearer 9), so the means won't move either. Nothing changes — the algorithm has converged after a single update. Let's also note the inertia at the end: \( (1-2.33)^2 + (2-2.33)^2 + (4-2.33)^2 + (7-9)^2 + (8-9)^2 + (12-9)^2 \approx 1.77 + 0.11 + 2.79 + 4 + 1 + 9 = 18.67 \). Smaller centroid moves in future rounds would only lower this further — but here we're already at the minimum for this assignment.

    In code: from scratch

    Here is k-means in plain NumPy: random initialization, the assign step (distances then argmin), the update step (mean of each cluster), repeated until the centroids stop moving. We test it on three clearly separated blobs.

    import numpy as np

def kmeans(X, k, n_iters=100, seed=0):
    rng = np.random.default_rng(seed)
    # 1) Initialize: pick k distinct data points as starting centroids.
    centroids = X[rng.choice(len(X), size=k, replace=False)]

    for _ in range(n_iters):
        # 2) ASSIGN: squared distance from every point to every centroid.
        # X[:, None, :] has shape (n, 1, d); centroids[None] has (1, k, d);
        # broadcasting gives an (n, k) table of distances.
        dists = np.linalg.norm(X[:, None, :] - centroids[None, :, :], axis=2)
        labels = dists.argmin(axis=1)            # nearest centroid per point

        # 3) UPDATE: each centroid becomes the mean of its assigned points.
        new_centroids = np.array([
            X[labels == j].mean(axis=0) if np.any(labels == j) else centroids[j]
            for j in range(k)
        ])

        # 4) STOP if nothing moved (converged).
        if np.allclose(new_centroids, centroids):
            break
        centroids = new_centroids

    inertia = ((X - centroids[labels]) ** 2).sum()  # within-cluster sum of squares
    return labels, centroids, inertia

# Make three round blobs centred at (0,0), (4,5), (5,0).
rng = np.random.default_rng(42)
X = np.vstack([
    rng.normal([0, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([4, 5], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([5, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
])

labels, centroids, inertia = kmeans(X, k=3, seed=1)
print("centroids:\n", np.round(centroids, 2))
print("inertia:", round(inertia, 1))
print("cluster sizes:", np.bincount(labels))

    Running this prints centroids very close to the three true centres — [[-0.02 -0.04], [4.01 4.98], [4.98 -0.06]] — an inertia of about 93.0, and cluster sizes: [50 50 50]: a perfect recovery of the three groups. Try changing seed=1 to seed=0 and you'll get a worse inertia of roughly 707 with sizes like [24 26 100] — two starting centroids landed in the same blob and never recovered. That single change is the whole "initialization matters" pitfall, live.

    In code: the library way

    scikit-learn gives you the same algorithm — but smarter (it uses k-means++ initialization and restarts several times to dodge bad starts), tested, and fast. It also exposes inertia_ so you can run an elbow plot, and PCA for dimensionality reduction. Remember to standardize before PCA (and usually before k-means too).

    import numpy as np
from sklearn.cluster import KMeans
from sklearn.preprocessing import StandardScaler
from sklearn.decomposition import PCA

rng = np.random.default_rng(42)
X = np.vstack([
    rng.normal([0, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([4, 5], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
    rng.normal([5, 0], 0.6, size=(50, 2)),
])

# --- Clustering with k-means ---
km = KMeans(n_clusters=3, n_init=10, random_state=0)
labels = km.fit_predict(X)
print("inertia_:", round(km.inertia_, 1))          # ~93.0
print("centroids:\n", np.round(km.cluster_centers_, 2))

# --- Elbow method: inertia for k = 1..6 ---
for k in range(1, 7):
    model = KMeans(n_clusters=k, n_init=10, random_state=0).fit(X)
    print(f"k={k}  inertia={model.inertia_:.0f}")

# --- PCA (standardize first!) ---
Xs = StandardScaler().fit_transform(X)
pca = PCA(n_components=2).fit(Xs)
print("explained_variance_ratio_:", np.round(pca.explained_variance_ratio_, 3))

    The k-means part reports the same inertia_ of about 93.0 we got from scratch — reassuring. The elbow loop prints inertias of roughly 1641, 720, 93, 80, 69, 59 for k = 1 to 6: a huge drop down to k = 3 and then a near-flat tail — a textbook elbow at k = 3, exactly the number of blobs we built. The PCA part prints explained_variance_ratio_: [0.657 0.343], meaning the first principal component already captures about 66% of the spread and the two together capture 100% (this data is genuinely 2D, so nothing is lost).

    Embeddings: a peek ahead

    PCA hands you a few numbers per row by finding directions of maximum variance — a hand-computed low-dimensional representation. An embedding is the same idea taken further: a low-dimensional vector that a model learns so that "similar things sit close together". A word embedding might place king, queen, and monarch near each other in, say, 300 numbers; a song embedding places similar songs nearby; a face embedding places photos of the same person nearby. The streaming service from our hook stores each listener as a short embedding, then clusters those — clustering on embeddings is a standard combo. The difference from PCA is that embeddings are learned from a task (predict the next word, tell faces apart) rather than computed from variance, so they capture meaning, not just spread. You'll build real learned embeddings in Module 13 — Sequences, NLP, and the Road to Attention and see them power Module 14 — Transformers and LLMs.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Explain the k-means assign-then-update loop to me with a brand-new analogy that isn't about a party, then quiz me on which step lowers inertia."
    • "Run my from-scratch k-means with seeds 0 through 5 and make a table of the final inertia for each — show me how often a bad initialization happens, and explain how k-means++ fixes it."
    • "Generate a 2D dataset of two long, thin, diagonal clusters and show me where plain k-means fails on it — then suggest what to do instead."
    • "Pair with me to add an elbow plot to the library code using matplotlib, and help me read where the elbow is."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "K-means works on any cluster shape." It assumes round, roughly equal-sized blobs, because it judges everything by distance to a single centre. It mangles long thin shapes, rings, or clusters of very different sizes. For those, look at DBSCAN or Gaussian mixtures.
    • Forgetting to scale. K-means uses raw distance, so a feature measured in the thousands (salary) drowns out one measured in tens (age) — the same fairness problem from Module 7. Standardize features first unless they already share units.
    • Trusting a single run. Random initialization can land two centroids in one blob and converge to a bad answer (you saw seed=0 do exactly this). Always use n_init > 1 (scikit-learn's default uses k-means++ and several restarts) and keep the lowest-inertia result.
    • Picking k by minimum inertia. Inertia always falls as k rises and hits zero when k equals the number of points — so "lowest inertia" wrongly says "use as many clusters as you have rows". Use the elbow, or a silhouette score, and your domain knowledge.
    • Treating PCA components as meaningful features. A principal component is a blend of all your original columns (e.g. "0.3 × height + 0.5 × weight − 0.2 × age"). It maximises variance, not interpretability, so don't expect "PC1 = income". It's great for visualization and speed, not for explaining to a stakeholder.
    • Running PCA on unstandardized data. PCA chases variance, so a column with large raw numbers dominates the components purely because of its units. Standardize before PCA so each feature gets a fair say.
    • Confusing clusters with truth. K-means will always return k clusters, even if the data has none. The groups are a hypothesis to investigate, not a fact.

    Exercises

    1. One k-means iteration by hand. Points (1D): 0, 1, 6, 10, 11. Start with centroids \( \mu_1 = 1 \) and \( \mu_2 = 10 \). Do one assign step and one update step. What are the new centroids?
      Show solution

      Assign: point 0 → |0−1|=1 vs |0−10|=10 → cluster 1. Point 1 → 0 vs 9 → cluster 1. Point 6 → |6−1|=5 vs |6−10|=4 → cluster 2. Point 10 → cluster 2. Point 11 → cluster 2. So cluster 1 = {0, 1}, cluster 2 = {6, 10, 11}. Update: \( \mu_1 = (0+1)/2 = 0.5 \); \( \mu_2 = (6+10+11)/3 = 27/3 = 9 \). New centroids: 0.5 and 9.

    2. Why does increasing k never increase inertia? And why does that make "choose k to minimise inertia" a bad rule?
      Show solution

      With more centroids, every point can be at least as close to its nearest centroid as before (an extra centroid can only help or be ignored), so the within-cluster sum of squared distances — inertia — can only stay the same or drop. In the extreme, with k equal to the number of points, each point is its own centroid and inertia is exactly 0. Because inertia is minimised by making k as large as possible, "minimise inertia" always says "use one cluster per point", which is useless. That's why we look for the elbow: the k after which extra clusters stop buying meaningful reductions.

    3. You cluster customers using two raw features: annual spend (range 500–50,000) and number of visits (range 1–40). Without scaling, which feature dominates the clustering, and what should you do?
      Show solution

      K-means measures distance, and spend ranges over tens of thousands while visits range over tens — so a difference of "1000 in spend" swamps a difference of "10 in visits". Distance, and therefore the clusters, are driven almost entirely by spend; visits barely matter. Fix it by standardizing both features (subtract the mean, divide by the standard deviation, as in Module 7) so each contributes on a comparable scale before running k-means.

    4. For a fraud-detection problem you have 2,000,000 normal transactions and only 30 known fraud cases, and fraudsters constantly invent new tricks. Argue whether a supervised classifier or anomaly detection is the better starting point.
      Show solution

      Anomaly detection is the stronger starting point. A supervised classifier needs many examples of both classes; with only 30 fraud cases it has almost nothing to learn the "fraud" pattern from, and it would tend to predict "normal" for everything to score high accuracy (the imbalance trap from Module 6). It would also be blind to genuinely new fraud styles, since it only learns the 30 it saw. Anomaly detection instead models the abundant "normal" behaviour and flags anything that deviates — so it can catch novel fraud it has never seen. Once you accumulate enough confirmed fraud labels, a supervised model (or a hybrid) becomes worth adding.

    5. PCA on a standardized 10-feature dataset gives explained_variance_ratio_ = [0.52, 0.23, 0.11, 0.06, 0.04, 0.02, 0.01, 0.005, 0.003, 0.002]. How many components keep at least 90% of the variance, and what does that tell you about the data?
      Show solution

      Add the ratios until you cross 0.90: 0.52, then 0.75 (two), then 0.86 (three), then 0.92 (four). So 4 components keep about 92% of the variance — just under with 3 (86%), comfortably over with 4. This tells you the 10 original features are highly redundant: four learned directions carry almost all the spread, so you can compress to 4 numbers per row (great for plotting in pairs, speeding up later models, or de-noising) while losing under 10% of the information.

    You've got this if you can…

    • State the two repeating steps of k-means and say which quantity (inertia) each one lowers.
    • Run one k-means iteration on a handful of 1D points without a computer.
    • Explain the elbow method and why you can't just minimise inertia over k.
    • Decide, for a given problem, whether to use clustering, anomaly detection, or a supervised model.
    • Say in one sentence what PCA does and why you must standardize first — and how an embedding differs from a PCA projection.
