    Module 12

    Convolutional Neural Networks for Vision

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain in plain words why a plain fully-connected network struggles on images, and count exactly how many weights it would need.
    • Describe what a convolution is — a small filter sliding over an image to detect a pattern — and compute one by hand.
    • Define filter/kernel, feature map, stride, padding, and pooling, and use the output-size formula to predict the shape of every layer.
    • Implement a single 2D convolution from scratch in NumPy, then build a small working CNN for MNIST digits in PyTorch.
    • Use transfer learning with a pretrained ResNet to get strong results from a small dataset — and explain when that beats training from scratch.
    • Recognize the classic architectures (LeNet, AlexNet, ResNet) and explain the residual connection idea.

    Why should I care?

    Open your phone's photo app and search "dog". It instantly finds every dog photo you ever took — across breeds, lighting, angles, and backgrounds it has never seen. Nobody wrote rules like "if there are four legs and a tail". The system learned what a dog looks like by being shown labelled examples, and the engine that made this practical is the convolutional neural network. The same idea reads handwritten zip codes, flags tumors in X-rays, and lets a self-driving car spot a stop sign. In this module you'll build the core machinery yourself — a convolution by hand, then a real CNN that reads handwritten digits — and then borrow a network trained on millions of images so you can classify your own photos with a few dozen examples.

    Intuition first

    You already met neural networks in Module 10 — Neural Network Fundamentals and learned to train them in Module 11. There, every neuron in a layer was connected to every input. That is called a fully-connected (or dense) layer. It works fine for small tabular data. For images it falls apart, for two reasons.

    Reason 1: the number of weights explodes. A modest colour photo is 224 pixels wide, 224 tall, with 3 colour channels (red, green, blue). That is 224 × 224 × 3 = 150,528 numbers per image. If the first dense layer had just 1,000 neurons, each connected to every input, that single layer would have 150,528 × 1,000 ≈ 150 million weights. One layer. That is too many parameters to store comfortably and far too many to learn from a normal-sized dataset without badly overfitting (recall overfitting from Module 6).

    Reason 2: a dense layer throws away the picture's geometry. To feed an image into a dense layer you must flatten it into one long list of numbers. The moment you do that, the network no longer knows that pixel #225 sits directly below pixel #1. Neighbouring pixels — which together form edges, corners, and textures — are scattered into unrelated slots. A dense net has to relearn that structure from scratch, and it has to learn a separate detector for a cat's ear in the top-left corner, another for the top-right, another for the middle, and so on. Nothing is shared.

    The rubber-stamp analogy. Imagine you're given a giant photo and a small transparent stamp with a tiny pattern printed on it — say, a short diagonal edge. You press the stamp onto the top-left of the photo and score how well the photo underneath matches the stamp's pattern. Then you slide the stamp one step right and score again. You keep sliding across and down until you've stamped the whole photo, writing each score into a grid. That grid of scores is a feature map: a map of where in the image this particular pattern appears. The stamp is the filter (or kernel). Because you reuse the same small stamp everywhere, you need only a handful of numbers to define it — not a separate detector per location. That single idea — one small reusable pattern detector, slid across the whole image — is the entire heart of a convolution.

    This solves both problems at once. The stamp is small (say 3×3 = 9 numbers), so we have very few weights. And because we slide the same stamp everywhere, a pattern is detected no matter where it appears — the network has learned the pattern once and applies it everywhere. This property is called translation equivariance: move the cat, and its activations move with it.

    A small filter is placed on the top-left of the input, scored, then slid step by step across the whole grid. Each score lands in one cell of the output feature map.

    Play with it: watch a real convolution happen below. The 3×3 filter slides across a 9×9 grayscale image; at each spot it multiplies the 9 overlapping pairs and sums them into one cell of the 7×7 feature map. Press Play, or Step one position at a time, and switch the kernel preset — notice how the vertical-edge and horizontal-edge filters light up exactly where brightness changes, while box blur smooths and identity just copies the centre pixel.

    Making it precise

    Let the input be a small grid of numbers (pixel brightnesses) called \( X \), and the filter be a smaller grid of numbers \( K \) (these numbers are the weights the network will learn). Place the filter on top of a patch of the input, multiply each filter number by the input number underneath it, and add all those products up into a single number. That number is one cell of the output feature map. Then move the filter and repeat.

    If the input patch and the filter are both \( F \times F \) (here \( F \) is the filter size, the side length of the square filter), the value written into the output at row \( i \), column \( j \) is

    \[ Y_{i,j} \;=\; \sum_{a=0}^{F-1}\;\sum_{b=0}^{F-1} \; X_{\,i+a,\; j+b}\; \cdot \; K_{a,b} \]

    In words: line the filter up starting at position \( (i,j) \), multiply each overlapping pair of numbers together, and sum them all into one output number. The two sums (over \( a \) and \( b \)) just walk across every cell of the filter. (Strictly, this operation is called cross-correlation; deep-learning libraries call it "convolution" and so will we — the only difference from textbook convolution is whether the filter is flipped first, and since the filter is learned it makes no practical difference.)

    Real networks add two more knobs and one more operation:

    • Stride \( S \) — how many pixels you slide the filter each step. \( S = 1 \) slides one pixel at a time (lots of overlap, big output). \( S = 2 \) jumps two pixels, halving the output size roughly.
    • Padding \( P \) — how many rings of zeros you add around the input border before convolving. Without padding the output shrinks every layer and edge pixels get visited less often. Adding zeros lets the filter centre sit over the corners too. Padding \( P = 0 \) is called valid convolution (no padding); padding chosen so the output matches the input size is called same convolution.
    • Pooling — after convolving, we often shrink the feature map by summarizing each little block. Max pooling with a 2×2 window keeps only the largest value in each 2×2 block, halving width and height. This makes the representation smaller, faster, and a bit robust to tiny shifts (a feature detected one pixel over still survives).

    The output-size formula tells you exactly how big a feature map will be after a convolution (or pooling) layer:

    \[ O \;=\; \left\lfloor \frac{W - F + 2P}{S} \right\rfloor + 1 \]

    where \( O \) is the output side length (in pixels), \( W \) is the input side length, \( F \) is the filter size, \( P \) is the padding added to each side, \( S \) is the stride, and \( \lfloor \cdot \rfloor \) means round down to the nearest whole number. The \( 2P \) is there because padding is added on both sides. Memorize this one formula — you will use it constantly to keep track of shapes, and getting a shape wrong is the #1 beginner bug.

    Quick check with the formula. A 28×28 image, a 3×3 filter, no padding, stride 1: \( O = \lfloor (28 - 3 + 0)/1 \rfloor + 1 = 25 + 1 = 26 \). Output is 26×26. Now add padding \( P = 1 \): \( O = \lfloor (28 - 3 + 2)/1 \rfloor + 1 = 27 + 1 = 28 \) — same size as the input, hence the name "same" padding.

    Channels. A colour image has 3 channels (R, G, B), so it is not really a flat grid but a stack of 3 grids. A filter then also has depth 3 — it has its own 3×3 grid for each input channel — and it sums across all channels to produce one output feature map. A convolutional layer has many such filters (say 32), producing 32 output feature maps stacked into a new 32-channel "image". Those 32 maps become the input channels for the next layer. So channel counts grow as spatial size shrinks: an image journeys from 3×224×224 toward something like 512×7×7 before a final dense layer makes the decision.

    PyTorch stores image batches in NCHW order: a 4-D array of shape (N, C, H, W) = (number of images in the batch, channels, height, width). Forgetting this and feeding, say, (H, W) or (N, H, W, C) is the second most common beginner bug. Keep NCHW taped to your monitor.

    Worked example — by hand

    Let's convolve a 3×3 input with a 2×2 filter, using valid padding (\( P=0 \)) and stride 1. By the formula the output is \( O = \lfloor (3 - 2 + 0)/1 \rfloor + 1 = 2 \), so a 2×2 output.

    Input \( X \) and filter \( K \):

    \[ X = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 2 & 3 \\ 0 & 1 & 2 \\ 3 & 1 & 0 \end{bmatrix} \qquad K = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 0 & -1 \end{bmatrix} \]

    This particular filter computes "(top-left value) minus (bottom-right value)", a crude diagonal-difference detector. There are four positions where a 2×2 window fits inside a 3×3 grid. For each, multiply overlapping numbers and add.

    Window 1 — top-left, rows 0–1, cols 0–1: \( \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 2 \\ 0 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \). Compute \( 1\cdot 1 + 2\cdot 0 + 0\cdot 0 + 1\cdot(-1) = 1 + 0 + 0 - 1 = \mathbf{0} \).

    Window 2 — top-right, rows 0–1, cols 1–2: \( \begin{bmatrix} 2 & 3 \\ 1 & 2 \end{bmatrix} \). Compute \( 2\cdot 1 + 3\cdot 0 + 1\cdot 0 + 2\cdot(-1) = 2 + 0 + 0 - 2 = \mathbf{0} \).

    Window 3 — bottom-left, rows 1–2, cols 0–1: \( \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 1 \\ 3 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \). Compute \( 0\cdot 1 + 1\cdot 0 + 3\cdot 0 + 1\cdot(-1) = 0 + 0 + 0 - 1 = \mathbf{-1} \).

    Window 4 — bottom-right, rows 1–2, cols 1–2: \( \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 2 \\ 1 & 0 \end{bmatrix} \). Compute \( 1\cdot 1 + 2\cdot 0 + 1\cdot 0 + 0\cdot(-1) = 1 + 0 + 0 - 0 = \mathbf{1} \).

    Assembling the four results in their grid positions gives the output feature map:

    \[ Y = \begin{bmatrix} 0 & 0 \\ -1 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \]

    Notice that 9 input numbers and 4 filter numbers produced a 2×2 summary, and that the same four filter weights were reused in all four windows. That reuse is exactly what saves us from the parameter explosion.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's turn the by-hand procedure into NumPy: slide the kernel over the matrix and compute each windowed sum. We'll reproduce the worked example exactly, then run the same edge-style filter on a tiny synthetic image so you can see a feature map appear.

    import numpy as np

def conv2d(X, K, stride=1, padding=0):
    """Valid/padded 2D cross-correlation of a single-channel image X with kernel K."""
    if padding > 0:
        X = np.pad(X, padding, mode="constant", constant_values=0)
    Hin, Win = X.shape
    F = K.shape[0]                       # assume square filter F x F
    Hout = (Hin - F) // stride + 1       # the output-size formula
    Wout = (Win - F) // stride + 1
    Y = np.zeros((Hout, Wout))
    for i in range(Hout):
        for j in range(Wout):
            r, c = i * stride, j * stride
            window = X[r:r+F, c:c+F]     # the patch under the filter
            Y[i, j] = np.sum(window * K) # multiply overlapping pairs, sum them
    return Y

# Reproduce the worked example
X = np.array([[1, 2, 3],
              [0, 1, 2],
              [3, 1, 0]])
K = np.array([[1, 0],
              [0, -1]])
print("Worked example output:\n", conv2d(X, K))

# A vertical-edge detector on a tiny image that is dark on the left, bright on the right
img = np.array([[0, 0, 9, 9],
                [0, 0, 9, 9],
                [0, 0, 9, 9],
                [0, 0, 9, 9]], dtype=float)
vedge = np.array([[ 1, -1],
                  [ 1, -1]])
print("Vertical-edge feature map:\n", conv2d(img, vedge))

    The first print reproduces our hand calculation exactly: [[0, 0], [-1, 1]]. The second filter, [[1,-1],[1,-1]], fires strongly only where brightness changes from left to right. The image is flat (0) on the left and flat (9) on the right, with one vertical boundary in the middle, so the feature map is near zero everywhere except the column straddling that boundary, where it reads about −18. The network sees a clear "there is a vertical edge here" signal — produced by just four weights. That is a convolution detecting a pattern.

    In code: the library way

    Doing this for real means many filters, many layers, ReLU nonlinearities (from Module 10), pooling, and gradient descent (from Module 4) to learn the filter numbers instead of hand-picking them. PyTorch gives us all of that. Here is a compact but complete CNN that learns to read MNIST handwritten digits (28×28 grayscale, ten classes 0–9).

    import torch
import torch.nn as nn
import torch.nn.functional as F
from torch.utils.data import DataLoader
from torchvision import datasets, transforms

# 1) Data. ToTensor() puts pixels in [0,1] and shapes them as (C,H,W)=(1,28,28).
#    Normalize subtracts the dataset mean and divides by its std (see Module 7).
tfm = transforms.Compose([
    transforms.ToTensor(),
    transforms.Normalize((0.1307,), (0.3081,)),   # MNIST mean/std
])
train_ds = datasets.MNIST(root="data", train=True,  download=True, transform=tfm)
test_ds  = datasets.MNIST(root="data", train=False, download=True, transform=tfm)
train_dl = DataLoader(train_ds, batch_size=64, shuffle=True)
test_dl  = DataLoader(test_ds,  batch_size=1000)

# 2) A small CNN. Track shapes with the output-size formula in comments.
class SmallCNN(nn.Module):
    def __init__(self):
        super().__init__()
        self.conv1 = nn.Conv2d(1, 32, kernel_size=3, padding=1)   # 1->32 ch, 28x28 stays 28x28
        self.conv2 = nn.Conv2d(32, 64, kernel_size=3, padding=1)  # 32->64 ch, 14x14 stays 14x14
        self.pool  = nn.MaxPool2d(2)                              # halves H and W
        self.fc1   = nn.Linear(64 * 7 * 7, 128)                  # flatten -> 128
        self.fc2   = nn.Linear(128, 10)                          # 128 -> 10 classes

    def forward(self, x):                  # x: (N,1,28,28)
        x = self.pool(F.relu(self.conv1(x)))   # -> (N,32,14,14)
        x = self.pool(F.relu(self.conv2(x)))   # -> (N,64,7,7)
        x = torch.flatten(x, 1)                # -> (N, 64*7*7)
        x = F.relu(self.fc1(x))                # -> (N,128)
        return self.fc2(x)                     # -> (N,10) raw scores (logits)

device = "cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu"
model  = SmallCNN().to(device)
opt    = torch.optim.Adam(model.parameters(), lr=1e-3)
loss_fn = nn.CrossEntropyLoss()            # combines softmax + log-loss (Module 5)

# 3) Train for one epoch (one pass over the data) to keep it quick.
model.train()
for images, labels in train_dl:
    images, labels = images.to(device), labels.to(device)
    opt.zero_grad()
    logits = model(images)
    loss = loss_fn(logits, labels)
    loss.backward()                        # backprop computes gradients (Module 11)
    opt.step()                             # gradient-descent update

# 4) Evaluate accuracy on the held-out test set.
model.eval()
correct = total = 0
with torch.no_grad():
    for images, labels in test_dl:
        images, labels = images.to(device), labels.to(device)
        pred = model(images).argmax(dim=1)
        correct += (pred == labels).sum().item()
        total   += labels.size(0)
print(f"Test accuracy: {correct/total:.4f}")

    After a single epoch this network typically reaches about 98% test accuracy — out of 10,000 test digits it misclassifies only a couple hundred. Train for a few more epochs and it passes 99%. Notice how the shapes flow: 1 channel becomes 32 then 64 (more pattern detectors deeper in), while the spatial size shrinks 28 → 14 → 7 via the two poolings. The flatten step turns the final 64×7×7 = 3,136 numbers into a vector that two dense layers map to 10 class scores. The convolutions did the heavy lifting of seeing; the dense layers just decide.

    Transfer learning: standing on a giant's shoulders

    MNIST is easy because we have 60,000 training images. What if you have 200 photos of two flower species? Training a big CNN from scratch on 200 images will overfit instantly. The fix is transfer learning: take a network already trained on millions of images (so its early filters already detect edges, textures, and shapes that are useful for any vision task), and reuse it. You freeze most of it and replace just the final classification layer with one sized for your classes, then train only that.

    import torch
import torch.nn as nn
from torchvision import models

# Load resnet18 with weights pretrained on ImageNet (1000 classes, millions of images).
weights = models.ResNet18_Weights.DEFAULT
net = models.resnet18(weights=weights)

# Freeze every existing parameter so training won't disturb the learned filters.
for p in net.parameters():
    p.requires_grad = False

# Replace the final fully-connected layer (1000 outputs) with our own (2 classes).
num_features = net.fc.in_features          # resnet18 -> 512
net.fc = nn.Linear(num_features, 2)        # new layer: its params DO require grad

# Only the new layer's parameters are trainable, so the optimizer sees just those.
opt = torch.optim.Adam(net.fc.parameters(), lr=1e-3)
loss_fn = nn.CrossEntropyLoss()

# IMPORTANT: preprocess YOUR images exactly the way the pretrained net expects.
preprocess = weights.transforms()   # resize to 224, center-crop, normalize with ImageNet stats

# Then a normal training loop over your small dataset, e.g.:
# for images, labels in your_loader:
#     opt.zero_grad()
#     loss = loss_fn(net(images), labels)
#     loss.backward(); opt.step()
print("Trainable params:", sum(p.numel() for p in net.parameters() if p.requires_grad))

    The print shows only ~1,026 trainable parameters (512 weights + bias times the new layer's structure) instead of ResNet's 11 million — yet on a small flower or pet dataset this routinely hits 90%+ accuracy in minutes. The pretrained filters supply the visual "common sense"; you only teach the network how to name your specific classes. The one non-negotiable: preprocess your images with the same resize and normalization the network was trained with (here, weights.transforms() does it for you). Feed it raw, differently-scaled pixels and accuracy collapses.

    A quick tour of classic architectures

    • LeNet-5 (1998, Yann LeCun). The original CNN, built to read handwritten digits on cheques. Two convolution-plus-pooling stages feeding small dense layers — essentially the structure of our SmallCNN above. It proved the convolution idea worked.
    • AlexNet (2012). A deeper, wider LeNet trained on GPUs that won the ImageNet competition by a huge margin and kicked off the deep-learning era. It popularized ReLU activations and dropout (both in Module 11) as standard ingredients.
    • ResNet (2015). When researchers stacked very deep networks (50, 100+ layers), accuracy mysteriously got worse — the gradient signal degraded over so many layers (the vanishing-gradient problem from Module 11). ResNet's fix is the residual connection (or skip connection): instead of asking a block of layers to compute a brand-new output \( H(x) \), you ask it to compute only a correction \( F(x) \) and add the input back: the block outputs \( F(x) + x \). If the best thing to do is nothing, the block can just learn \( F(x) = 0 \) and pass the input through unchanged. And because of that "+x", gradients have a clean shortcut path straight back through the network, so even 100+ layers train well. This one trick unlocked very deep networks and is everywhere today, including inside the Transformers of Module 14.

    A residual block: the layers learn a correction F(x), and the input x is added back via a skip connection, giving F(x)+x.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Run my from-scratch conv2d NumPy code, then show me what the output feature map looks like if I change the kernel to a horizontal-edge detector [[1,1],[-1,-1]] and explain why it differs."
    • "Generate 5 output-shape problems using the formula (W − F + 2P)/S + 1 with different W, F, P, S, and check my answers one by one."
    • "Pair with me to take the MNIST CNN and adapt it to the FashionMNIST dataset, then help me debug any tensor-shape errors I hit."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • Forgetting the channel dimension. A grayscale image is shape (1, 28, 28), not (28, 28). Conv2d always expects a channel axis even when there is only one channel. Add it with x.unsqueeze(0) or transforms.ToTensor(), which inserts it for you.
    • Wrong batch shape (NCHW). PyTorch wants (N, C, H, W). A single image must still be a 4-D batch of size 1: (1, 1, 28, 28). Passing (28, 28) or NHWC order throws a shape error or silently learns garbage. When in doubt, print(x.shape) before every layer.
    • Not normalizing images. Raw pixels (0–255) make training unstable; the loss can explode or stall. Always scale to roughly zero-mean, unit-variance — ToTensor() then Normalize(...). For transfer learning you must use the pretrained model's own mean and std, not your data's.
    • A giant dense layer right after flatten. If you flatten a large feature map straight into a wide dense layer you reintroduce the parameter explosion. Pool down to a small spatial size first (we went to 7×7), or use adaptive pooling, before the dense head.
    • Thinking more filters or layers always helps. Bigger models overfit small datasets (Module 6). On small data, transfer learning from a pretrained net almost always beats training a large CNN from scratch — and is faster.
    • Confusing kernel size with number of filters. kernel_size=3 sets the filter's 3×3 spatial extent; the out_channels argument sets how many different filters (how many feature maps) the layer produces. They are independent knobs.

    Exercises

    1. Use the output-size formula to find the output side length for an input of width \( W = 32 \), filter \( F = 5 \), padding \( P = 0 \), stride \( S = 1 \). Then redo it with \( P = 2 \).
      Show solution

      With \( P=0 \): \( O = \lfloor (32 - 5 + 0)/1 \rfloor + 1 = 27 + 1 = 28 \). With \( P=2 \): \( O = \lfloor (32 - 5 + 4)/1 \rfloor + 1 = 31 + 1 = 32 \). The padding of 2 on each side exactly preserves the input size for a 5×5 filter — that is the "same" padding for \( F=5 \) (in general \( P = (F-1)/2 \)).

    2. By hand, convolve \( X = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 2 & 3 \end{bmatrix} \) with the 2×2 filter \( K = \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 1 \\ 1 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \), valid padding, stride 1. What single number do you get, and what does this filter compute?
      Show solution

      The output is \( O = \lfloor(2-2)/1\rfloor + 1 = 1 \), a single 1×1 value. Multiply each pair and sum: \( 1\cdot1 + 0\cdot1 + 2\cdot1 + 3\cdot1 = 1 + 0 + 2 + 3 = 6 \). A filter of all ones simply sums the values under it; divide by 4 and it would be a 2×2 average (a blur). So this is a box/blur-style filter.

    3. A dense network's first layer connects every pixel of a 100×100 grayscale image to 500 neurons. How many weights is that (ignoring biases)? Compare to a single Conv2d layer with 500 filters of size 3×3 on the same one-channel image.
      Show solution

      Dense: \( 100 \times 100 = 10{,}000 \) inputs, each connected to 500 neurons, so \( 10{,}000 \times 500 = 5{,}000{,}000 \) weights. Conv: each filter is \( 3 \times 3 \times 1 = 9 \) weights, and there are 500 filters, so \( 9 \times 500 = 4{,}500 \) weights — over a thousand times fewer, and they're reused across every position. This is the parameter-sharing payoff in numbers.

    4. In the MNIST SmallCNN, trace the tensor shape after each layer for an input batch of 16 images. Where does the 64×7×7 in fc1 come from?
      Show solution

      Input: (16, 1, 28, 28). After conv1 (padding 1, 3×3): (16, 32, 28, 28). After first pool (2×2): (16, 32, 14, 14). After conv2: (16, 64, 14, 14). After second pool: (16, 64, 7, 7). Flatten keeps the batch axis and combines the rest: (16, 64·7·7) = (16, 3136). So fc1 must accept 3136 = 64×7×7 inputs. If you changed the padding, kernel sizes, or number of poolings, you'd recompute this with the output-size formula and update fc1 accordingly.

    5. You have 300 labelled images across 3 plant-disease classes and a deadline. Would you train a CNN from scratch or use transfer learning? Justify, and name the two preprocessing steps you must get right.
      Show solution

      Use transfer learning. 300 images is far too few to train a deep CNN from scratch without severe overfitting (Module 6), and a pretrained ResNet already knows generic visual features. Load a pretrained net, freeze the backbone, replace the final layer with a 3-class Linear, and train just that. The two preprocessing essentials: (1) resize/crop images to the input size the network expects (224×224 for ResNet18), and (2) normalize with the pretrained model's own mean and std (use weights.transforms()). Skip either and accuracy falls apart even though the model is excellent.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Explain in one breath why we use convolutions instead of dense layers on images (few shared weights, keeps spatial structure).
    • Compute a small 2D convolution by hand and predict any layer's output shape with \( (W - F + 2P)/S + 1 \).
    • State what stride, padding, pooling, channels, and feature maps each mean — and what NCHW stands for.
    • Read the MNIST CNN code and say what each layer does to the tensor shape.
    • Describe transfer learning and a residual connection, and say when transfer learning beats training from scratch.

    Module 13

    Sequences, NLP, and the Road to Attention

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain why text and other sequences break the "fixed grid of numbers" assumption every model so far has relied on — they have order and variable length.
    • Describe a recurrent network (RNN / LSTM) in plain words as a model that carries a hidden state forward, and say why remembering things from long ago is genuinely hard for it.
    • Explain what a word embedding is — words turned into vectors where similar meanings sit nearby — and compute cosine similarity between two vectors by hand and in code.
    • Explain tokenization and Byte-Pair Encoding (BPE): why models read sub-word chunks instead of whole words or single letters.
    • Compute a tiny self-attention step from scratch — build Q, K, V, score them, softmax the scores, and mix the values — and explain why attention fixes the RNN's memory problem.
    • See clearly how this module is the on-ramp to Module 14 — Transformers and LLMs, Conceptually.

    Why should I care?

    Read this sentence: "The trophy didn't fit in the suitcase because it was too big." What does "it" refer to — the trophy or the suitcase? You know instantly it's the trophy (a suitcase being too big wouldn't stop something fitting inside it). To get that right, a model must connect "it" all the way back to "trophy," seven words earlier, while ignoring the equally-close "suitcase." Every model you've built so far — linear regression, logistic regression, the dense and convolutional networks of Modules 10–12 — would choke on this, because they expect a fixed-size grid of numbers with no notion of order. Language is the opposite: it's an ordered stream of variable length where a word's meaning depends on words far away. This module is the story of how the field learned to handle that — first clumsily with recurrence, then beautifully with attention, the single idea that powers every modern translator, chatbot, and large language model. By the end you'll have computed an attention step with your own hands, and Module 14's transformers will feel like an assembly of parts you already understand.

    Intuition first

    Until now, every input was a tidy rectangle: a house had exactly 4 features (Module 3), an image had exactly 28×28 pixels (Module 12). The model could assume "feature 3 is always the bedroom count." Text refuses to cooperate in two ways. First, length varies: one review is 5 words, the next is 500. Second, order carries meaning: "dog bites man" and "man bites dog" use identical words but mean opposite things. A model that treats input as an unordered bag of features literally cannot tell those two sentences apart. So we need machinery built for ordered, variable-length streams.

    The first good idea was recurrence: read the sequence one item at a time, left to right, keeping a running summary in your head that you update at each step. That running summary is called the hidden state.

    Reading aloud with a sticky note. Imagine reading a sentence one word at a time, and after each word you scribble a quick summary of "the story so far" on a single sticky note, crossing out the old summary and writing a new one. You never look back at earlier words — only at your latest note. By the end, the note is your understanding of the whole sentence. That sticky note is the hidden state, and rewriting it at every word is exactly what a recurrent network does. The flaw is already visible: a single small note can only hold so much. By word 200, the gist of word 1 has been overwritten and re-summarized so many times that it's faded to almost nothing. That fading is the long-range memory problem, and it's the whole reason attention was invented.

    Here's the recurrent loop as a picture. The same little box is applied again and again; each copy takes the next word and the previous note, and produces an updated note.

    The same RNN cell, "unrolled" across three words. The green hidden state \( h \) (the sticky note) is the only path information can travel along — everything word 1 contributes must survive being rewritten at every later step.

    Before any of this works, though, we hit a more basic problem: a neural network only eats numbers, and "cat" is not a number. We need two things — a way to chop text into pieces (tokenization) and a way to turn each piece into a vector (embeddings). Let's build intuition for both, then return to fix the RNN's memory with attention.

    Words as vectors: embeddings

    The naive way to turn a word into numbers is one-hot encoding: give every word in your vocabulary its own slot, and represent a word as a vector that is 1 in its slot and 0 everywhere else. With a 50,000-word vocabulary, "cat" becomes a 50,000-long vector of zeros with a single 1. This is hopeless for two reasons: the vectors are gigantic, and — worse — every pair of distinct words is equally far apart. "cat" and "dog" are exactly as unrelated as "cat" and "democracy," because their 1s sit in different slots. The numbers carry zero meaning.

    An embedding fixes this. Instead of one slot per word, we represent each word as a short, dense vector — say 50 to 300 numbers, none of them necessarily zero — and we learn those numbers during training (with gradient descent, Module 4). The training pressure pushes words that appear in similar contexts to get similar vectors. The famous result: words land in a space where nearby means "similar in meaning." "cat" and "dog" end up close; "cat" and "democracy" end up far. Even directions become meaningful — the vector arithmetic king − man + woman ≈ queen roughly works, because the "royalty" and "gender" ideas got encoded along consistent directions.

    A map of meaning. Think of a giant map where every word is a pin. We don't decide where the pins go; training slides them around until words used in similar ways cluster together — a "kitchen" neighborhood (spoon, plate, oven), a "weather" neighborhood (rain, storm, cloudy), and so on. To ask "how related are two words?" you just measure how close their pins are. The twist versus a real map: it has dozens or hundreds of dimensions, not two, so a single pin can be near "fruit" along one axis and near "tech company" along another. That's how one vector for "apple" can sit between both worlds.

    How do we measure "closeness" between two vectors? Not raw distance — we usually care about direction, not length. The standard tool is cosine similarity: the cosine of the angle between two vectors. It's 1 when they point the same way, 0 when perpendicular (unrelated), and −1 when opposite. We'll make this precise in a moment and compute it by hand.

    Tokenization and Byte-Pair Encoding (BPE)

    Before a word can become a vector, we must decide what a "word" even is — that's tokenization, the step that splits raw text into the units a model processes (called tokens). Two obvious choices both fail:

    • Split on whole words. Now your vocabulary is enormous, and you choke on anything unseen: a new word, a typo ("teh"), a name, an emoji, or "antidisestablishmentarianism" all become a single unknown token, and the model learns nothing from them. Worse, "run," "running," and "runs" are three unrelated entries that share no structure.
    • Split into single characters. Now your vocabulary is tiny and nothing is ever "unknown" — but sequences become very long (each word is many tokens), and the model must rediscover from scratch that "c-a-t" forms a meaningful chunk. Long sequences are expensive (you'll see exactly why under attention).

    Byte-Pair Encoding (BPE) is the practical compromise that nearly every modern language model uses: tokens are sub-word chunks, learned from data. The recipe is delightfully simple. Start with every text broken into individual characters. Then repeat: find the most frequent adjacent pair of symbols and merge it into a new single symbol; record that merge. Do this a fixed number of times (e.g. 30,000 merges). Frequent pieces like "th", "ing", and common whole words like "the" get merged early and become single tokens; rare words get left as several smaller pieces.

    The payoff: common words are one token (efficient), rare or novel words gracefully fall back to known sub-word pieces (no "unknown" token ever needed), and related words share structure — "running" might tokenize as run + ning, sharing the run token with "runs." A worked BPE merge sequence appears in the exercises, and you'll see real tokenizer output in the library section below. The one thing to internalize now: tokens are usually not whole words. "tokenization" itself might be three tokens; a space is often glued to the front of the next token. This trips up almost every beginner.

    The wall RNNs hit — and the idea that breaks through

    Recurrent networks (and their smarter cousin the LSTM, which adds gates to protect the hidden state and helps a lot) carried the field for years. But they have two stubborn problems baked into their design:

    • They're forgetful over long ranges. Information from word 1 must pass through every intermediate step to reach word 200, getting squeezed and rewritten each time. Mathematically, the influence tends to shrink multiplicatively at each step (the "vanishing gradient" problem from Module 11 — Training Deep Networks That Actually Work), so long-range links fade. LSTMs soften this but don't cure it.
    • They're inherently sequential. To compute the hidden state at word 200 you need word 199's, which needs 198's, and so on. You cannot compute them in parallel — the loop forbids it. On modern GPUs that love doing thousands of things at once, this is a brutal bottleneck. Training on huge text is painfully slow.

    Here's the breakthrough question that leads to attention: why force information through a single bottleneck note, step by step at all? What if, when processing the word "it," the model could look directly at every other word in the sentence at the same time and decide, for each one, "how much should this word inform my understanding of 'it' right now?" No relay race, no fading — every word reachable in one hop, and all the looking can happen in parallel. That direct, all-to-all, learnable looking-up is self-attention.

    Making it precise

    Cosine similarity

    For two vectors \( \mathbf{a} \) and \( \mathbf{b} \) (bold means "a list of numbers," a vector), cosine similarity is:

    \[ \cos(\theta) = \frac{\mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b}}{\lVert \mathbf{a} \rVert \, \lVert \mathbf{b} \rVert} \]

    Reading each piece: \( \mathbf{a} \cdot \mathbf{b} \) is the dot product — multiply the vectors element by element and add up the results (you met this in Module 1 — Just-Enough Math Intuition). \( \lVert \mathbf{a} \rVert \) (read "the norm of a") is the vector's length, computed as \( \sqrt{a_1^2 + a_2^2 + \dots} \). Dividing the dot product by both lengths cancels out how long the vectors are, leaving only how aligned their directions are — exactly the cosine of the angle \( \theta \) between them. Value near 1 means "pointing the same way" (similar); near 0 means "perpendicular" (unrelated); near −1 means "opposite."

    Self-attention

    Self-attention starts from a sequence of token vectors — say \( n \) tokens, each a vector. From each token vector we produce three new vectors by multiplying it with three learned weight matrices:

    • a Query \( \mathbf{q} \) — "what am I, this token, looking for?"
    • a Key \( \mathbf{k} \) — "what do I, this token, offer to others who might look at me?"
    • a Value \( \mathbf{v} \) — "if someone attends to me, here's the actual information I hand over."

    The names are a database metaphor: a query is matched against keys, and you retrieve the matching values — except here the match is soft (every key matches a little), and the weights are learned. Stacking the per-token vectors as rows gives matrices \( Q \), \( K \), \( V \). The whole operation is one compact formula:

    \[ \text{Attention}(Q, K, V) = \text{softmax}\!\left( \frac{Q K^{\top}}{\sqrt{d_k}} \right) V \]

    Let's unpack it left to right, because every symbol earns its place:

    • \( Q K^{\top} \) — multiply the queries by the transpose (the flip, rows-become-columns) of the keys. Entry \( (i, j) \) of the result is the dot product of token \( i \)'s query with token \( j \)'s key: a raw score for "how much should token \( i \) attend to token \( j \)?" High when their directions align. For \( n \) tokens this is an \( n \times n \) score matrix — every token scored against every token.
    • \( \div \sqrt{d_k} \) — divide by the square root of \( d_k \), the dimension (length) of the key vectors. This is just a scaling fix: in high dimensions dot products get large, which would make the next step (softmax) too "spiky." Dividing keeps the numbers tame. (\( d_k = 64 \) is typical.)
    • \( \text{softmax}(\cdot) \) — applied to each row, softmax (from Module 5 — Logistic Regression and Classification) turns that row of raw scores into positive weights that sum to 1. Now row \( i \) reads "I, token \( i \), put 70% of my attention on token A, 30% on token B." These are the attention weights.
    • \( \times V \) — multiply the weight matrix by the values. Each token's output is a weighted average of all the value vectors, weighted by how much it chose to attend to each. Token "it" pulls mostly the value of "trophy" and gets an updated representation infused with "trophy."

    That's the entire mechanism. Notice the two RNN problems vanish at once: every token reaches every other token in one step (no fading relay), and the whole \( n \times n \) computation is one big matrix multiply that a GPU does in parallel (no sequential loop). The price — which we'll flag now and pay attention to in Module 14 — is that an \( n \times n \) score matrix means cost grows with the square of the sequence length. Double the text, quadruple the work. That quadratic cost is the central tension in modern long-context models.

    Self-attention from the word "it." Thicker line = more attention. The model has learned to point "it" mostly at "trophy" (0.62), with some at "suitcase" (0.28) and almost none at the function word "the." These weights are the softmaxed scores, and they sum to 1.

    Play with it: pick a query token (click a word, a heatmap row, or use ◀ ▶). Watch the arcs, the bars, and the highlighted heatmap row update — every query's row of weights is a softmax of query·key similarities and sums to 1. Notice how content words ("cat", "sat", "mat") attend to each other, while the function words ("the", "on") cluster apart.

    Worked example — by hand

    Part A: cosine similarity. Compare \( \mathbf{a} = [1, 0] \) and \( \mathbf{b} = [0.7, 0.7] \). Dot product: \( (1)(0.7) + (0)(0.7) = 0.7 \). Lengths: \( \lVert \mathbf{a} \rVert = \sqrt{1^2 + 0^2} = 1 \); \( \lVert \mathbf{b} \rVert = \sqrt{0.7^2 + 0.7^2} = \sqrt{0.49 + 0.49} = \sqrt{0.98} \approx 0.99 \). Cosine similarity \( = \frac{0.7}{1 \times 0.99} \approx 0.707 \). That's the cosine of 45°, which is exactly right: \( [0.7, 0.7] \) points up-and-right at 45°, while \( [1, 0] \) points straight right, so the angle between them is 45° and \( \cos 45^\circ \approx 0.707 \). The vectors are somewhat similar — pointing partly the same way.

    Part B: self-attention for 2 tokens. Two tokens with tiny 2-dimensional Q, K, V vectors. (To keep the arithmetic clean we'll skip the \( \sqrt{d_k} \) scaling here and note where it would go — the code section below includes it.)

    • Queries: \( \mathbf{q}_1 = [1, 0] \), \( \mathbf{q}_2 = [0.5, 0.5] \)
    • Keys: \( \mathbf{k}_1 = [1, 1] \), \( \mathbf{k}_2 = [0, 2] \)
    • Values: \( \mathbf{v}_1 = [2, 0] \), \( \mathbf{v}_2 = [0, 4] \)

    Step 1 — score every query against every key (the \( Q K^{\top} \) matrix). Each entry is a dot product:

    • \( \mathbf{q}_1 \cdot \mathbf{k}_1 = (1)(1) + (0)(1) = 1 \)    \( \mathbf{q}_1 \cdot \mathbf{k}_2 = (1)(0) + (0)(2) = 0 \)
    • \( \mathbf{q}_2 \cdot \mathbf{k}_1 = (0.5)(1) + (0.5)(1) = 1 \)    \( \mathbf{q}_2 \cdot \mathbf{k}_2 = (0.5)(0) + (0.5)(2) = 1 \)

    So the 2×2 score matrix is \( \begin{bmatrix} 1 & 0 \\ 1 & 1 \end{bmatrix} \). Row 1 is token 1's scores against tokens 1 and 2; row 2 is token 2's. (With scaling we'd divide each by \( \sqrt{2} \approx 1.414 \) here.)

    Step 2 — softmax each row into attention weights. Softmax of a row \( [s_1, s_2] \) is \( \big[\frac{e^{s_1}}{e^{s_1}+e^{s_2}}, \frac{e^{s_2}}{e^{s_1}+e^{s_2}}\big] \). For row 1, \( [1, 0] \): \( e^{1} = 2.718 \), \( e^{0} = 1 \), sum \( = 3.718 \). Weights \( = [\,2.718/3.718,\; 1/3.718\,] = [0.731,\; 0.269] \). Token 1 attends 73% to itself and 27% to token 2 — makes sense, its query \( [1,0] \) aligns more with key \( [1,1] \) than with \( [0,2] \).

    For row 2, \( [1, 1] \): both scores equal, so \( e^{1} = e^{1} \) and the weights split evenly: \( [0.5,\; 0.5] \). Token 2 has no preference between the two keys.

    Step 3 — mix the values using those weights (the \( \times V \) step). Each token's output is a weighted average of \( \mathbf{v}_1 = [2,0] \) and \( \mathbf{v}_2 = [0,4] \):

    • Token 1 output \( = 0.731 \cdot [2, 0] + 0.269 \cdot [0, 4] = [1.462, 0] + [0, 1.076] = [1.462,\; 1.076] \).
    • Token 2 output \( = 0.5 \cdot [2, 0] + 0.5 \cdot [0, 4] = [1, 0] + [0, 2] = [1,\; 2] \).

    And that's a complete self-attention pass. Each token walked away with a new vector that blends information from every token, weighted by learned relevance. Token 1, attending mostly to itself, kept more of \( \mathbf{v}_1 \); token 2, attending equally, landed exactly between the two values. Every number here is reproduced by the code below.

    In code: from scratch

    First, cosine similarity on a toy "embedding" where two of the three words are meant to be related. We hand-pick vectors so "cat" and "dog" point similarly while "car" points elsewhere.

    import numpy as np

def cosine(a, b):
    return np.dot(a, b) / (np.linalg.norm(a) * np.linalg.norm(b))

# pretend these tiny 2D vectors came out of a trained embedding
cat = np.array([0.9, 0.1])
dog = np.array([0.8, 0.2])
car = np.array([0.1, 0.95])

print(f"cat vs dog: {cosine(cat, dog):.3f}")   # animals -> high
print(f"cat vs car: {cosine(cat, car):.3f}")   # unrelated -> low

    This prints cat vs dog: 0.991 and cat vs car: 0.214. The two animals score near 1 (their pins sit close on our map of meaning), while "cat" and "car" — similar spelling, unrelated meaning — score low. That gap is the whole point of embeddings: similarity reflects meaning, not letters.

    Now self-attention for the same three-step recipe, this time with the \( \sqrt{d_k} \) scaling that real models use. We reuse the exact Q, K, V from the worked example so you can match the (scaled) numbers.

    import numpy as np

def softmax(x):
    # subtract the row max for numerical stability, then normalize each row
    e = np.exp(x - x.max(axis=-1, keepdims=True))
    return e / e.sum(axis=-1, keepdims=True)

# two tokens, each with a 2D query, key, and value (stacked as rows)
Q = np.array([[1.0, 0.0],
              [0.5, 0.5]])
K = np.array([[1.0, 1.0],
              [0.0, 2.0]])
V = np.array([[2.0, 0.0],
              [0.0, 4.0]])

d_k = Q.shape[1]                       # key/query dimension = 2
scores = Q @ K.T / np.sqrt(d_k)        # 2x2 raw scores, scaled
weights = softmax(scores)              # each row sums to 1
output  = weights @ V                  # weighted mix of the value vectors

print("scaled scores:\n", np.round(scores, 3))
print("attention weights:\n", np.round(weights, 3))
print("output:\n", np.round(output, 3))

    The output is:

    scaled scores:
 [[0.707 0.   ]
 [0.707 0.707]]
attention weights:
 [[0.67  0.33 ]
 [0.5   0.5  ]]
output:
 [[1.34  1.321]
 [1.    2.   ]]

    Compare to the by-hand result: the weights here are \( [0.67, 0.33] \) instead of \( [0.731, 0.269] \), and token-1's output is \( [1.34, 1.321] \) instead of \( [1.462, 1.076] \). The shapes match exactly; the small differences come entirely from the \( \div \sqrt{2} \) scaling we skipped by hand — dividing the scores shrinks the gap between them, so softmax produces gentler, less peaky weights. Token 2's scores were already equal, so scaling doesn't change its 50/50 split or its \( [1, 2] \) output. This three-line core — scores → softmax → mix values — is, no exaggeration, the heart of every transformer in Module 14.

    In code: the library way

    You will almost never hand-roll tokenization; you load a tokenizer that ships with a pretrained model. Hugging Face's AutoTokenizer downloads the right one and turns a sentence into tokens and integer IDs. (Install with pip install transformers; the first run downloads a small tokenizer file.)

    from transformers import AutoTokenizer

tok = AutoTokenizer.from_pretrained("bert-base-uncased")

sentence = "Tokenization isn't magic."
tokens = tok.tokenize(sentence)        # human-readable sub-word pieces
ids    = tok.encode(sentence)          # integer ids the model actually eats

print(tokens)
print(ids)
print(tok.convert_ids_to_tokens(ids))  # ids back to tokens (with specials)

    This prints something like:

    ['token', '##ization', 'isn', "'", 't', 'magic', '.']
[101, 19204, 3989, 3475, 1005, 1056, 3894, 1012, 102]
['[CLS]', 'token', '##ization', 'isn', "'", 't', 'magic', '.', '[SEP]']

    Look closely and the lessons from the BPE section come alive. "Tokenization" splits into token + ##ization — the ## marks a piece that continues the previous word, exactly the sub-word behavior we wanted. "isn't" breaks into isn + ' + t. And the model's own bookends [CLS] and [SEP] get added automatically. Every token maps to an integer ID; those IDs are what get looked up in the embedding table next.

    That lookup is one line in PyTorch. torch.nn.Embedding is literally a learnable table with one row per vocabulary entry; you index it with token IDs and get back the embedding vectors, which then flow into the rest of the network and get trained by gradient descent.

    import torch
import torch.nn as nn

vocab_size, dim = 30522, 8             # bert vocab size, tiny 8D embeddings
embedding = nn.Embedding(vocab_size, dim)

ids = torch.tensor([101, 19204, 3989]) # a few token ids from above
vectors = embedding(ids)               # shape: (3 tokens, 8 numbers each)
print(vectors.shape)                   # torch.Size([3, 8])

    This prints torch.Size([3, 8]): three token IDs in, three 8-dimensional vectors out. The values start random and become meaningful only after training — which is the crucial point of the pitfalls below: embeddings are learned, never hand-written.

    Work this with your Claude Code companion

    Paste these to your Claude Code companion as you study:

    • "Run my from-scratch self-attention NumPy code, then change \( \mathbf{q}_1 \) to [0, 1] and show me how the attention weights and outputs change — explain in words why token 1 now attends differently."
    • "Walk me through Byte-Pair Encoding on the toy corpus 'low low lower newest widest' step by step: show each most-frequent pair, each merge, and the final vocabulary."
    • "Generate 4 practice problems where I compute a 2×2 attention step by hand (give me small Q, K, V), then check my score matrix, softmax, and final outputs."
    • "Pair with me to extend my NumPy attention to 3 tokens and 3-dimensional vectors, and review whether my matrix shapes and the softmax axis are correct."

    Common pitfalls & misconceptions

    • "Embeddings are hand-designed dictionaries." No — nobody decides that "cat" should be \( [0.9, 0.1] \). Embeddings start as random numbers and are learned by gradient descent alongside the rest of the model; the meaningful geometry (cat near dog) emerges from training on lots of text. The toy vectors in this module were hand-picked only to illustrate cosine similarity.
    • Confusing tokens with words. A token is usually a sub-word. "tokenization" can be several tokens, and a leading space is often part of a token. When you hear "this model has a 4,000-token limit," that is not 4,000 words — it's often closer to 3,000 English words. Always check the actual tokenizer output, as we did above.
    • Thinking BPE understands grammar or meaning. BPE is pure frequency-counting on character sequences — it merges common adjacent pairs and knows nothing about syntax or semantics. The meaning all comes later, from the embeddings and attention that operate on the resulting tokens.
    • "Attention is just a fancy RNN." They're opposites in spirit. An RNN passes information step by step through a single hidden state (sequential, forgetful). Attention lets every token read every other token directly and simultaneously (parallel, no fading). That difference is exactly why transformers replaced RNNs.
    • Forgetting attention is quadratic in sequence length. The score matrix is \( n \times n \), so doubling the input roughly quadruples compute and memory. This is why context windows are a big deal and why "efficient attention" is an active research area — a theme you'll meet again in Module 14.
    • Mixing up Q, K, and V. A query asks ("what am I looking for?"), a key advertises ("what do I offer?"), a value delivers ("here's my actual content"). The score uses query·key; the output is built from values. Swapping them silently produces wrong results that still run without error.

    Exercises

    1. Compute the cosine similarity of \( \mathbf{a} = [3, 4] \) and \( \mathbf{b} = [4, 3] \) by hand. Are they more or less similar than \( [1, 0] \) and \( [0.7, 0.7] \) from the worked example?
      Show solution

      Dot product: \( (3)(4) + (4)(3) = 12 + 12 = 24 \). Lengths: \( \lVert \mathbf{a} \rVert = \sqrt{9 + 16} = \sqrt{25} = 5 \); \( \lVert \mathbf{b} \rVert = \sqrt{16 + 9} = 5 \). Cosine \( = 24 / (5 \times 5) = 24/25 = 0.96 \). That's much closer to 1 than the worked example's \( 0.707 \), so these two vectors are more similar — they point in nearly the same direction (a small angle, about 16°), whereas \( [1,0] \) and \( [0.7,0.7] \) were 45° apart.

    2. Run one BPE merge by hand. Starting vocabulary is characters; the corpus is the single word "banana" (treat it as the character sequence b a n a n a). What is the most frequent adjacent pair, and what does one merge produce?
      Show solution

      List the adjacent pairs in "b a n a n a": (b,a), (a,n), (n,a), (a,n), (n,a). Counts: (a,n) appears twice, (n,a) appears twice, (b,a) once. There's a tie between "an" and "na" at 2 each; BPE breaks ties by a fixed rule (e.g. first encountered), so say we merge "an". The sequence becomes b "an" "an" a — that is, b · an · an · a, four symbols instead of six. If we ran a second merge, "an a" or "an an" pairs would now be candidates. Notice how repeated structure ("an") gets compressed into a single reusable token, which is exactly why BPE makes frequent patterns cheap.

    3. For a self-attention step, the raw score matrix (before softmax) for two tokens is \( \begin{bmatrix} 2 & 0 \\ 0 & 0 \end{bmatrix} \). Softmax each row to get the attention weights. Which token is more "decisive" about where it looks, and why?
      Show solution

      Row 1, \( [2, 0] \): \( e^2 = 7.389 \), \( e^0 = 1 \), sum \( = 8.389 \). Weights \( = [7.389/8.389,\; 1/8.389] = [0.881,\; 0.119] \). Row 2, \( [0, 0] \): both \( e^0 = 1 \), sum \( = 2 \), weights \( = [0.5,\; 0.5] \). Token 1 is far more decisive — it pours 88% of its attention onto token 1 and only 12% onto token 2, because its scores differ a lot. Token 2's scores are equal, so it spreads attention evenly (maximum uncertainty). Larger gaps between scores produce sharper, more confident attention.

    4. Continuing exercise 3, suppose the value vectors are \( \mathbf{v}_1 = [10, 0] \) and \( \mathbf{v}_2 = [0, 10] \). Compute each token's output vector.
      Show solution

      Token 1 used weights \( [0.881, 0.119] \): output \( = 0.881 \cdot [10, 0] + 0.119 \cdot [0, 10] = [8.81, 0] + [0, 1.19] = [8.81,\; 1.19] \). Token 2 used weights \( [0.5, 0.5] \): output \( = 0.5 \cdot [10, 0] + 0.5 \cdot [0, 10] = [5,\; 5] \). Token 1, attending mostly to \( \mathbf{v}_1 \), comes out close to \( [10, 0] \); token 2, attending equally, lands exactly between the two values. The output is always a weighted average of the value vectors.

    5. Explain, in 3–4 sentences, why a plain RNN struggles with the sentence "The keys that I left on the kitchen table this morning before the long meeting are gone," specifically linking "keys" to "are." Then say how self-attention handles the same link.
      Show solution

      For the RNN to make "are" (plural) agree with "keys," information about "keys" must travel through every word in between — "that I left on the kitchen table this morning before the long meeting" — surviving the hidden state being rewritten at each of those ~12 steps. Over that distance the contribution of "keys" tends to fade (the long-range memory / vanishing-gradient problem), so the agreement is fragile. Self-attention removes the relay entirely: when processing "are," the model computes a direct attention score between "are" and "keys" and can place high weight on it in a single hop, regardless of the 12 words in between. Distance no longer dilutes the connection.

    You've got this if you can…

    • Explain in one breath why sequences need special models (order + variable length) and why a bag-of-features model fails on "dog bites man" vs "man bites dog."
    • Describe an RNN's hidden state as a sticky-note summary and name its two weaknesses (forgetful, sequential).
    • Compute cosine similarity between two small vectors and say what near-1, near-0, and near-−1 mean.
    • State why we use sub-word tokens (BPE) instead of whole words or single characters, and not be surprised that "tokenization" is several tokens.
    • Carry out a 2-token self-attention step end to end: build the score matrix from Q and K, softmax each row, and mix the V vectors — and say why this beats recurrence.

    Curated resources

    • Jay Alammar — The Illustrated Transformer — the friendliest visual walkthrough of Q/K/V and self-attention; read it right after this module and before Module 14.
    • StatQuest — Word Embeddings & Attention (YouTube) — Josh Starmer's slow, picture-first explanations of embeddings and the attention mechanism.
    • Hugging Face LLM Course — hands-on chapters on tokenizers (including BPE) and embeddings using the exact AutoTokenizer tools above.
    • Karpathy — Let's build GPT (YouTube) — builds self-attention from scratch in code; ambitious but the single best way to make attention click once you've finished here.

    Projects

    Pick one or work through several. Each is small enough to finish in a sitting yet real enough to teach something the prose can't. Use your Claude Code companion to pair, debug, and check your numbers — but type the core loops yourself.

    Project 1 — Build a tiny BPE tokenizer from scratch

    • Goal: implement Byte-Pair Encoding end to end so you truly understand why tokens are sub-words.
    • Dataset: any small plain-text file — a few paragraphs of a public-domain book from Project Gutenberg, or even the toy corpus "low low lower newest widest".
    • Milestones: (1) split the text into characters and count adjacent pairs; (2) write a loop that finds the most frequent pair, merges it everywhere, and records the merge; (3) run it for N merges and print the learned vocabulary; (4) write an encode function that applies the learned merges to a new word.
    • Success: your tokenizer turns a frequent word into one token and a rare word into several sub-word pieces, with no "unknown" token ever needed.
    • Stretch: compare your token count for a paragraph against Hugging Face's bert-base-uncased tokenizer and explain the differences.

    Project 2 — A cosine-similarity word-neighbors explorer

    • Goal: see the "map of meaning" for real by finding each word's nearest neighbors in a pretrained embedding.
    • Dataset: a small set of pretrained GloVe vectors (e.g. the 50-dimensional glove.6B.50d file), or any few-thousand-word embedding table you can load.
    • Milestones: (1) load the vectors into a dictionary mapping word to NumPy array; (2) implement cosine similarity; (3) write nearest(word, k) that returns the k most similar words; (4) try it on "king", "paris", "happy".
    • Success: the neighbors of "king" are clearly royalty/leadership words, not random ones.
    • Stretch: test the famous analogy by computing king - man + woman and finding its nearest neighbor; report whether "queen" shows up near the top.

    Project 3 — Self-attention, fully from scratch in NumPy

    • Goal: generalize the 2-token by-hand example into a working attention function for any number of tokens and dimensions.
    • Dataset: none needed — make up small random Q, K, V matrices, or derive them from a few embedding vectors plus random weight matrices.
    • Milestones: (1) write a row-wise softmax; (2) compute scaled scores Q @ K.T / sqrt(d_k); (3) softmax and mix the values; (4) assert every row of the weight matrix sums to 1.
    • Success: for the module's 2-token inputs your function reproduces the printed scaled output [[1.34, 1.321], [1.0, 2.0]].
    • Stretch: add a causal mask (set scores above the diagonal to a large negative number before softmax) so each token can attend only to itself and earlier tokens — the trick that makes a language model predict left to right.

    Project 4 — Inspect a real tokenizer's quirks

    • Goal: build intuition for how token counts differ from word counts, and how leading spaces and casing change tokenization.
    • Dataset: a dozen sentences of your own, including a long rare word, an emoji, a number like "1234567", and a word with and without a leading space.
    • Milestones: (1) load AutoTokenizer for both bert-base-uncased and gpt2; (2) for each sentence print the tokens and the token count; (3) compare token count to word count; (4) tokenize "hello" versus " hello" and note the difference.
    • Success: you can state, with examples, three concrete ways tokens differ from words.
    • Stretch: find the single English sentence under 10 words that produces the most tokens you can, and explain why.

    Project 5 — RNN vs. attention on a long-range agreement task

    • Goal: feel the long-range memory problem by making a model decide subject-verb agreement across a long gap.
    • Dataset: generate synthetic sentences of the form "The [noun(s)] that ... [verb]", where the verb must agree (is/are) with a noun many words back; vary the gap length.
    • Milestones: (1) generate a few thousand labeled examples; (2) train a small PyTorch RNN to predict is/are; (3) train a small single-layer self-attention model on the same data; (4) plot accuracy versus gap length for both.
    • Success: the RNN's accuracy decays as the gap grows while the attention model stays roughly flat — the module's central claim, made visible.
    • Stretch: visualize the attention weights for "are" and confirm the model puts high weight on the far-back subject noun.
    Module 14

    Transformers and LLMs, Conceptually

    Learning objectives

    By the end of this module you'll be able to:

    • Explain every part of a transformer block — self-attention, multi-head attention, the feed-forward layer, residual connections, layer normalization, and positional encoding — and say why each one exists.
    • Describe how a large language model (LLM) is trained to predict the next token, and apply a softmax over the vocabulary to turn raw scores into next-token probabilities by hand.
    • Run a real pretrained model in a few lines using the Hugging Face Transformers pipeline, and sketch a retrieval-augmented generation (RAG) system with embeddings and a FAISS vector index.
    • Choose between prompting, RAG, and fine-tuning (full vs. parameter-efficient / LoRA), and explain quantization in one breath.
    • Decide when a problem calls for classical ML (Modules 3–9) versus an LLM, on cost, latency, data, and reliability grounds.
    • Explain what an LLM agent is, why tool use matters, and what the Model Context Protocol (MCP) standardizes.

    Why should I care?

    In 2017 a single paper, "Attention Is All You Need," replaced the slow, one-word-at-a-time recurrent networks you met in Module 13 — Sequences & NLP with an architecture that reads a whole sentence at once and learns which words matter to which. That architecture, the transformer, is the engine inside ChatGPT, Claude, Gemini, every modern translation system, the protein-folding breakthroughs in biology, and the code-completion tool you may already use. Here is the part that surprises people: an LLM is, at its core, doing one almost-trivial thing — guessing the next word, over and over. That is it. Everything that feels like reasoning, writing, and answering questions emerges from a model that got extremely good at "what word comes next?" trained on a large slice of the internet. This module is your modern-literacy capstone: you will understand what is actually happening inside these systems, run one yourself, and learn the practical skills — prompting, RAG, fine-tuning, knowing when not to use an LLM — that separate someone who pastes prompts from someone who builds reliable systems. You will not train a giant model (that costs millions of dollars and is nobody's first project); you will understand them deeply and use them well.

    Intuition first

    You already have most of the pieces. In Module 13 — Sequences & NLP you learned that words become vectors (embeddings) and that attention lets a model, when processing one word, look back at other words and decide how much to "pay attention" to each. The transformer is what you get when you throw away the slow recurrent loop entirely and build the whole network out of attention plus a few simple, repeated parts. Let's assemble it conceptually before any math.

    The one new idea: self-attention reads the whole sentence at once

    Take the sentence "The animal didn't cross the street because it was too tired." What does "it" refer to — the animal or the street? A human knows instantly: the animal. A recurrent network had to carry that information forward word by word, and it tended to forget across long gaps. Self-attention solves this directly: when the model processes the word "it," it looks at every other word in the sentence simultaneously and computes a relevance score for each. "Animal" gets a high score, "street" a lower one, and the model blends in mostly the meaning of "animal." Every word does this for every other word, all at once — which is also why transformers train so fast on modern hardware: there is no sequential loop to wait on, just big matrix multiplications.

    The meeting-room analogy. Picture each word in your sentence as a person sitting around a table, and each person is trying to update their own understanding. Every person holds up a small sign describing what they're looking for (a query), and another sign describing what they offer (a key), and they carry a notebook of actual content (a value). To update, you the listener compare your "looking for" sign against everyone's "offering" sign; whoever matches best, you copy the most from their notebook. The word "it" is looking for "the noun I refer to"; "animal" is offering "I'm a noun, an animal"; the match is strong, so "it" copies meaning from "animal." That comparison-and-copy, done by every word for every word, is self-attention. Multi-head attention just runs several of these meetings in parallel, each table focusing on a different kind of relationship — one table tracks grammar, another tracks who-did-what-to-whom — and then merges their conclusions.

    Why positions are needed

    Here's a subtlety that trips everyone up. Self-attention treats the input as a set, not a sequence — it has no built-in sense of order. To attention as described so far, "dog bites man" and "man bites dog" look identical, because it just compares every word to every word with no notion of who came first. That's a disaster for language, where order is meaning. The fix is positional encoding: before the words enter the first attention layer, we add a small position-dependent pattern to each word's vector, so the vector for "dog-in-position-1" differs from "dog-in-position-3." Now the model can tell order apart. Think of it as stamping each word with its seat number before the meeting starts.

    The supporting cast: feed-forward, residuals, and layer norm

    Attention mixes information between words. After that, each word's vector is passed through a small feed-forward network (the same two-layer network you built in Module 10 — Neural Network Fundamentals, applied to every position independently) that lets the model do extra per-word processing — refining the meaning it just gathered. Two more pieces keep deep stacks of these blocks trainable, both reused from Module 11 — Training Deep Networks. A residual connection adds a layer's input back to its output (output = input + what-the-layer-computed), so information and gradients have a clean shortcut path and very deep stacks don't lose the signal. Layer normalization rescales each word's vector to a stable mean and spread before each sub-layer, which keeps the numbers from exploding or vanishing as they flow through dozens of blocks. None of these are exotic — they are the plumbing that makes 96-layer-deep networks actually learn.

    Stack one of these blocks — attention, then feed-forward, each wrapped in a residual and a layer norm — and you have a transformer block. Stack 12, or 32, or 96 of them, put word embeddings plus positions at the bottom and a next-word predictor at the top, and you have a large language model. Here is the whole block in one picture.

    One transformer block. Self-attention lets words exchange information; the feed-forward layer refines each word; residual connections (dashed "skip" paths) and layer norm keep the deep stack trainable. A full LLM stacks N of these.

    What "predict the next token" really means

    A token is a chunk of text — sometimes a whole word, often a word-piece like "ing" or "play," sometimes a single character. The model's vocabulary is a fixed list of, say, 50,000 tokens. After the final transformer block, the model looks at the running text so far and produces one score (a logit) for every token in the vocabulary: "how likely is each token to come next?" A softmax (the same function from Module 5 — Logistic Regression, now over 50,000 classes instead of 2) turns those scores into probabilities that sum to 1. Pick a token, append it to the text, and repeat. That loop — score the vocabulary, softmax, pick, append — is the entire generation process. "Pretraining" is just doing this billions of times on real text, nudging the weights each time so the model's guess matches the word that actually came next. From that single objective, grammar, facts, style, and a startling amount of reasoning fall out as side effects.

    Making it precise

    Self-attention as Query, Key, Value

    Each word's embedding vector \( x \) is turned into three vectors by multiplying it by three learned weight matrices: a query \( q = x W_Q \) (what this word is looking for), a key \( k = x W_K \) (what this word offers), and a value \( v = x W_V \) (the actual content this word will share). Here \( W_Q, W_K, W_V \) are matrices of weights the model learns during training — the same kind of adjustable numbers you have tuned since Module 3 — Linear Regression, just arranged in a grid. Collecting all words' queries into a matrix \( Q \), all keys into \( K \), and all values into \( V \), the whole attention operation is one compact formula:

    \[ \text{Attention}(Q, K, V) = \text{softmax}\!\left( \frac{Q K^\top}{\sqrt{d_k}} \right) V \]

    Let's read it left to right. The product \( Q K^\top \) (each query dotted with each key) is a grid of match scores: entry \((i, j)\) is how much word \( i \)'s query matches word \( j \)'s key — bigger means "more relevant." We divide by \( \sqrt{d_k} \), the square root of the key dimension \( d_k \) (the length of a key vector), purely to keep the scores from getting so large that the softmax becomes razor-sharp and gradients vanish — it's a scaling safety valve. The softmax turns each row of scores into weights between 0 and 1 that sum to 1, so each word ends up with a probability distribution over which other words to attend to. Finally, multiplying those weights by \( V \) produces, for each word, a weighted blend of all the value vectors — mostly the content of whichever words it attended to most. In one sentence: each word builds its new representation as a relevance-weighted average of every word's content.

    Multi-head attention

    One attention computation can only emphasize one kind of relationship at a time. Multi-head attention runs \( h \) separate attention computations (the "heads") in parallel, each with its own \( W_Q, W_K, W_V \), then concatenates their outputs and mixes them with one more weight matrix \( W_O \):

    \[ \text{MultiHead} = \text{Concat}(\text{head}_1, \dots, \text{head}_h)\, W_O \]

    where each \( \text{head}_i \) is the attention formula above with its own learned matrices. Because each head learns to focus on a different pattern — one might track subject-verb agreement, another long-range references like our "it" → "animal" — the model captures many relationship types at once, like running several specialist meetings and combining their notes.

    Residual connection and layer norm

    Each sub-layer (attention or feed-forward) is wrapped so its output is

    \[ \text{out} = \text{LayerNorm}\big(x + \text{Sublayer}(x)\big) \]

    where \( x \) is the sub-layer's input. The \( x + \dots \) is the residual (or "skip") connection: it adds the original input back, so even if the sub-layer learns nothing useful at first, information still passes straight through — and gradients flow backward through that shortcut without shrinking to zero, which is what lets us stack dozens of layers (the vanishing-gradient cure from Module 11 — Training Deep Networks). LayerNorm then rescales the result so, for each word's vector, the values have mean 0 and standard deviation 1 (then a learned scale and shift are applied), keeping the magnitudes stable as data flows through many blocks.

    Positional encoding

    Because attention is order-blind, we add a position signal to each word embedding before the first block. The original transformer used fixed sine and cosine waves of different frequencies; for an embedding dimension index \( i \) at sequence position \( pos \):

    \[ PE_{(pos,\,2i)} = \sin\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{\,2i/d}}\right), \qquad PE_{(pos,\,2i+1)} = \cos\!\left(\frac{pos}{10000^{\,2i/d}}\right) \]

    where \( pos \) is the word's position in the sentence (0, 1, 2, …), \( d \) is the embedding size, and \( i \) indexes the dimensions. You don't need to memorize this; the idea is all that matters. Each position gets a unique fingerprint of wave values, added onto its word's vector, so "first word" and "fifth word" carry distinguishable patterns. Modern models often learn the position vectors instead, or use rotary methods, but the goal is unchanged: give attention a way to know order.

    Next-token prediction with softmax over the vocabulary

    After the top block, a final linear layer produces a logit vector \( z \) with one entry per vocabulary token. The probability that the next token is token \( j \) is the softmax:

    \[ P(\text{token} = j) = \frac{e^{z_j}}{\sum_{m} e^{z_m}} \]

    where \( z_j \) is the logit (raw score) for token \( j \), \( e^{z_j} \) exponentiates it (making everything positive and amplifying differences), and the denominator sums over all tokens \( m \) so the probabilities add to 1. Training minimizes the cross-entropy loss from Module 5 — Logistic Regression: \( -\log P(\text{the token that actually came next}) \). Low loss means the model assigned high probability to the correct next token. That's the entire learning signal, repeated over trillions of tokens of text.

    Worked example — by hand

    Suppose our tiny vocabulary is just three tokens — "cat", "sat", "mat" — and after reading "The cat" the model outputs the logits \( z = [\,2.0,\ 1.0,\ 0.1\,] \). Let's turn these into next-token probabilities with the softmax, by hand.

    Step 1 — exponentiate each logit (using \( e \approx 2.71828 \)):

    • \( e^{2.0} \approx 7.389 \)
    • \( e^{1.0} \approx 2.718 \)
    • \( e^{0.1} \approx 1.105 \)

    Step 2 — add them up to get the normalizing denominator:

    \[ 7.389 + 2.718 + 1.105 = 11.212 \]

    Step 3 — divide each exponential by that sum:

    • \( P(\text{cat}) = 7.389 / 11.212 \approx 0.659 \)
    • \( P(\text{sat}) = 2.718 / 11.212 \approx 0.242 \)
    • \( P(\text{mat}) = 1.105 / 11.212 \approx 0.099 \)

    So the model says the next token is most likely "cat" (66%), then "sat" (24%), then "mat" (10%) — and \( 0.659 + 0.242 + 0.099 = 1.000 \), exactly as probabilities must. A model set to "greedy" decoding always picks the top token ("cat"). One set to sample rolls a weighted die: 66% of the time it draws "cat," but sometimes it picks "sat" or even "mat," which is why the same prompt can give different answers. A temperature knob rescales the logits before softmax — dividing by a temperature below 1 sharpens the distribution toward the top choice (more deterministic), above 1 flattens it (more random and creative). At temperature 0.5 these same logits become roughly \( [0.864,\ 0.117,\ 0.019] \) — far more confident in "cat"; at temperature 2.0 they flatten to about \( [0.502,\ 0.304,\ 0.194] \).

    Play with it: drag the temperature T and watch the next-token distribution sharpen toward one word (low T, greedy) or flatten into many plausible words (high T, creative). Hit Sample to roll a weighted die and pick the actual next token — exactly what an LLM does at every step.

    In code: from scratch

    Let's make the worked example runnable. We implement softmax in NumPy (with the standard numerical-stability trick of subtracting the max logit, which never changes the result but prevents overflow), reproduce the hand calculation, then show greedy vs. sampled next-token selection over our tiny vocabulary.

    import numpy as np

def softmax(z, temperature=1.0):
    z = np.asarray(z, dtype=float) / temperature
    z = z - z.max()              # stability: subtract max, result is unchanged
    exp = np.exp(z)
    return exp / exp.sum()

vocab  = ["cat", "sat", "mat"]
logits = np.array([2.0, 1.0, 0.1])

probs = softmax(logits)
print("probabilities:", np.round(probs, 3))   # [0.659 0.242 0.099]
print("sum:", round(probs.sum(), 6))           # 1.0

# Greedy decoding: always take the highest-probability token.
greedy = vocab[int(np.argmax(probs))]
print("greedy next token:", greedy)            # cat

# Sampling: draw a token according to the probabilities (set a seed to reproduce).
rng = np.random.default_rng(0)
draws = rng.choice(vocab, size=10, p=probs)
print("10 sampled tokens:", list(draws))

# Temperature changes how 'sharp' the distribution is.
print("T=0.5:", np.round(softmax(logits, 0.5), 3))   # [0.864 0.117 0.019]
print("T=2.0:", np.round(softmax(logits, 2.0), 3))   # [0.502 0.304 0.194]

    The printed probabilities are [0.659 0.242 0.099] — matching the hand calculation exactly — and they sum to 1.0. Greedy decoding returns "cat". The 10 sampled tokens are mostly "cat" with an occasional "sat" or "mat," demonstrating why sampling makes generation varied. The temperature lines show the distribution sharpening at 0.5 and flattening at 2.0. This little loop — score, softmax, pick, repeat — is genuinely what a giant LLM does at every step; the only difference is that its logits come from billions of parameters instead of a hand-typed array, and its vocabulary has tens of thousands of tokens, not three.

    In code: the library way

    You will almost never build a transformer yourself. The standard tool is Hugging Face transformers, whose pipeline downloads a pretrained model and wraps tokenization, the forward pass, and decoding into one call. Two everyday tasks:

    from transformers import pipeline

# 1) Sentiment classification with a small pretrained model.
classify = pipeline("sentiment-analysis")
print(classify("I was skeptical, but this course is genuinely excellent."))
# -> [{'label': 'POSITIVE', 'score': 0.9998...}]

# 2) Text generation: feed a prompt, get a continuation.
generate = pipeline("text-generation", model="gpt2")
out = generate("Machine learning is", max_new_tokens=20, do_sample=True,
               temperature=0.8, truncation=True)
print(out[0]["generated_text"])

    The sentiment pipeline returns a label and a confidence score. The generation pipeline appends up to 20 new tokens to your prompt, sampling at temperature 0.8 — exactly the score-softmax-pick loop from the previous section, just with GPT-2's 50,257-token vocabulary. The first time you run each line it downloads the model (a few hundred megabytes); after that it's cached locally.
